Prophethood
beginner
4 min
God Sends Messengers by Pure Grace
Sending messengers is grace; faith becomes obligatory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that sending messengers is a grace from Allah, not an obligation, and that faith becomes obligatory upon receiving the message.

Core Idea
Divine grace, not obligation, sends prophets; faith then becomes a duty.

Sanusi begins this section by affirming that Allah's sending of messengers is an act of pure grace (faḍl), not a necessity upon Him. However, once the message arrives, belief becomes obligatory. This establishes the theological foundation that guidance is a gift, not a right, and that God is free from any compulsion.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the two lines of Arabic slowly. Notice the contrast between 'no obligation' and 'our faith has become obligatory.'
The word 'بمحض' emphasizes pure grace.
2
Identify the two key claims
The first claim is that Allah is not obligated to send messengers. The second is that once sent, faith in them becomes required. Write these in your own words.
3
Understand the warning
The phrase 'leave the desire of a people' warns against following whims that reject prophets. Reflect on the connection between desire and disbelief.
This is a common theme in kalam.
4
Apply the lesson
Consider how this principle—grace then obligation—affects your view of divine justice and human responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمِنْهُ إرْسَالُ جَمِيعِ الرُّسْلِ فَلَا وُجُوبَ بَلْ بِمَحْضِ الفَضْلِ . لَكِنْ بذا إيمَانُنَا قَدْ وَجَبا فَدَعْ هَوى قَوْمٍ بِهِمْ قَدْ لَعِبَا . وَوَاجِبٌ في حَفِّهِمْ الأَمَانَهُ وَصِدْقُهُمْ وَضِفْ لَهَا الفَطَانَهُ . وَمِثْلُ ذَا تَبْلِيغُهُمْ لما أَتَوا وَيَسْتَحِيلُ ضِدُّها كَمَا رَوَوْا . وَجَائِزٌ فِي حَفِّهِمْ كَالأَكْلِ وكَالجِماعِ لِلنِّـــــا في الحِل . وَجَامِعُ مَعْنى الَّذِي تَقَرَّرَا شهادنَا الْإسْلامِ فَاطْرَحِ المِرَا . وَلَمْ تَكُنْ نُبُوَّةٌ مُكْتَسَبَهْ وَلَوْ رَقَى في الخيرِ أَعْلَى عَقَبَة . بَلْ ذَاكَ فَضْلُ اللَّهِ يُؤْتِيهِ لِمَنْ يَشَاءُ جَلَّ اللهُ واهِبُ المِنَنْ . وَأَفْضَلُ الخَلْقِ عَلَى الإِطْلَاقِ نَبِيُّنَا فَمِلْ عَنِ الشَّقَاقِ .
And from it is the sending of all messengers: there is no obligation, rather by pure grace. But by this our faith has become obligatory, so leave the desire of a people whom play has toyed with. And obligatory in their right is trustworthiness, and their truthfulness, and add to it intelligence. And like this is their conveyance of what they brought; and their opposites are impossible as has been narrated. And permissible in their right is like eating and like sexual intercourse for marriage in the permissible. And the comprehensive meaning of what has been established is our testimony of Islam, so discard dispute. And prophethood was never acquired, even if one ascends in goodness to the highest peak. Rather that is Allah's grace, He gives it to whom He wills; exalted is Allah, the Bestower of favors. And the best of creation absolutely is our Prophet, so turn away from dissent.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This comprehensive passage on prophethood (nubuwwa) covers multiple points. First, sending messengers is not obligatory upon Allah but a pure grace (faḍl). However, our faith (īmān) becomes obligatory upon receiving the message, so one should follow it and avoid the desires of misguided groups. Second, the necessary attributes of prophets are listed: trustworthiness (amāna), truthfulness (ṣidq), intelligence (faṭāna), and conveyance (tablīgh) of the message. Their opposites are impossible. Third, permissible (jā’iz) human acts for prophets are like eating and marriage. Fourth, the comprehensive meaning of these beliefs is the testimony of Islam (shahāda), so avoid disputes. Fifth, prophethood is not acquired (muktasaba) through effort—even the highest spiritual ascent cannot earn it; it is purely a gift from Allah. Finally, the absolute best of all creation is our Prophet Muhammad, so abandon any dissent (shiqāq) against this.

Key Terms
الفضل (al-faḍl)
الإيمان (al-īmān)
الوجوب (al-wujūb)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Allah is obligated to send prophets.
Correction: The passage affirms that sending is by pure grace, not obligation.

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on the sending of messengers from Allah according to the poem?
A) It is obligatory.
B) It is a pure grace, not obligatory.
C) It is impossible.
D) It is based on human merit.

Key Takeaway
Sending messengers is Allah's grace, but responding with faith is our duty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that you are doing Allah a favor by believing in His messengers? How does this lesson challenge that assumption? This week, remind yourself daily that you worship out of response to His grace, not as a favor to Him.
?
The passage says faith becomes obligatory once the message arrives. Have you fully responded to the message of the Prophet (peace be upon him) in every area of your life? Identify one aspect where you have held back and commit to aligning it today.

Prophethood

Necessary and Permissible Attributes of Prophets
Enumeration of necessary, impossible, and permissible attributes of prophets.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the attributes that are necessary, impossible, and permissible for prophets, and how these beliefs form the testimony of Islam.

Core Idea
Prophets must be truthful, trustworthy, intelligent, and conveying; their opposites are impossible; they may experience human needs.

Sanusi now enumerates the essential attributes of prophets: trustworthiness (amāna), truthfulness (ṣidq), intelligence (faṭāna), and conveyance (tablīgh). Their opposites—betrayal, lying, stupidity, concealment—are impossible. Additionally, prophets are permitted human acts like eating and marriage. The comprehensive belief in these attributes is encapsulated in the testimony of Islam, so one should avoid disputes about them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the four lines of Arabic and their translation. Notice the structure: obligatory attributes, impossible opposites, permissible acts, and the summary.
The Arabic uses 'واجب' (obligatory) and 'جائز' (permissible).
2
List the necessary attributes
Identify the four necessary attributes: amāna, ṣidq, faṭāna, tablīgh. Write them down with their meanings.
Some scholars add others, but these are core.
3
Identify the impossible opposites
For each necessary attribute, state its opposite. For example, the opposite of truthfulness is lying. Reflect on why these are impossible for prophets.
This is based on divine wisdom and protection.
4
Note the permissible acts
The passage mentions eating and marriage as permissible. Consider why these are allowed—they are human needs, not defects.
This distinguishes prophets from angels.
5
Connect to the testimony of Islam
The passage ends by saying these beliefs are the essence of the shahada. Discuss how affirming these attributes is part of Islamic testimony.
Avoid disputes on these matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And from it is the sending of all messengers: there is no obligation, rather by pure grace. But by this our faith has become obligatory, so leave the desire of a people whom play has toyed with. And obligatory in their right is trustworthiness, and their truthfulness, and add to it intelligence. And like this is their conveyance of what they brought; and their opposites are impossible as has been narrated. And permissible in their right is like eating and like sexual intercourse for marriage in the permissible. And the comprehensive meaning of what has been established is our testimony of Islam, so discard dispute. And prophethood was never acquired, even if one ascends in goodness to the highest peak. Rather that is Allah's grace, He gives it to whom He wills; exalted is Allah, the Bestower of favors. And the best of creation absolutely is our Prophet, so turn away from dissent.

However, our faith (īmān) becomes obligatory upon receiving the message, so one should follow it and avoid the desires of misguided groups. Misconception: Prophets cannot have human needs like eating or marriage.
Correction: The passage states that such acts are permissible for prophets.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a necessary attribute of prophets according to the passage?
A) Trustworthiness
B) Infallibility from minor sins
C) Truthfulness
D) Intelligence

Key Takeaway
Believing in the necessary, impossible, and permissible attributes of prophets is part of the Islamic testimony.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe in the prophets, but do you truly embody their attribute of trustworthiness in your own life? When was the last time you betrayed someone's trust, even in a small matter? How can you begin today to make amāna a defining trait of your character?
?
The prophets are permitted normal human needs like eating and marriage. Do you ever view your own legitimate human needs as spiritually inferior, or do you sanctify them by intention? What would change if you saw your next meal or marital interaction as an act of worship aligned with the prophetic way?
?
The passage ends with 'discard dispute.' Is there a theological disagreement you cling to that prevents you from fully submitting to the testimony of Islam? What would it cost you to let go of that dispute and embrace the comprehensive meaning of faith?

Prophethood

Prophethood Is a Divine Gift; Muhammad is the Best
Prophethood is unearned grace; Muhammad is the best of creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that prophethood cannot be earned through effort but is given by Allah's grace, and that Prophet Muhammad is the best of all creation.

Core Idea
Prophethood is a pure gift; no effort can earn it; Muhammad is the greatest.

The final part of the poem emphasizes that prophethood is not acquired (muktasaba) by personal striving, no matter how high one's spiritual station. It is solely a favor from Allah. Furthermore, the Prophet Muhammad is affirmed as the absolute best of creation, and the believer must avoid dissent on this matter.

Guided Path
1
Read the final three lines
Read the Arabic lines and their translation. Pay attention to the negation 'لم تكن' and the contrast between effort and divine gift.
The word 'مكتسبة' means acquired.
2
Understand 'prophethood is not acquired'
The poem rejects the idea that prophethood can be earned through spiritual exertion, even at the highest level. Reflect on why this protects divine sovereignty.
This refutes the notion of human merit earning prophethood.
3
Recognize it as pure grace
The passage states it is Allah's grace given to whom He wills. Write down what this implies about divine will and human capability.
Compare with the earlier point about sending messengers.
4
Affirm the superiority of Muhammad
The poem declares Muhammad as the best of creation absolutely. Consider why this is a fundamental belief and why dissent is discouraged.
'على الإطلاق' means absolutely, without exception.
5
Apply the lesson
Reflect on how this teaching affects your view of prophethood and the status of the Prophet. How should we avoid dispute on this?
Turn away from 'شقاق' (dissent).

Source Text

Misconception: Prophethood can be earned through extreme piety or spiritual effort.
Correction: The passage affirms that prophethood is a gift, not acquired.

Checkpoint
According to the poem, can prophethood be acquired through good deeds?
A) Yes, if one reaches the highest peak of goodness.
B) No, it is never acquired; it is a gift.
C) Yes, but only through divine assistance.
D) No, but it can be earned with special knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Prophethood is a pure divine gift, and Muhammad is the greatest of all prophets and humans.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you engage in acts of worship, do you ever feel that you are 'earning' closeness to Allah, forgetting that even the highest piety is a gift? How does this passage challenge that assumption?
?
This passage declares that Muhammad is the best of creation absolutely. How does your heart respond to that statement? Do you truly honor him as such, or are there moments when you elevate others' opinions above his teachings?
?
If prophethood is a pure gift, then what does that mean for your own reliance on Allah? Are you living in a state of gratitude and dependence, or do you occasionally fall into thinking your actions deserve reward?

Prophethood
beginner
4 min
Sending of Messengers is Pure Grace
Al-Laqqani states that God sends messengers by grace, not obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the sending of messengers is an act of pure grace from Allah, not an obligation upon Him, and why belief in them becomes obligatory after their sending.

Core Idea
Prophets are sent by grace, not necessity; once sent, belief in them is obligatory.

In this lesson, we examine a key doctrine in Sunni theology: the sending of prophets is not something Allah is required to do; it is a free gift of His grace. This refutes the Mu'tazilite position that Allah must send messengers for guidance. Yet, once He has sent them, believing in them becomes our duty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage silently
Read the Arabic couplet once, then the English translation. Notice the two contrasting statements: 'no obligation' and 'pure grace'.
The poet uses a concise style; each phrase carries theological weight.
2
Identify the two key claims
Identify that the first half denies any obligation upon Allah to send messengers, and the second half asserts that after sending, faith in them becomes obligatory for us.
The word 'lakin' (but) signals the shift from divine freedom to human duty.
3
Connect to the warning
The poet warns against following whims (hawa) of people who were deluded. Reflect on who these people might be and how this warning reinforces the doctrine.
The warning likely targets those who claim obligation or deny messengers altogether.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمِنْهُ إرْسَالُ جَمِيعِ الرُّسْلِ فَلَا وُجُوبَ بَلْ بِمَحْضِ الفَضْلِ لَكِنْ بذا إيمَانُنَا قَدْ وَجَبا فَدَعْ هَوى قَوْمٍ بِهِمْ قَدْ لَعِبَا
And among it is the sending of all messengers—so there is no obligation, rather it is by pure grace. But by this our faith has become obligatory, so leave the whims of a people who have been played with.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage discusses the sending of prophets. The poet affirms that God sending all messengers (jami' al-rusul) is not an obligation upon Him (fa la wujub); rather it is an act of pure grace (mahd al-fadl). This refutes the Mu'tazilite view that God must send prophets for the guidance of humanity. However, the poet adds that because God has sent them, 'our faith has become obligatory' (imamuna qad wajaba)—meaning belief in them is now required. The poet warns against following the whims (hawa) of certain people who have been led astray or deceived.

Key Terms
إرْسَال
رُسْل
وُجُوب
فَضْل
إِيمَان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Allah is required to send prophets for human guidance.
Correction: The passage states that sending messengers is not obligatory (la wujub), but pure grace (mahd al-fadl).

Checkpoint
Why does God send messengers according to the passage?
A) Because it is obligatory upon Him.
B) Out of pure grace.
C) Because humans deserve it.
D) To increase human merit.

Key Takeaway
Allah's sending of messengers is a free act of grace, not a duty; but once sent, belief in them becomes required of us.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat belief in the prophets as a mere option, or do you recognize it as an obligatory response to God's grace that He sent them? Have you ever felt that God owes you guidance—and how does this poem correct that assumption?
?
The poet warns against 'the whims of a people who have been played with.' Where in your life have you followed your own desires rather than what the messengers brought? Will you today repent from following that hawa?
?
If God's sending prophets is pure grace, how does that change your attitude toward the Qur'an and sunnah? Are you grateful for the prophets, or do you take them for granted?

Prophethood

The Four Essential Attributes of Prophets
Al-Laqqani enumerates the four obligatory attributes of prophets and states that their opposites are impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four obligatory attributes of prophets according to the Ash'ari school: trustworthiness, truthfulness, intelligence, and conveyance, and why their opposites are impossible.

Core Idea
Prophets are protected by Allah with four essential qualities that ensure their message is reliable.

In this short passage from the Jawharat al-Tawhid, Al-Laqqani lays out the foundational attributes that every prophet must possess. These are not merely recommended; they are obligatory (wajib) and integral to the prophetic office. The poet also asserts that the opposites of these attributes are impossible for prophets, establishing their infallibility in these specific respects.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Carefully read the Arabic lines and their translation. Notice the poetic form and the concise listing of attributes.
2
Identify each attribute
List the four attributes mentioned: amanah, sidq, fatanah, tabligh. Understand what each means in the context of prophethood.
These are the essential qualities that make a prophet trustworthy.
3
Understand the implications of 'impossible'
The poet states that the opposites are impossible (yastahil). This means prophets cannot be treacherous, liars, foolish, or concealers of revelation. This is a key Ash'ari position on infallibility (isma).
4
Connect to the broader aqeedah
Reflect on why these attributes are essential: without them, the message of prophethood would not be trustworthy. This establishes the reliability of all prophetic revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَواجِبٌ في حَفِّهِمْ الأَمَانَهُ وَصِدْقُهُمْ وَضِفْ لَهَا الفَطَانَهُ وَمِثْلُ ذَا تَبْلِيغُهُمْ لما أَتَوا وَيَسْتَحِيلُ ضِدُّها كَمَا رَوَوْا
And obligatory in their protection is trustworthiness and their truthfulness, and add to that intelligence. And similarly their conveyance of what they brought; and their opposites are impossible, as they narrated.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage enumerates the necessary attributes (sifat wajiba) of prophets. The poet states that it is obligatory for prophets to be protected (fi hafihim) with al-amanah (trustworthiness), sidq (truthfulness), and al-fatanah (intelligence). Additionally, they must convey (tabligh) what they have been commanded to deliver. The opposites of these—treachery, lying, foolishness, and concealment—are impossible (yastahil) for them. These are standard Ash'ari articles: prophets must be immune from sin and error in conveying revelation.

Key Terms
أمانة
صِدْق
فَطَانَة

Prophets are endowed with four essential attributes—trustworthiness, truthfulness, intelligence, and conveyance—and are protected from their opposites, ensuring the reliability of their revelation.

?

Prophetic Humanity and the Essence of Islam
Al-Laqqani states permissible acts for prophets and defines Islam as the shahada.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that prophets are human beings with permissible physical needs, and that the essence of Islam is the testimony of faith (shahada).

Core Idea
Prophets are human; Islam is the shahada.

In this concise passage, Al-Laqqani clarifies two fundamental points: the humanity of prophets and the core of Islam. He affirms that prophets engage in permissible worldly acts like eating and marriage, countering exaggerated views. He then distills Islam to its essence—the testimony of faith—and advises leaving aside disputes. This lesson unpacks these twin teachings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the poet's compact style—two rulings in two lines.
The Arabic may have OCR inconsistencies, but the meaning is clear.
2
Identify the two rulings
Find the statement about prophets' permissible acts (eating and lawful intercourse). Then locate the definition of Islam as the comprehensive meaning of the shahada.
The poet uses 'جامع معنى' to indicate the essence.
3
Reflect on the implications
Why does affirming prophets' humanity matter? Why is Islam defined as testimony rather than actions? Consider how this shapes theological focus.
This connects to avoiding extremes in understanding prophethood and faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَجَائِزٌ فِي حَفِّهِمْ كَالأَكْلِ وكَالجِماعِ لِلِنِّـا في الحِل وَجَامِعُ مَعْنى الَّذِي تَقَرَّرَا شهادنَا الْإسْلامِ فَاطْرَحِ المِرَا
And permissible in their protection is like eating and like intercourse for women in lawful union. And the comprehensive meaning of what has been established is our testimony of Islam, so abandon disputation.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
The poet first clarifies that prophets are not superhuman in every sense; they may engage in permissible physical acts such as eating (akl) and lawful marital intercourse (jima' fi al-hill). This affirms their humanity. Then the poet gives a concise definition of Islam: it is the comprehensive meaning (jami' ma'na) of what has been established (taqarrara), namely the testimony (shahada) of Islam. The poet advises to abandon quarrelling (al-mira) about this. This likely refers to the Shahada (testimony of faith) as the essence of Islam.

Prophethood is a Divine Gift, Not Acquired
Al-Laqqani denies that prophethood is earned and affirms it as divine favor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that prophethood is not a human achievement but a pure bestowal from Allah, granted solely by His will.

Core Idea
Prophethood cannot be earned; it is a pure gift from Allah.

In this brief but profound couplet, al-Laqqani clears a fundamental misconception: prophethood is not a prize for human effort. Even if a person reaches the utmost peak of piety, they cannot acquire prophethood. It remains solely within Allah's discretion. This lesson examines the poet's assertion and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the strong negation 'وَلَمْ تَكُنْ' and the contrasting affirmation 'بَلْ ذَاكَ فَضْلُ اللَّهِ'.
The passage is a concise poetic statement; every word carries weight.
2
Identify the core claim
The poet asserts that prophethood is not acquired (مُكْتَسَبَة). This contradicts any idea that one can earn prophethood through effort or merit.
3
Understand the reason
The poet explains why: prophethood is a favor (فَضْل) from Allah, given to whom He wills. It is a gift, not a wage.
The phrase 'يُؤْتِيهِ لِمَنْ يَشَاءُ' emphasizes divine sovereignty.
4
Reflect on the theological lesson
Consider why this matters: it guards against thinking that humans can force Allah's choice or that prophethood is a natural result of perfection. It upholds Allah's absolute freedom.
This aligns with Sunni belief in divine bestowal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَمْ تَكُنْ نُبُوَّةٌ مُكْتَسَبَهْ وَلَوْ رَقَى في الخيرِ أَعْلَى عَقَبَة بَلْ ذَاكَ فَضْلُ اللَّهِ يُؤْتِيهِ لِمَنْ يَشَاءُ جَلَّ اللهُ واهِبُ المِنَنْ
And prophethood was not acquired, even if one ascends in goodness to the highest peak. Rather that is the favor of Allah; He gives it to whom He wills. Exalted is Allah, the Bestower of favors.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage refutes the idea that prophethood can be earned through personal effort. The poet states that prophethood (nubuwwa) is not acquired (muktasaba), even if a person reaches the highest level of goodness (a'la aqaba). Instead, it is a favor (fadl) from Allah, granted solely by His will. This counters rationalist views that prophethood might be attainable through perfection. The poet glorifies Allah as the Bestower of favors (wahib al-minan).

Correction: Prophethood is solely a gift from Allah, granted by His will, not attained by human striving.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage says 'rather that is the favor of Allah' – a direct refutation of earning salvation. In what area of your life are you trying to earn Allah's love through your own strength? Will you surrender that effort today and ask Him to be the Bestower, not you the achiever?

Muhammad: The Greatest of All Creation
Al-Laqqani declares Muhammad's superiority and ranks prophets above angels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position on the hierarchy of creation: Prophet Muhammad is the most virtuous, followed by the other prophets, and then the angels.

Core Idea
Muhammad surpasses all prophets and angels in virtue.

This passage from al-Laqqani's Jawharat al-Tawhid establishes a definitive ranking among Allah's creation. The poet declares our Prophet Muhammad as the absolute best, then orders the prophets after him, and the angels after them. This hierarchy is a foundational creedal point in Sunni Islam, guarding against any claims that angels might be superior to prophets.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and Translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal English translation. Let the poet's words speak directly before analysis.
Notice the rhyme and rhythm of the original.
2
Identify the Three Levels
The passage lists three groups in order of merit: Prophet Muhammad, then the prophets, then the angels. Find the Arabic words for each.
3
Understand the Warning
The poet says 'so turn away from discord' — this emphasizes the settled nature of this hierarchy. Avoid debate over this ranking.
This reflects the Ash'ari commitment to avoiding unnecessary conflict over clear texts.
4
Reflect on Miracles and Protection
The passage ends with miracles (mu'jizat) granted as honor, and divine protection (isma) as certain for prophets and angels. Consider how these support their status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَفْضَلُ الخَلْقِ عَلَى الإِطْلَاقِ نَبِيُّنَا فَمِلْ عَنِ الشَّقَاقِ وَالأَنْبِيَا يَلُونَهُ في الفَضْ وَبَعْدَهُمْ مَلائِكَهُ ذِي الفَضْلِ هذا وَقَوْم فَصَّلُوا إِذ فَضَّلُوا وَبَعْضُ كُلِّ بَعْضَهُ قَدْ يَفْضُلُ بالمُعْجِزَاتِ أُيّدُوا تَكَرُّمَا وَعِصْمَةَ الْبَارِي لِكُلِّ حَتّما
And the best of creation absolutely is our Prophet, so turn away from discord. And the prophets follow him in merit, and after them are His angels of merit. This is so, and a group made distinctions when they preferred, and some of each may surpass others. They were supported by miracles as an honor, and the protection of the Creator for each is certain.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage establishes the hierarchy of creation. The poet begins by stating that the best of all creation (afdal al-khalq 'ala al-itlaq) is our Prophet Muhammad, and advises turning away from disagreement (shiqaq). Then the prophets (al-anbiya) follow him in merit (fadl), and after them come the angels (mala'ikahu dhi al-fadl). This is the Ash'ari position: Prophet Muhammad is superior to all other prophets and angels. The poet acknowledges that some scholars made distinctions (fassalu) within these groups, as some individuals may surpass others (ba'd kull ba'dahu qad yafdul). Finally, the poet mentions that prophets and angels are supported by miracles (mu'jizat) as an honor (takarruman), and their protection (isma) from the Creator is certain (hatman).

Key Terms

خَلْق
نَبِي
أَنْبِيَا
مَلائِكَة
فَضْل
مُعْجِزَة
عِصْمَة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that certain angels (like Jibril) are superior to prophets.
Correction: The passage clearly states that prophets follow Muhammad in merit, and angels come after them, placing all prophets above all angels.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, who are the most virtuous after Prophet Muhammad?
A) The angels
B) The prophets
C) The companions
D) The martyrs

Key Takeaway
Prophet Muhammad is the absolute best of creation, followed by the prophets, then the angels — a settled hierarchy in Sunni creed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly honor Prophet Muhammad as the absolute best of creation in your heart, or do you sometimes value your own opinions, desires, or even other religious figures above his teachings? When you face a choice between what he commanded and what you want, which one wins?
?
The passage warns to 'turn away from discord' regarding Muhammad's superiority. Are you engaged in any arguments, comparisons, or discussions that undermine or question his unique rank? How can you actively defend his honor without falling into useless debate?
?
Knowing that Muhammad is the greatest of all creation, what concrete change will you make today to show that you believe this? Not just in words, but in a specific action that prioritizes his sunnah over your comfort or convenience.

Muhammad's Universal Prophethood and Finality of His Law
Al-Laqqani declares Muhammad's universal prophethood and abrogation of previous laws.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Muhammad was sent to all of creation and that his law is final and cannot be abrogated by any later religion.

Core Idea
Muhammad's sharia abrogates all previous laws and will never be replaced.

Al-Laqqani begins by asserting the uniqueness of the Prophet Muhammad. Allah completed and universalized religion through him. This means his sharia is the final and comprehensive law for humanity until the end of time. The passage then declares that previous religions are abrogated by his law, and no other law can abrogate his.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the key verbs tammama (completed) and 'ammama (universalized).
2
Identify the three claims
The passage makes three related claims: completion through Muhammad, his law not abrogated by another, and his law abrogates others. Find each claim.
The phrase 'hatta al-zaman yunsakh' means until the end of time.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why these claims are foundational. They establish the finality of prophethood and the permanent authority of Islamic law.
This is a core tenet of Muslim belief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخص خيرُ الخلق أنْ قد تَمَّمَا به الجميعَ ربُّنا وعمَّما بعثَهُ فشرعُهُ لا يُنْسَخُ بغيره حتَّى الزَّمانُ يُنْسَخُ وَنَسْـخُـهُ لِشَرْعِ غَيْرِهِ وَقَعْ حَتْمًا أَذلَّ اللهُ مَنْ لَهُ مَنَعْ شخَ بعض شرعِهِ بالبعض أَجِزْ وَمَا فِي ذَا لَهُ مِنْ غَضِ ومعجزاته كثيرةٌ غُرَرْ منها كلامُ اللهِ مُعْجِزُ البَشَرُ واجزِمْ بمعراجِ النَّبيِّ كما رَوَوْا وبرّئَنْ لعائشة ممَّا رَمَوا
And the best of creation was singled out in that our Lord completed and universalized everything through him. He sent him, so his law is not abrogated by another, until time is abrogated. And its abrogation of the law of others occurred necessarily; Allah humiliated whoever prevented His right. And abrogate some of his law with others, permit it; there is no anger in that. And his miracles are numerous, brilliant; among them is the Speech of Allah, miraculous to humans. And be certain of the Prophet's ascension as they narrated, and absolve Aisha of what they accused.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage covers several topics. First, the uniqueness of Muhammad: Allah completed and universalized everything through him (tammama wa 'ammama). His sharia is not abrogated (la yunsakh) by any other, until the end of time (hatta al-zaman yunsakh). He abrogated previous laws (naskhahu li-shar' ghayrihi waqa' hatman). Then the poet permits abrogation within his own law (ishkh ba'd shar'ihi bil-ba'd ajiz) and says there is no anger (ghadab) in that. Next, miracles: Muhammad's miracles are many, especially the Quran as the Speech of Allah, miraculous to humanity. Finally, the poet commands belief in the Prophet's Night Journey and Ascension (mi'raj) as narrated, and the exoneration of Aisha from the slander (ifk) against her.

تَمَّم
عَمَّم
نَسْخ
شَرِيعَة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that previous religions are still valid for their followers after Muhammad's mission.
Correction: The passage states that Muhammad's law abrogates all previous laws necessarily.

Checkpoint

A) It confirms them
B) It abrogates them necessarily
C) It ignores them
D) It contradicts them

Key Takeaway
Muhammad's prophethood is universal and final, abrogating all previous laws and remaining unchanged until the end of time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Examine your heart: is there any residue of desire for another path, even if only in hypothetical thoughts? What act of commitment can you make today to reaffirm that Muhammad's law is sufficient for all time?

Abrogation Within the Law of Muhammad
Al-Laqqani permits abrogation within Muhammad's law and denies divine anger.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that abrogation within Islamic law is permissible and does not imply divine anger or inconsistency.

Core Idea
Allah can change rulings within Islam; this is not a sign of anger but of wisdom.

After establishing the abrogation of previous laws, Al-Laqqani addresses a potential question: can Allah abrogate some rulings within Muhammad's own law? He affirms that this is permissible and that there is no anger (ghadab) in it. This clarifies that abrogation is part of divine wisdom, not a flaw.

Guided Path
1
Read the line about abrogation
Read the Arabic line 'شخَ بعض شرعِهِ بالبعض أَجِزْ وَمَا فِي ذَا لَهُ مِنْ غَضِ' and its translation. Note the command 'ajiz' (permit).
The Arabic is corrupt but the meaning is clear from context.
2
Understand permissibility
The poet says abrogation of some parts by others is permitted. This means later rulings can replace earlier ones within Islam.
3
Note the absence of anger
The phrase 'wa ma fi dha lahu min ghad' explicitly denies divine anger. This reassures that abrogation is not arbitrary.
This addresses theological concerns about God's immutability.

Miracles: Quran, Ascension, and Aisha's Exoneration
Al-Laqqani lists the Quran, Mi'raj, and Aisha's exoneration as miracles/signs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three key miraculous signs: the Quran as an enduring miracle, the physical Ascension (Mi'raj), and the divine vindication of Aisha from slander.

Core Idea
The Quran is the greatest miracle, and belief in the Mi'raj and Aisha's innocence is obligatory.

Al-Laqqani closes the poem by mentioning specific miraculous aspects of Muhammad's prophethood. First, the Quran itself is an everlasting miracle, unattainable by human effort. Second, he commands belief in the Night Journey and Ascension (Mi'raj) based on transmitted reports. Third, he underscores the exoneration of Aisha from the infamous slander, a story that demonstrates divine protection of the Prophet's household.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three items
The passage mentions the Quran as a miracle, the Mi'raj, and Aisha's exoneration. Find each in the text.
2
Understand the Quran as miracle
The Quran is described as 'Kalam Allah' and 'mu'jiz al-bashar' (miraculous to humans). Reflect on its inimitability.
This is a central proof of prophethood.
3
Affirm the Mi'raj and Aisha's innocence
The poet commands to 'be certain of the ascension' and to 'absolve Aisha'. These are matters of belief based on revelation.
Denial of these would contradict established Islamic teachings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخص خيرُ الخلق أنْ قد تَمَّمَا به الجميعَ ربُّنا وعمَّما بعثَهُ فشرعُهُ لا يُنْسَخُ بغيره حتَّى الزَّمانُ يُنْسَخُ وَنَسْـخُـهُ لِشَرْعِ غَيْرِهِ وَقَعْ حَتْمًا أَذلَّ اللهُ مَنْ لَهُ مَنَعْ شخَ بعض شرعِهِ بالبعض أَجِزْ وَمَا فِي ذَا لَهُ مِنْ غَضِ ومعجزاته كثيرةٌ غُرَرْ منها كلامُ اللهِ مُعْجِزُ البَشَرُ واجزِمْ بمعراجِ النَّبيِّ كما رَوَوْا وبرّئَنْ لعائشة ممَّا رَمَوا
And the best of creation was singled out in that our Lord completed and universalized everything through him. He sent him, so his law is not abrogated by another, until time is abrogated. And its abrogation of the law of others occurred necessarily; Allah humiliated whoever prevented His right. And abrogate some of his law with others, permit it; there is no anger in that. And his miracles are numerous, brilliant; among them is the Speech of Allah, miraculous to humans. And be certain of the Prophet's ascension as they narrated, and absolve Aisha of what they accused.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage covers several topics. First, the uniqueness of Muhammad: Allah completed and universalized everything through him (tammama wa 'ammama). His sharia is not abrogated (la yunsakh) by any other, until the end of time (hatta al-zaman yunsakh). He abrogated previous laws (naskhahu li-shar' ghayrihi waqa' hatman). Then the poet permits abrogation within his own law (ishkh ba'd shar'ihi bil-ba'd ajiz) and says there is no anger (ghadab) in that. Next, miracles: Muhammad's miracles are many, especially the Quran as the Speech of Allah, miraculous to humanity. Finally, the poet commands belief in the Prophet's Night Journey and Ascension (mi'raj) as narrated, and the exoneration of Aisha from the slander (ifk) against her.

Key Terms
مُعْجِزَة
كَلَام الله
مِعْرَاج
عَائِشَة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some deny the physical reality of the Mi'raj or question Aisha's innocence.
Correction: The passage commands definitive belief in both the Mi'raj and Aisha's exoneration.

Checkpoint

A) Splitting the moon
B) The Quran
C) Water from fingers
D) The talking tree

Key Takeaway
Belief in the Quran's miraculous nature, the physical Ascension of the Prophet, and the innocence of Aisha are essential Islamic tenets.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If someone close to you was falsely accused, would you defend them as firmly as Allah defended Aisha? Examine your own tongue: have you ever spread or entertained a rumor without certainty? What will you do differently?

The Hierarchy of Prophets and Angels
Verse 67 defines the hierarchy of prophets and angels in virtue.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ranking of prophets and angels in virtue after Allah, as stated in verse 67.

Core Idea
Prophets are first in virtue after Allah, then angels, with individuals excelling within each group.

This lesson clarifies a fundamental point of Islamic theology: the relative status of prophets and angels. The poet establishes that prophets are the highest creation in virtue after Allah Himself, followed by the angels. Some scholars add nuance that individuals within each group may differ in rank.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse
Read verse 67 carefully, noting the order and the additional detail about scholars who elaborated.
The verse contains two main points: the ranking and the possibility of internal excellence.
2
Identify the hierarchy
Identify that prophets are 'next to Him' (Allah) in virtue, and angels come after prophets.
3
Understand the scholarly detail
The phrase 'and some people detailed when they gave precedence' refers to scholars who further distinguished ranks within prophets and angels.
This shows the depth of theological discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٦٧ . والأَنْبِيَا يَلُونَهُ في الفَضْ وَبَعْدَهُمْ مَلائِكَهُ ذِي الفَضْلِ .. هذا وَقَوْم فَصَّلُوا إِذ فَضَّلُوا وَبَعْضُ كُلِّ بَعْضَهُ قَدْ يَفْضُلُ ٦٨ - بالمُعْجِزَاتِ أُيّدُوا تَكَرُّمَا وَعِصْمَةَ الْبَارِي لِكُلِّ حَتّما ٦٩ - وخص خيرُ الخلق أنْ قد تَمَّمَا به الجميعَ ربُّنا وعمَّما
67. And the prophets are next to Him in virtue, and after them His angels of virtue. This, and some people detailed when they gave precedence, and some of each may excel another. 68. They were supported by miracles as an honor, and the protection of the Creator is necessary for each. 69. And the best of creation was specified that he indeed completed and encompassed everything by our Lord.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is a set of three verses from the poem. Verse 67 states that the prophets are the highest in virtue after God, then the angels, with some scholars adding further detail that individuals within each group may excel others. Verse 68 establishes that prophets are supported by miracles as a divine favor, and that God's protection ('isma) is necessarily granted to every prophet. Verse 69 singles out the best of creation, Prophet Muhammad, who was given the distinction of completing and encompassing all previous messages and blessings by the command of Allah.

Abrogation of Previous Laws and Within the Prophet's Law
Three verses on the abrogation of other shari'as and permissibility of abrogation within Muhammad's law.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic doctrine of naskh (abrogation) regarding the finality of Prophet Muhammad's law, the abrogation of earlier divine laws, and the permissibility of abrogation within his own law.

Core Idea
Muhammad's law is final; it abrogates others and may abrogate itself.

These three verses from the Jawharat al-Tawhid establish the key principles of naskh. They affirm that the Prophet's shari'a is the last, that it necessarily supersedes previous revelations, and that some rulings within it can be replaced by later ones. Understanding this doctrine is essential for grasping the chronology and hierarchy of divine legislation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the finality of Muhammad's law
Verse 70 states that the Prophet's law is not abrogated by any other until the end of time. Notice the phrase 'حتى الزمان ينسخ' meaning until time itself ends.
2
Recognize the abrogation of earlier laws
Verse 71 affirms that the Prophet's law abrogates the laws of previous prophets as a necessary matter. Those who deny this are condemned.
3
Accept internal abrogation within his law
Verse 72 permits the abrogation of some rulings of the Prophet's law by later rulings within the same religion, denying any wrong in this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٧٠ - بعثَهُ فشرعُهُ لا يُنْسَخُ بغيره حتَّى الزَّمانُ يُنْسَخُ ، ٧١ - وَنَسْـخُـهُ لِشَرْعِ غَيْرِهِ وَقَعْ حَتْمًا أَذلَّ اللهُ مَنْ لَهُ مَنَعْ ٧٢ - شخَ بعض شرعِهِ بالبعض أَجِزْ وَمَا فِي ذَا لَهُ مِنْ غَضِ
70. His sending: his law is not abrogated by any other until the time is abrogated. 71. And its abrogation of the law of others occurred necessarily; Allah humiliated one who prevented it. 72. [And] abrogation of some of his law by some [other part], permit it, and there is no anger in this.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
These verses address the doctrine of naskh (abrogation). Verse 70 states that the Shari'a of Prophet Muhammad is never abrogated by any subsequent law until the end of time ('until the time is abrogated' meaning until the world ends). Verse 71 affirms that abrogation of earlier divine laws by Muhammad's law did occur, and it is necessary (ḥatmā), and those who deny it are humiliated by Allah. Verse 72 permits abrogation within the Prophet's own law (some rulings replaced by later ones), stating there is no objection to it. The poet uses 'أجِز' (permit) and denies any 'غض' (anger or disapproval).

Key Terms
النسخ
الشرع
الأزمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that Muhammad's law can be abrogated by a later prophet or law.
Correction: Verse 70 clearly states that his law is not abrogated by any other until the end of time.

Checkpoint
What does verse 70 say about the Prophet's law being abrogated?
A) It is abrogated by later prophets
B) It is not abrogated by any other until the end of time
C) It is abrogated only partly
D) It is abrogated by consensus

Key Takeaway
The Prophet Muhammad's shari'a is the final divine law; it abrogates all previous laws and may also abrogate some of its own rulings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Affirming the Prophet's Mi'raj
A verse commanding decisive belief in the Prophet's Mi'raj.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the obligation to affirm the Prophet's night journey and ascension (Mi'raj) with certainty, as transmitted in authentic reports.

Core Idea
Belief in the Mi'raj is a required certainty, not a matter of doubt.

In this verse, the poet commands decisive affirmation of the Prophet's Mi'raj, highlighting that it is a foundational belief transmitted through authentic sources. Before examining the text, consider why this event is often questioned and why the poet insists on certainty.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse carefully
Read the Arabic verse and its translation. Notice the imperative verb 'wa-jzim' (and be decisive) which indicates obligation.
The word 'kamā rawaw' (as they narrated) refers to the authentic hadith reports.
2
Identify the command
The first part of the verse commands certainty about the Mi'raj. Reflect on the strength of the language.
3
Understand the basis
The command is tied to 'as they narrated', meaning the reports of the companions. This shows the source of knowledge is revelation.
4
Address a common doubt
Some question the physical reality of the Mi'raj. The verse removes that doubt by demanding decisive belief.
This lesson aims to establish that certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Before reading the verse, recall that her innocence was declared in the Qur'an (Surah an-Nur), and this poetic reaffirmation strengthens that ruling.

Guided Path

Identify the command

3
Connect to Quranic context
This echoes the Quranic declaration of Aisha's innocence in Surah an-Nur, ayat 11-20. The verse reinforces that obligation.
The slander (ifk) is a historical event.
4
Apply the lesson
Believers must hold firmly to Aisha's innocence and reject any slander against her. This is part of love and respect for the Prophet's family.

Does the Prophet Benefit from Our Salat?
Author presents objection on Prophet's benefit from salat and scholarly opinion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly disagreement on whether the Prophet benefits from believers' salat and the proper context for discussing this view.

Core Idea
The Prophet may benefit from our salat, but speak of it only in teaching.

This passage addresses a subtle theological question: Does the Prophet Muhammad ﷺ benefit from the blessings (salat) we send upon him? Since salat from humans is a form of supplication (du'a), some scholars have debated whether it yields benefit for him. The author presents the view that he does benefit, while advising caution in expressing this opinion publicly.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Carefully read the Arabic and translation, noticing the structure: 'If you say... The answer is...'
The phrase 'عن الوزير. محي' is unclear; it may be a source reference.
2
Identify the scholarly disagreement
The passage states there is disagreement among scholars. Note the quoted opinion that he benefits like other prophets.
3
Understand the boundary

The Meanings of Salat and Salam from Allah, Angels, and Humans
The commentator defines salat from Allah, angels, and humans, and distinguishes it from gratitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinct meanings of the words salat and salam when attributed to Allah, angels, and humans, and why this matters for proper understanding of Islamic doctrine.

Core Idea
One word, three meanings: salat is mercy from Allah, supplication from humans, and seeking forgiveness from angels.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks the phrase 'thumma salam Allah' from the poem and clarifies what 'salat' really means when it comes from different beings. This is crucial because misunderstanding these terms can lead to anthropomorphic errors or misreading of Quranic verses. Let's see how the commentator distinguishes these meanings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Let the commentator's own definitions come through.
Pay attention to the footnote markers indicating annotations.
2
Identify the three definitions of salat
The passage gives three distinct definitions of salat: from Allah, from angels, and from humans. List each one and note the Arabic terms used.
These definitions are a key part of Islamic theology and exegesis.
3
Understand the scope of salat
The commentator notes that salat from Allah is not conditioned on being a response to a blessing, making it more general than gratitude. Ask yourself why this distinction matters.
4
Reflect on the meaning of salam
The phrase 'thumma salam Allah' is explained as a greeting fitting Allah. Consider how this relates to the concept of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(ثُمَّ سَلَامُ الله) (١) أي: تحيَّتَهُ اللَّائِقَةُ به، بحسب ما عنده تعالى. من (مع صَلَاتِه) أي: رحمتِه المقرُونَةِ بالتَّعظيم، أو مُطلقها. والصَّلاة من الله: الرَّحمةُ، ومِن الملائكة: الاستغفارُ، ومن الآدميين: التضرع والدُّعاء (٢). ولم يُشترط فيه أن يكون في مقابلة نعمة؛ فهو مِن هذه الناحية أعم من الشكر.
(Then the peace of Allah) — that is, His greeting that is fitting for Him, according to what He has. From (with His prayer) — that is, His mercy accompanied by magnification, or absolute mercy. And prayer from Allah is mercy, from the angels is seeking forgiveness, and from humans is supplication and petition. And it is not conditioned to be in return for a blessing; so from this aspect it is more general than gratitude.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the poet's phrase 'thumma salam Allah' (then the peace of Allah). The commentator interprets 'salam' as a greeting (tahiyyah) that is appropriate to Allah's majesty. The phrase 'ma'a salatih' (with His prayer) refers to Allah's mercy accompanied by glorification, or simply His mercy. The word 'salat' has different meanings depending on the subject: from Allah it is mercy, from angels it is seeking forgiveness (istighfar), and from humans it is supplication (tadarru') and petition (du'a'). The commentator then notes that this salat (i.e., Allah's salat) is not conditioned on being in response to a blessing, so it is more general than shukr (gratitude).

Does the Prophet Benefit from Our Salat? Two Scholarly Views
The commentator presents two views on whether the Prophet benefits from believers' salat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two scholarly opinions on whether the Prophet benefits from our salat and the proper intention when sending salat.

Core Idea
Scholars differ: does salat benefit the Prophet or just us? Both views teach humility.

When you send salat upon the Prophet, do you benefit him or yourself? Classic scholars offer two legitimate perspectives, and each comes with spiritual advice. Let's examine both views and the caution about speaking openly.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice the question-answer structure.
2

First opinion: Prophet benefits. Second opinion: benefit returns to the one praying.

Intention is key: salat is a means of drawing close to Allah, not a transaction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن قلت: هل ينتفع رسولُ الله صلى الله عليه وسلم بهذه الصلاة الصادرة منا، والتي معناها الدعاء له؟ فالجواب: أنَّ للعلماء في ذلك خلافًا. فقيل: إنه ينتفع بصلاتنا عليه كباقي الأنبياء، ولكن ينبغي للإنسان ألا يُصرح بذلك إلَّا في مقام التعليم. وقيل: المنفعة عائدة على المُصلي؛ لأنَّ النَّبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قد أفرغ الله تعالى عليه الكمالات، والكامل يقبل الزيادة في الكمال، غاية الأمر أنه لا ينبغي للمصلي عليه أن يلاحظ ذلك، كما أشرنا إليه، وإنما يلاحظ أنَّه بذلك يتقرب إلى الله لينال مقصوده.

Commentary

The second opinion is that the benefit actually returns to the person who sends the salat, because the Prophet has already received all perfections from Allah. Although the perfect can still receive increase in perfection, the one sending salat should not focus on that; rather, his intention should be to draw near to Allah to attain his own goal. The passage thus outlines two positions and cautions about public speech.

The Relationship Between Nabi and Rasul
Definition of nabi and its relation to rasul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between a nabi (prophet) and a rasul (messenger) in Islamic theology, and why the author of the poem chose the term 'nabi'.

Core Idea
A nabi is more general than a rasul; every rasul is a nabi, but not vice versa.

In this passage, the commentator explains the key term 'nabi' used in the opening line of the poem. He clarifies that a nabi is a human who receives divine revelation, whether or not he is commanded to preach, while a rasul is specifically commanded to convey the message. This distinction is crucial for understanding the author's choice of words and the broader theological hierarchy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Let the author's definitions sink in before analyzing.
2
Compare the definitions of nabi and rasul
Identify the precise definitions given: a nabi receives revelation of a law; a rasul receives revelation and is commanded to convey it. Notice the key difference: the command to convey.
The term 'shar'' refers to a divine law.
3
Understand the two reasons for using 'nabi'
The commentator gives two reasons: to harmonize with Quranic verse (Ahzab 56), and to indicate that the Prophet deserves blessings even more by virtue of messengership, since prophethood is more general.
The second reason uses the principle of 'first priority' (bi-l-awla).
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: why does this distinction matter for theology? How does it relate to the poem's emphasis on tawhid?
This distinction appears in many kalam texts; it's foundational.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عَلَى نَبِيٍّ جَاءَ بِالتَّوْحِيدِ وَقَدْ خَلَا الدِّينُ عَن التَّوْحِيدِ (عَلَى نَبِيٍّ): هو إنسان أوحي إليه بشرع، أُمر بتبليغه أم لا، فهو أعمُّ مِن الرَّسولِ، الذي هو إنسان أوحي إليه بشرع وأمر بتبليغه. وعكس بعضهم قال: لأن الرسل تكون من الملائكة، ولظاهر قوله تعالى: ﴿وَكَانَ رَسُولًا نَبِيًّا﴾ [مريم: ٥١] وقال السعد في المقاصد: متساويان. وإنما عبر المصنف بـ (نَبِيٍّ) دون (رسول) لأمرين: الأول: موافقة الآية الكريمة: ﴿إِنَّ الله وَمَلَائِكَتَهُ يُصَلُّونَ عَلَى النَّبِيِّ﴾ [الأحزاب: ٥٦]، والثاني: الإشارة إلى أنه يستحق الصلاة والسلام عليه بعنوان النُّبوة التي هي أعم من الرِّسالة؛ فيكون مستحقا لهما بعنوان الرِّسالة من باب الأولى؛ فإنَّ الرِّسالة حيث وجِدَت وجدت النُّبوة، ولا عكس.
Upon a prophet who came with tawhid while the religion had become devoid of tawhid. (Upon a prophet): He is a human to whom revelation of a law was given, whether or not he was commanded to convey it, so he is more general than the messenger, who is a human to whom revelation of a law was given and commanded to convey it. And some reversed, saying that messengers include angels, and due to the apparent meaning of His saying: 'And he was a messenger, a prophet' [Maryam: 51]. And al-Sa'd said in al-Maqasid: They are equivalent. And the author only expressed with (nabiyy) instead of (rasul) for two reasons: first, to agree with the noble verse: 'Indeed Allah and His angels send prayers upon the prophet' [Ahzab: 56]; second, to indicate that he deserves prayer and peace upon him by virtue of prophethood which is more general than messengership; so he deserves them by virtue of messengership even more so, because wherever messengership exists, prophethood exists, and not the reverse.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'nabi' (prophet) used in the poem. It begins with the line of poetry: 'Upon a prophet who came with tawhid...' Then the commentator defines nabi as a human being who receives revelation of a law (shar'), whether or not he is commanded to convey it. This makes nabi more general (a'amm) than rasul (messenger), because rasul is a human who receives revelation and is commanded to convey it. Some scholars reversed this, arguing that messengers can be angels and adducing the verse where the Prophet is described as 'rasulan nabiyyan.' Al-Sa'd in al-Maqasid considered them equivalent. The commentator then gives two reasons why the author chose 'nabi' over 'rasul': (1) to match the Quranic verse in Surah al-Ahzab, and (2) to indicate that the Prophet deserves salat and salam even more by virtue of messengership, since prophethood is more general and encompasses messengership.

Correction: This passage clarifies that a nabi is more general than a rasul; every rasul is a nabi, but not every nabi is a rasul. The key difference is the command to convey the revelation.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

The Two Linguistic Roots of the Word 'Nabi' (Prophet)
The commentator explains the etymology of 'nabi' from two possible roots.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two possible linguistic derivations of the Arabic word 'nabi' and how each produces the same word.

Core Idea
The word 'nabi' can come from 'news' or 'height', both fitting the prophet's role.

The word 'nabi' (prophet) is not a simple term; linguists have debated its root. The commentator presents two possibilities: one from 'naba' (news) and another from 'nabwah' (height). Each derivation carries nuanced meanings about the prophet's function. Let's explore these origins.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the two pronunciations and derivations. Then read the translation.
Notice that 'nabiyy' and 'nabi'' are both valid pronunciations.
2
Identify the first derivation (from naba')
Trace how 'nabi'' comes from 'naba' (news). Note the active and passive senses: the prophet as informer or informed.
The hamza version has only one derivation.
3
Identify the second derivation (from nabawah)
See how 'nabiyy' can also come from 'nabwah' (height). Again there are active and passive interpretations.
Phonological changes (softening hamza, assimilating ya) explain the forms.
4
Compare the two derivations
Notice that both roots yield the same word 'nabiyy' and both allow active/passive meanings, complementing each other.
This shows richness of Arabic and depth of prophetic concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقال (نَبِيُّ) بتشديد الياء، ويقال (نَبِيءٌ) بالهمزة في آخره. فأما المهموز فلا يحتمل في الاشتقاق إلا وجها واحدا، وهو أن يكون أصله النبأ وهو الخبر، والنبيء: فعيل منه إما بمعنى الفاعل؛ لأنه المخبر عن الله تعالى إن كان مع نبوته رسولا، ولأنه يخبر عن حال نفسه لكي يحترمه الناس إن لم يكن رسولا، أو بمعنى المفعول لأنَّ ملك الوحي يخبره. فأما النَّبِيُّ بتشديد الياء فيحتمل في الاشتقاق وجهين: أولهما أن يكون من النبأ، وأصله على هذا الوجه نبئ، فسُهلت الهمزة بقلبها ياء ثم أُدغمت الياء في الياء، ويأتي فيه الوجهان المذكوران. وثانيهما: أنَّ أصله من النَّبْوَةِ - بفتح فسكون - بمعنى الارتفاع، وأصله نَبِيٌّ على زنة عَلِيمٌ، فلما اجتمعت الواو والياء، وسبقت إحداهما بالسكون قلبت الواو ياء، وأُدْغِمت الياء في الياء. ويحتمل على هذا أيضا أن يكون فعيلا بمعنى فاعل لأنه رافع لشأن من اتبعه، وأن يكون بمعنى مفعول لأنه مرفوع المنزلة. وإنما عبر المصنف بـ (نَبِيٍّ) دون (رسول) لأمرين: الأول: موافقة الآية الكريمة: ﴿إِنَّ الله وَمَلَائِكَتَهُ يُصَلُّونَ عَلَى النَّبِيِّ﴾ [الأحزاب: ٥٦]، والثاني: الإشارة إلى أنه يستحق الصلاة والسلام عليه بعنوان النُّبوة التي هي أعم من الرِّسالة؛ فيكون مستحقا لهما بعنوان الرِّسالة من باب الأولى.
It is said (nabiyy) with a doubled ya, and it is said (nabi') with a hamza at the end. As for the one with hamza, it does not admit in derivation except one way: that its origin is naba' meaning news, and nabi' is fa'il from it, either in the sense of the active participle because he is the informer from Allah if he is a messenger with his prophethood, or because he informs about his own state so that people respect him if he is not a messenger, or in the sense of the passive participle because the angel of revelation informs him. As for nabi with a doubled ya, it admits two ways in derivation: the first is that it is from naba', and its origin on this is nabi', then the hamza was softened by changing it to a ya and then the ya was assimilated into the ya, and the two mentioned senses apply. The second is that its origin is from nabawah - with fatha then sukun - meaning elevation, and its origin is nabiyy on the measure of 'alim, then when the waw and ya came together and one of them was preceded by sukun, the waw was turned into a ya, and the ya was assimilated into the ya. And on this also, it is possible that it is fa'il in the sense of the active participle because he raises the status of those who follow him, or in the sense of the passive participle because he is of elevated rank. And the author only expressed with (nabiyy) instead of (rasul) for two reasons: first, to agree with the noble verse: 'Indeed Allah and His angels send prayers upon the prophet' [Ahzab: 56]; second, to indicate that he deserves prayer and peace upon him by virtue of prophethood which is more general than messengership; so he deserves them by virtue of messengership even more so.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is a linguistic analysis of the word 'nabi' (prophet). The commentator notes two pronunciations: nabiyy (with shaddah on the ya) and nabi' (with final hamza). For the hamza version, the derivation is straightforward: it comes from naba' (news), and can mean either the one who informs (active) or the one who is informed (passive). For the version without hamza but with shaddah, there are two possible derivations. First, it could also be from naba', with the hamza softened to ya and then assimilated, retaining the same two active/passive meanings. Second, it could be from nabawah (elevation), meaning 'height'. On this second derivation, it can again be active ('raiser' of followers) or passive ('raised' in rank). The commentator then reiterates the two reasons the author (al-Laqani) chose 'nabiyy' over 'rasul': to match the Quranic verse and to emphasize the broader title of prophethood, such that the right to salat and salam is even more firmly established.

Key Terms
نبي
نبيء
نبأ
نبوة
اشتقاق
فعيل
همزة
إعلال
إدغام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'nabi' has only one linguistic root (from 'naba' meaning news).
Correction: Linguists also propose a derivation from 'nabwah' (height), and both roots yield valid interpretations.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following are the two possible roots for the word 'nabi' (with shaddah)?
A) 'Naba' (news) and 'rasul' (messenger)
B) 'Naba' (news) and 'nabwah' (height)
C) 'Nabwah' (height) and 'ilm (knowledge)
D) 'Naba' (news) only

Key Takeaway
The Arabic word 'nabi' (prophet) can be derived from either 'naba' (news) or 'nabwah' (height), each providing complementary meanings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear the word 'prophet', do you think of them as people who bring news from Allah or as people of elevated status? Which aspect do you feel more connected to in your daily life?
?
If a prophet is both an informer and one who raises others, which role do you need to experience more in your own relationship with them? How can you actively seek that?
?

Why 'Nabiyy' Was Chosen Over 'Rasul'
The commentator gives two reasons for the author's choice of 'nabiyy' over 'rasul'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two reasons the author of Jawharat al-Tawhid used the word 'nabiyy' instead of 'rasul' in his poem.

Core Idea
The author chose 'nabiyy' to match the Quran and to highlight prophethood's broader scope.

In the famous poem Jawharat al-Tawhid, the author al-Laqani uses the word 'nabiyy' (prophet) rather than 'rasul' (messenger) when discussing sending blessings upon the Prophet. The commentator explains this choice with two key reasons: one linguistic and one theological. Let's examine them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation focusing on the two reasons given for choosing 'nabiyy' over 'rasul'.
The reasons are explicitly listed as 'first' and 'second'.
2
Understand the first reason
The author wanted to conform to the Quranic verse in Surah al-Ahzab (56) which uses 'nabiyy'.
This shows respect for Quranic phrasing.
3
Understand the second reason
Prophethood (nubuwwa) is a broader category than messengership (risala). Blessings due to prophethood imply blessings due to messengership even more so.
This is an a fortiori argument: general includes specific.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
By using the broader term, the author emphasizes that the Prophet's status as a prophet already merits blessings, and his status as messenger makes it even more certain.
This highlights the comprehensiveness of prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقال (نَبِيُّ) بتشديد الياء، ويقال (نَبِيءٌ) بالهمزة في آخره. فأما المهموز فلا يحتمل في الاشتقاق إلا وجها واحدا، وهو أن يكون أصله النبأ وهو الخبر، والنبيء: فعيل منه إما بمعنى الفاعل؛ لأنه المخبر عن الله تعالى إن كان مع نبوته رسولا، ولأنه يخبر عن حال نفسه لكي يحترمه الناس إن لم يكن رسولا، أو بمعنى المفعول لأنَّ ملك الوحي يخبره. فأما النَّبِيُّ بتشديد الياء فيحتمل في الاشتقاق وجهين: أولهما أن يكون من النبأ، وأصله على هذا الوجه نبئ، فسُهلت الهمزة بقلبها ياء ثم أُدغمت الياء في الياء، ويأتي فيه الوجهان المذكوران. وثانيهما: أنَّ أصله من النَّبْوَةِ - بفتح فسكون - بمعنى الارتفاع، وأصله نَبِيٌّ على زنة عَلِيمٌ، فلما اجتمعت الواو والياء، وسبقت إحداهما بالسكون قلبت الواو ياء، وأُدْغِمت الياء في الياء. ويحتمل على هذا أيضا أن يكون فعيلا بمعنى فاعل لأنه رافع لشأن من اتبعه، وأن يكون بمعنى مفعول لأنه مرفوع المنزلة. وإنما عبر المصنف بـ (نَبِيٍّ) دون (رسول) لأمرين: الأول: موافقة الآية الكريمة: ﴿إِنَّ الله وَمَلَائِكَتَهُ يُصَلُّونَ عَلَى النَّبِيِّ﴾ [الأحزاب: ٥٦]، والثاني: الإشارة إلى أنه يستحق الصلاة والسلام عليه بعنوان النُّبوة التي هي أعم من الرِّسالة؛ فيكون مستحقا لهما بعنوان الرِّسالة من باب الأولى.
It is said (nabiyy) with a doubled ya, and it is said (nabi') with a hamza at the end. As for the one with hamza, it does not admit in derivation except one way: that its origin is naba' meaning news, and nabi' is fa'il from it, either in the sense of the active participle because he is the informer from Allah if he is a messenger with his prophethood, or because he informs about his own state so that people respect him if he is not a messenger, or in the sense of the passive participle because the angel of revelation informs him. As for nabi with a doubled ya, it admits two ways in derivation: the first is that it is from naba', and its origin on this is nabi', then the hamza was softened by changing it to a ya and then the ya was assimilated into the ya, and the two mentioned senses apply. The second is that its origin is from nabawah - with fatha then sukun - meaning elevation, and its origin is nabiyy on the measure of 'alim, then when the waw and ya came together and one of them was preceded by sukun, the waw was turned into a ya, and the ya was assimilated into the ya. And on this also, it is possible that it is fa'il in the sense of the active participle because he raises the status of those who follow him, or in the sense of the passive participle because he is of elevated rank. And the author only expressed with (nabiyy) instead of (rasul) for two reasons: first, to agree with the noble verse: 'Indeed Allah and His angels send prayers upon the prophet' [Ahzab: 56]; second, to indicate that he deserves prayer and peace upon him by virtue of prophethood which is more general than messengership; so he deserves them by virtue of messengership even more so.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is a linguistic analysis of the word 'nabi' (prophet). The commentator notes two pronunciations: nabiyy (with shaddah on the ya) and nabi' (with final hamza). For the hamza version, the derivation is straightforward: it comes from naba' (news), and can mean either the one who informs (active) or the one who is informed (passive). For the version without hamza but with shaddah, there are two possible derivations. First, it could also be from naba', with the hamza softened to ya and then assimilated, retaining the same two active/passive meanings. Second, it could be from nabawah (elevation), meaning 'height'. On this second derivation, it can again be active ('raiser' of followers) or passive ('raised' in rank). The commentator then reiterates the two reasons the author (al-Laqani) chose 'nabiyy' over 'rasul': to match the Quranic verse and to emphasize the broader title of prophethood, such that the right to salat and salam is even more firmly established.

رسول
نبوة
رسالة

Key Takeaway
The author used 'nabiyy' to align with the Quran and to show that prophethood's broader scope ensures the Prophet deserves blessings even more than messengership would.

Objection on the Timing of Guidance by the Sword and Its Answer
Commentary refutes an objection about the conjunction 'fa' and historical timing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the concept of relative immediacy (al-taʿqīb) resolves the apparent contradiction between the poetic line's wording and the historical timeline of jihad.

Core Idea
Immediacy in Arabic grammar is relative to context, not clock time.

This passage addresses a subtle but significant objection about the order of events in the poet's description of the Prophet. The objection arises from the use of the conjunction 'fa' (then), which normally implies immediate sequence. However, history shows that jihad was not commanded until years later. The commentator resolves this by clarifying that 'immediacy' is a flexible concept, varying with context.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection: the conjunction 'fa' in the verse suggests that the Prophet guided by the sword immediately after coming with tawḥīd. But historically, jihad came after the migration, causing a seeming contradiction.
2
Identify the grammatical issue
The 'fa' indicates order and immediacy (al-taʿqīb). The objector argues this forces a literal immediate sequence.
The Arabic phrase is: الفَاءُ يَقْتَضِي التَّرْتِيبَ وَالتَّعْقِيبَ.
3
Learn the answer
The answer is: 'التَّعْقِيبُ فِي كُلِّ شَيْءٍ بِحَسَبِهِ' — immediacy is relative. It can mean without separation or with a long period, depending on context.
This principle avoids the contradiction.
4
Apply the concept
Reflect on how this same principle of contextual immediacy applies to other theological and linguistic discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالتَّعْقِيبُ فِي كُلِّ شَيْءٍ بِحَسَبِهِ. هَذِهِ العِبَارَةُ لِدَفْعِ اعْتِرَاضٍ وَرَدَ عَلَى عِبَارَةِ المُصَنِّفِ، وَحَاصِلُهُ أَنَّ جُمْلَةَ «أَرْشَدَ الخَلْقَ لِدِينِ الحَقِّ بِسَيْفِهِ» مَعْطُوفَةٌ عَلَى جُمْلَةِ «جَاءَ بِالتَّوْحِيدِ» الَّتِي هِيَ صِفَةٌ لِلنَّبِيِّ، وَحَرْفُ العَطْفِ الَّذِي هُوَ الفَاءُ يَقْتَضِي التَّرْتِيبَ وَالتَّعْقِيبَ، وَحِينَئِذٍ يَكُونُ المَعْنَى أَنَّ الرَّسُولَ أَرْشَدَ الخَلْقَ بِالسَّيْفِ عَقِيبَ مَجِيئِهِ بِالحَقِّ مِنْ غَيْرِ مُهْلَةٍ وَاقِعَةٍ بَيْنَ المَجِيءِ وَالإِرْشَادِ بِالسَّيْفِ، مَعَ المَعْلُومِ أَنَّ الإِرْشَادَ بِالسَّيْفِ الَّذِي هُوَ الجِهَادُ لَمْ يَحْصُلْ إِلَّا بَعْدَ مُضِيِّ مُدَّةٍ طَوِيلَةٍ إِقَامَتِهِ بِمَكَّةَ بَعْدَ البَعْثَةِ؛ لِأَنَّ الجِهَادَ لَمْ يُشْرَعْ إِلَّا بَعْدَ الهِجْرَةِ. وَمُلَخَّصُ الجَوَابِ: أَنَّ التَّعْقِيبَ فِي كُلِّ شَيْءٍ بِحَسَبِهِ، فَتَارَةً يَكُونُ مِنْ غَيْرِ مُدَّةٍ فَاصِلَةٍ بَيْنَهُمَا، وَتَارَةً بِطُولِ الزَّمَنِ.
And immediacy (al-taʿqīb) in every thing is according to its measure. This expression is to repel an objection that occurred to the author’s phrase. Its summary: that the clause 'He guided the creation to the religion of truth with his sword' is coordinated with the clause 'He came with tawḥīd' which is a description of the Prophet, and the conjunction 'fa (then)' necessitates order and immediacy. Then accordingly the meaning would be that the Messenger guided the creation with the sword immediately after his coming with the truth without any delay occurring between the coming and the guidance with the sword, whereas it is known that the guidance with the sword, which is jihād, did not happen except after a long period of his stay in Mecca after the mission, because jihād was not legislated except after the migration. And the summary of the answer: that immediacy (al-taʿqīb) in every thing is according to its measure: sometimes it is without a separating period between them, and sometimes with a length of time.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and its answer concerning the poetic line's phrasing. The objection is based on the use of the conjunction 'fa' (then) in 'He guided the creation to the religion of truth with his sword'. This 'fa' implies immediate sequence: that the guidance by sword occurred right after the Prophet came with tawḥīd. However, historically, jihād was not commanded until the migration to Medina, many years after the beginning of his mission in Mecca. So the apparent contradiction is that the literal reading suggests an immediate jihad, which is historically false. The answer is succinct: 'الْتَّعْقِيبُ فِي كُلِّ شَيْءٍ بِحَسَبِهِ' — immediacy is relative to each context. Sometimes 'immediate' means without any temporal gap at all; in other contexts, it can mean after a long period. Thus, the ordering can be stretched across the entire prophetic career, and the 'fa' does not force a literal minute-by-minute sequence. The commentator notes that this is a summary of a fuller discussion.

Key Terms
التَّعْقِيب
الفَاء
الجِهَاد
الإِرْشَاد بِالسَّيْف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The conjunction 'fa' always indicates immediate temporal succession.
Correction: Al-taʿqīb is relative; 'fa' can indicate order without literal immediacy, as explained by the commentator.

Checkpoint
What is the main objection addressed in this passage?
A) The Prophet never used a sword.
B) The conjunction 'fa' contradicts the timing of jihad.
C) The phrase 'religion of truth' is ambiguous.
D) The Prophet guided by sword only after migration, so the phrase is false.

Key Takeaway
The commentator resolves the objection by showing that 'immediacy' is relative; the conjunction 'fa' does not force a literal minute-by-minute sequence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse of the Qur'an or a hadith that seems to demand immediate action, do you pause to consider the context, or do you rush to apply it literally? How can the principle that 'immediacy is relative' save you from misunderstanding?
?
The poet’s phrase appeared contradictory to history. Have you ever rejected a religious text because it seemed to conflict with facts or your experience? How does this lesson challenge you to seek deeper understanding rather than dismiss?
?
In your life, do you become impatient when Allah's promises seem delayed? When you pray for guidance or relief, do you expect immediate results? How does the concept of context-based timing help you trust Allah’s wisdom over your own timeline?

The Name Muhammad: A Transferred Proper Noun
Explanation of the linguistic derivation and significance of the name 'Muhammad'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Prophet's name 'Muhammad' is a transferred proper noun derived from the passive participle of praise, reflecting his abundant praiseworthy qualities and the universal praise he receives from all creation.

Core Idea
The name 'Muhammad' itself proclaims his praiseworthiness.

Before exploring the prophetic title 'al-‘Āqib', the commentator examines the very name 'Muhammad'. This is not an arbitrary label but a name with prior usage, derived from the description 'praised'. By understanding its linguistic background, we appreciate that even the Prophet's name points to his exalted status and the hope that all beings would praise him—a hope that was realized.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the name Muhammad
Read the Arabic passage that discusses the name 'Muhammad'. Notice how the commentator explains that it is a 'transferred proper noun' (علم منقول) from the passive participle.
The term 'transferred' means the word was used before it became a proper name.
2
Understand the two possibilities for the missing nunation
The commentator notes that the nunation is omitted on 'Muhammad' either for poetic meter or because it is in a genitive construction (idāfa). Consider how this affects the meaning.
3
Reflect on the significance of the name
The commentator says the Prophet was named Muhammad because of his many praiseworthy qualities and the hope that all would praise him—and he was indeed praised. Contemplate how the name itself is a description of his character.
This is a reminder that even the Prophet's name is not just a label but a description.
