Statement of the meaning of religion, and its being devoid of tawhid before his mission. 4. Muhammad, the successor to the messengers of his Lord, and his family, and his companions, and his party. (Muhammad (1): is a substitute for 'a prophet' specifying him, and it is a proper noun transferred (2) from the passive participle of a doubled verb, by which our Prophet ﷺ was named, due to the abundance of his praiseworthy qualities, and the hope that the inhabitants of heaven and earth would praise him, and he was so (3). And he described him with 'al-‘Āqib' (4), which is the one by whose step people are resurrected (5), and not... (1) His saying 'Muhammad' with its nunation omitted for meter, like making the bā of 'al-‘Āqib' silent. And you can consider the omission of nunation as for idāfa; based on that it is a combination of name and title due to the implication of praise in 'al-‘Āqib'. Al-Amīr. (2) His saying 'and it is a transferred proper noun' i.e., not an improvised one (murtajal); the transferred is that which had prior usage before becoming a proper noun in other contexts, and the improvised is that which had no prior usage before becoming a proper noun. Al-Shanawāni. (3) In a copy: 'for that' and omitting 'kān', so the phrase becomes 'and the hope that the inhabitants of heaven and earth would praise him for that.' (4) His saying 'al-‘Āqib' is the one who comes at the end and the last, and that is due to the perfection of his rank, and in his lateness there is abrogation of the law of others, and also the greatest fruit in things comes at the end like water in digging a well. Al-Shanawāni. (5) It is reported in a hadith that the Prophet ﷺ said: 'I am al-‘Āqib, there is no prophet after me.' And the scholars interpreted al-‘Āqib – as the commentator mentioned – as the one by whose step people are resurrected. The meaning of 'they are resurrected by his step' is that they come to their Lord following his sunnah, his law, and his way. And they said: the meaning of his saying 'there is no prophet after me' is that no prophet's prophethood begins after him. Their intention by that is to guard against what is reported that Jesus son of Mary – peace be upon our Prophet and him – will descend at the end of time. He was only given the title al-‘Āqib so that his law, which is the most complete of laws and the most sufficient for what mankind needs in their immediate and future lives, would abrogate all laws. If you say: If we interpret al-‘Āqib as the seal of the messengers of God, then the author's saying 'to the messengers of his Lord' becomes superfluous, not indicating anything new. I say: It is better to make al-‘Āqib mean 'the seal' absolutely, without being restricted to the messengers of God being the sealed ones. On this, his saying 'to the messengers (of his Lord)' comes as a beneficial new meaning, and this is called tajrīd. Al-Miḥyī.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage provides a commentary on a line of the poem that mentions the Prophet Muhammad ﷺ with the title 'al-‘Āqib'. The commentator first discusses the name 'Muhammad' itself, explaining that it is a 'transferred proper noun' (ʻalam manqūl) derived from the passive participle of the verb ḥammada (to praise), indicating that the Prophet was named so because of his many praiseworthy qualities and the hope that all beings would praise him—which indeed happened. He also notes the omission of the nunation on 'Muhammad' for metrical reasons, and the possibility of treating it as an idāfa construction. The commentator then explains the title 'al-‘Āqib', which literally means 'the one who comes after'. He cites a hadith where the Prophet says 'I am al-‘Āqib, there is no prophet after me.' The scholars interpret 'al-‘Āqib' as the one by whose step people will be resurrected—meaning that people will be raised following his sunnah and sharia. The commentator addresses a potential objection: if 'al-‘Āqib' simply means 'the seal of the messengers', then the phrase 'to the messengers of his Lord' in the poem would be redundant. The response is to interpret 'al-‘Āqib' as 'the seal' in an absolute sense (without specifying whom he seals), so that the phrase 'to the messengers' adds new information. This rhetorical device is called 'tajrīd' (abstraction). The passage thus clarifies the meaning of these prophetic titles and defends the poem's wording.

Key Terms
محمد
علم منقول
مرتجل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the name 'Muhammad' is an invented name (murtajal) with no prior meaning.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that it is a transferred noun (manqūl) from the passive participle, meaning 'praised', and was used before it became his proper name.

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's name 'Muhammad' is a transferred proper noun meaning 'the praised one', reflecting his many praiseworthy qualities and the universal praise he receives.

The Title al-‘Āqib: The One Who Comes After
Explanation of the title 'al-‘Āqib' and the rhetorical device of tajrīd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of the prophetic title 'al-‘Āqib', the interpretation that people are resurrected following his step, and the rhetorical device of tajrīd used in the poem to avoid redundancy.

Core Idea
al-‘Āqib means not only the last prophet but the one by whose example people are resurrected.

After discussing the name 'Muhammad', the commentator turns to the descriptive title 'al-‘Āqib'. This title is rich in meaning: it indicates that the Prophet is the last of the messengers, and also that mankind will be resurrected following his law. The commentator also addresses a potential redundancy in the poem and introduces the rhetorical device of tajrīd to resolve it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on al-‘Āqib
Read the Arabic passage that discusses the title 'al-‘Āqib'. Note the hadith quoted and the interpretation given by the scholars.
2
Understand the interpretation of 'resurrection by his step'
The commentator explains that 'al-‘Āqib' means that people are resurrected following his step, i.e., following his sunnah and law. Consider why this interpretation is important.
3
Analyze the objection and the answer using tajrīd
The commentator presents an objection: if al-‘Āqib means 'seal of the messengers', then the phrase 'to the messengers of his Lord' would be redundant. The answer is to interpret al-‘Āqib as 'the seal' in an absolute sense, so that 'to the messengers' adds the new meaning that he seals them. This is tajrīd.

Statement of the meaning of religion, and its being devoid of tawhid before his mission. 4. Muhammad, the successor to the messengers of his Lord, and his family, and his companions, and his party. (Muhammad (1): is a substitute for 'a prophet' specifying him, and it is a proper noun transferred (2) from the passive participle of a doubled verb, by which our Prophet ﷺ was named, due to the abundance of his praiseworthy qualities, and the hope that the inhabitants of heaven and earth would praise him, and he was so (3). And he described him with 'al-‘Āqib' (4), which is the one by whose step people are resurrected (5), and not... (1) His saying 'Muhammad' with its nunation omitted for meter, like making the bā of 'al-‘Āqib' silent. And you can consider the omission of nunation as for idāfa; based on that it is a combination of name and title due to the implication of praise in 'al-‘Āqib'. Al-Amīr. (2) His saying 'and it is a transferred proper noun' i.e., not an improvised one (murtajal); the transferred is that which had prior usage before becoming a proper noun in other contexts, and the improvised is that which had no prior usage before becoming a proper noun. Al-Shanawāni. (3) In a copy: 'for that' and omitting 'kān', so the phrase becomes 'and the hope that the inhabitants of heaven and earth would praise him for that.' (4) His saying 'al-‘Āqib' is the one who comes at the end and the last, and that is due to the perfection of his rank, and in his lateness there is abrogation of the law of others, and also the greatest fruit in things comes at the end like water in digging a well. Al-Shanawāni. (5) It is reported in a hadith that the Prophet ﷺ said: 'I am al-‘Āqib, there is no prophet after me.' And the scholars interpreted al-‘Āqib – as the commentator mentioned – as the one by whose step people are resurrected. The meaning of 'they are resurrected by his step' is that they come to their Lord following his sunnah, his law, and his way. And they said: the meaning of his saying 'there is no prophet after me' is that no prophet's prophethood begins after him. Their intention by that is to guard against what is reported that Jesus son of Mary – peace be upon our Prophet and him – will descend at the end of time. He was only given the title al-‘Āqib so that his law, which is the most complete of laws and the most sufficient for what mankind needs in their immediate and future lives, would abrogate all laws. If you say: If we interpret al-‘Āqib as the seal of the messengers of God, then the author's saying 'to the messengers of his Lord' becomes superfluous, not indicating anything new. I say: It is better to make al-‘Āqib mean 'the seal' absolutely, without being restricted to the messengers of God being the sealed ones. On this, his saying 'to the messengers (of his Lord)' comes as a beneficial new meaning, and this is called tajrīd. Al-Miḥyī.

Commentary
This passage provides a commentary on a line of the poem that mentions the Prophet Muhammad ﷺ with the title 'al-‘Āqib'. The commentator first discusses the name 'Muhammad' itself, explaining that it is a 'transferred proper noun' (ʻalam manqūl) derived from the passive participle of the verb ḥammada (to praise), indicating that the Prophet was named so because of his many praiseworthy qualities and the hope that all beings would praise him—which indeed happened. He also notes the omission of the nunation on 'Muhammad' for metrical reasons, and the possibility of treating it as an idāfa construction. The commentator then explains the title 'al-‘Āqib', which literally means 'the one who comes after'. He cites a hadith where the Prophet says 'I am al-‘Āqib, there is no prophet after me.' The scholars interpret 'al-‘Āqib' as the one by whose step people will be resurrected—meaning that people will be raised following his sunnah and sharia. The commentator addresses a potential objection: if 'al-‘Āqib' simply means 'the seal of the messengers', then the phrase 'to the messengers of his Lord' in the poem would be redundant. The response is to interpret 'al-‘Āqib' as 'the seal' in an absolute sense (without specifying whom he seals), so that the phrase 'to the messengers' adds new information. This rhetorical device is called 'tajrīd' (abstraction). The passage thus clarifies the meaning of these prophetic titles and defends the poem's wording.

Key Takeaway

The Meaning of 'al-`Aqib' as the Seal of Prophethood
The commentator defines 'al-`Aqib' and answers an objection about the phrase 'li-rusuli Rabbihi'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the significance of the title 'al-`Aqib' given to Prophet Muhammad, its implications for the finality of prophethood, and how the phrase 'li-rusuli Rabbihi' in the poem adds a specific nuance.

Core Idea
The Prophet is al-`Aqib: the last prophet after whom no new prophethood begins, and even Jesus will follow his law.

The commentator begins by unpacking the title 'al-`Aqib' (the Last), one of the names of Prophet Muhammad. He explains that this title signifies not only chronological finality but also the superiority and abrogation of all previous laws. A potential objection arises concerning the phrase 'li-rusuli Rabbihi' in the poem, which the commentator resolves through a rhetorical device called tajreed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the full context of the commentator's discussion.
Pay attention to the objection and answer, as they involve a nuanced rhetorical point.
2
Understand the definition of al-`Aqib
Note how the commentator defines al-`Aqib as the one after whom no new prophet begins his prophethood, and how he supports it with the Prophet's hadith.
3
Examine the Jesus clarification
Notice the careful explanation that Jesus will descend but not as a new prophet; he will follow Muhammad's sharia.
4
Analyze the objection and answer
Look at the objection about redundancy and the commentator's solution using tajreed, which adds a new benefit to the phrase.

ووَصفَهُ بـ (الْعَاقِبْ) وهو الذي يُحشرُ النَّاسُ على قدمه، وليس بعده نبي تُبتدأُ نبوته. قوله: (العاقب) هو الذي يأتي في العقب والآخر، وذلك لكمال رتبته، وفي تأخره نسخ لشرع غيره، وأيضا الثمرة العظمى في الأشياء تأتي آخرها كالماء في حفر البئر. ورد في حديث أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: «أنا العاقب، فلا نبي بعدي» وفسر العلماء العاقب بأنه الذي يُحشَر الناسُ على قدمه، ومعنى (يُحشرون على قدمه) أنهم يأتون ربهم على سنته وشرعه وطريقه، وقالوا: المراد بقوله فلا نبي بعدي أَنَّه لا تُبتدئ نبوة نبي بعده، ومرادهم بذلك أن يحترزوا عما ورد أنَّ عيسى ابن مريم ينزل في آخر الزمان، وإنما كان له العاقب؛ ليكون شرعه الذي هو أكمل الشَّرائع وأوفاها بما يحتاج إليه البشر في عاجل حياتهم وآجلها ناسخا لكل الشرائع. فإن قلت: إذا فسرنا العاقب بأنه الخاتم الرسل وقع قول المصنف: «لرسل ربه» مستدركا لا يدل على شيء جديد. قلت: الأفضل أن نجعل العاقب بمعنى الخاتم مطلقا عن التقييد بكون المختوم به هم رسل الله، وعلى هذا يجيء قوله: «لرسل ربه» مفيداً فائدة جديدة، وهذا يسمى التجريد. وأما السيد عيسى وإن كان ينزل آخر الزمان، فلا تبتدأ نبوته بعد نبينا، بل ينزل حاكما بشرع نبينا محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم.
And He described him as (al-`Aqib) – he is the one upon whose footstep the people will be gathered, and there is no prophet after him whose prophethood is initiated. His statement: (al-`Aqib) is the one who comes in the succession and at the end, and that is due to the perfection of his rank, and in his being last is the abrogation of the law of other than him, and also the greatest fruit of things comes at their end like water in digging a well. It is reported in a hadith that the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, said: 'I am al-`Aqib, there is no prophet after me,' and the scholars interpreted al-`Aqib – as the commentator mentioned – as the one upon whose footstep the people will be gathered, and the meaning of 'they are gathered upon his footstep' is that they come to their Lord according to his sunnah, law, and path. And they said: the intent of his saying 'there is no prophet after me' is that no prophecy of a prophet is initiated after him, and by that they intend to guard against what is reported that Jesus son of Mary – prayers and peace be upon our Prophet and upon him – will descend at the end of time, and he is al-`Aqib only so that his law, which is the most complete of laws and the most sufficient for what humanity needs in the immediate and future life, may abrogate all laws. For if you say: If we interpret al-`Aqib as the seal of the messengers, then the author's statement 'for the messengers of his Lord' becomes redundant, not indicating anything new. I say: It is better to make al-`Aqib mean the seal absolutely, without restriction to the sealed being the messengers of Allah, and on this his statement 'for the messengers of his Lord' comes as beneficial with a new benefit, and this is called tajreed (abstraction). And as for the master Jesus, even though he will descend at the end of time, his prophethood is not initiated after our Prophet, rather he descends as a judge according to the law of our Prophet Muhammad, may Allah bless him and grant him peace.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage defines the title 'al-`Aqib' (the Last) given to the Prophet Muhammad. The commentator first states that al-`Aqib is the one upon whose footstep people will be gathered, and that after him no prophet begins his prophethood. He then expands on the meaning of al-`Aqib: it refers to one who comes at the end, both in sequence and in rank, and that his being last entails the abrogation of all previous laws. The commentator supports this with a hadith where the Prophet says 'I am al-`Aqib, there is no prophet after me.' The scholars interpret 'gathered upon his footstep' as following his sunnah and sharia. The commentator then addresses an important nuance: the hadith's phrase 'no prophet after me' is understood to mean no new prophethood begins after him, which safeguards against the case of Jesus, who will descend at the end of time but will not start a new prophethood; rather he will follow Muhammad's sharia. Next, the commentator raises an objection: if al-`Aqib simply means 'the seal of the messengers', then the poet's phrase 'li-rusuli Rabbihi' (for the messengers of his Lord) becomes redundant, since it already implied he is the last. The answer is that al-`Aqib should be understood as 'the seal' in an unrestricted sense, not confined to messengers; thus the phrase 'li-rusuli Rabbihi' adds the specification that the seal is specifically for the messengers of his Lord, which is a new benefit. This rhetorical figure is called tajreed (abstraction). The passage concludes by reiterating that Jesus does not start a new prophethood but rules by Muhammad's law.

Key Terms
العاقب
الخاتم
التجريد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Jesus will descend as a new prophet with his own sharia.
Correction: Jesus will descend as a follower of Muhammad's sharia, not initiating a new prophethood.

Checkpoint
What does the title 'al-`Aqib' mean according to the commentator?
A) The one who comes first
B) The one after whom no new prophet begins his prophethood
C) The one who gathers people on his footstep only
D) The one who abrogates all previous laws

Key Takeaway
The title 'al-`Aqib' affirms that Prophet Muhammad is the final prophet, and any later figure like Jesus will follow his law, not start a new one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly submit to the Prophet's sunnah as the complete and final law for every aspect of your life, or are there areas where you still look to other sources for guidance? Identify one such area and commit to aligning it with his teaching.
?
The commentator says the greatest fruit comes at the end. Have you placed your greatest spiritual efforts in following the Prophet's way, or are you distracted by lesser pursuits? What is one distraction you can remove today to focus more on his sunnah?
?
Do you accept that no scholar, leader, or movement can bring a new law that contradicts the Qur'an and sunnah? Is there any innovation you have adopted that you need to abandon in order to truly honor the Prophet as al-`Aqib? Repent from it and return to his path.

The Meaning of 'Rabb' and the Linguistic Form of 'Rusul'
Definition of 'Rabb' and linguistic note on 'Rusul'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic meaning of 'Rabb' as derived from tarbiyah and the reason for the sukun in 'Rusul' as used in the poem.

Core Idea
‘Rabb’ means Lord, Master, and originates from the concept of gradual upbringing.

Before diving into the theological discussion about prophethood, the commentator clarifies two key terms: 'Rabb' and the plural 'Rusul'. Understanding these will help you follow the later argument. The passage gives the lexical meaning of 'Rabb' and explains a metrical license in the poem that affects the pronunciation of 'Rusul'.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of 'Rabb'
Read the opening lines defining 'Rabb' as master, owner, and as a verbal noun from tarbiyah (upbringing). Understand that 'tarbiyah' means bringing something to its intended limit gradually.
Note that 'Rabb' can be used for multiple meanings, not just God.
2
Examine the plural form 'Rusul'
The commentator discusses the original pronunciation (with damma on ra and sin) and explains that the author used sukun on the sin for metrical reasons. This is allowed in Arabic poetry.
Compare with similar plurals like 'subur' from 'sabur'.
3
Understand the metrical license
The author's use of sukun is a poetic necessity, permissible in both singular and plural. The commentator gives the example of 'unuq' (neck) with sukun on the second letter.
This explains why 'Rusul' is written with sukun in the matn.

والرَّبُّ يُقال لمعانٍ، منها: السَّيِّدُ والمَالِكُ، وهو في الأصل مصدر بمعنى التربية، وهي: تبليغ الشَّيء شيئًا فشيئًا إلى الحد الذي أراده المُربِّي. الرُّسُل: جمع رسول، وأصله بضم الراء والسين المهملتين، كما قالوا: صُبر، وغفر - بضم أولهما وثانيهما ـ في جمع صَبور وغَفور، إلا أنَّ المصنف سَكَنَ سين الرُّسل لإقامة الوزن، وهذا سائغ في المفرد والجمع، وقد نطقوا في عنق ونحوه بسكون الثَّاني. وقول الشارح: «أي: لجميع الأنبياء» إشارة إلى دفع اعتراض ورد على عبارة المصنف، وحاصله: أنه صلى الله عليه وسلم خاتم للأنبياء كما أنه خاتم للرسل، وإثبات كونه خاتما لرسل الله لا يستلزم إثبات كونه خاتماً للأنبياء؛ لأن الرُّسل أخصُّ من الأنبياء، ولا يلزم من كونه خاتما للأخص كونه خاتما للأعم، ولو أنه قال: «الخاتم الأنبياء» لكان أحسنَ وأصرح؛ إذ يلزم من كونه خاتما للأنبياء كونه خاتما للرُّسل، لأنَّه ليس لنا رسولٌ إِلَّا وهو نبي، وحاصل هذا الجواب أن المراد بالرُّسل: الأنبياء، من باب إطلاق العام وإرادة الخاص، فهو مجاز مرسل. ولهذا الاعتراض جوابان آخران غير هذا الجواب؛ أولها أن في الكلام اكتفاء - وهو ما يذكره النحاة بعنوان حذف الواو مع ما عطفت ـ وتقدير الكلام: العاقب لرسل وأنبياء ربه، على حد قوله تعالى: (سَرَابِيلَ تَقِيكُمُ الخَرَ) [النحل: ۸۱] فإنَّ التقدير: - والله أعلم - تقيكم الحر والبرد، وثانيهما: أنا ندعي أنّ الرَّسول والنَّبيَّ بمعنى واحد، وليس أحدهما خاصاً والآخر عاما كما تقولون، بل هما متساويان، فكل رسول نبي، وكل نبي رسول، وهو رأي بعض المحققين، ويجوز أن يكون هذا الجواب ممَّا أشار إليه الشارح.
And 'Rabb' (Lord) is said for meanings, among them: the master and the owner, and it is originally a verbal noun meaning tarbiyah (upbringing), which is: conveying something gradually to the limit that the one who raises it intended. 'Ar-Rusul' (the messengers) is the plural of 'Rasul', and its origin is with damma on the ra' and the sin, as they said: 'subur' and 'ghufur' with damma on the first and second letters in the plural of 'sabur' and 'ghafur', except that the author made the sin of 'ar-rusul' sukun to establish the meter, and this is permissible in the singular and plural, and they pronounced 'unuq' and the like with sukun on the second letter. And the statement of the commentator: 'i.e., for all the prophets' is an indication to repelling an objection that occurred regarding the author's expression, and its summary: that he (peace be upon him) is the seal of the prophets just as he is the seal of the messengers, and proving that he is the seal of the messengers of Allah does not entail proving that he is the seal of the prophets, because the messengers are more specific than the prophets, and it does not follow from his being the seal of the more specific that he is the seal of the more general; and if he had said 'the seal of the prophets' it would have been better and clearer, because it follows from his being the seal of the prophets that he is the seal of the messengers, since we have no messenger except that he is a prophet. The summary of this answer is that what is meant by 'the messengers' is the prophets, from the category of using the general and intending the specific, so it is a majaz mursal. And for this objection there are two other answers besides this one: first, that in the speech there is iktifa' – which is what the grammarians mention under the heading of deleting the waw along with what it conjoins – and the estimated speech is: 'al-`Aqib li-rusuli wa-anbiya'i Rabbihi' (the last for the messengers and prophets of his Lord), analogous to His exalted saying: (sarabila taqikumu al-harra) [Al-Nahl 81], for the estimation is – and Allah knows best – 'taqikumu al-harra wa al-bard' (protects you from the heat and the cold); and second, that we claim that 'Rasul' and 'Nabi' are one in meaning, and one is not more specific and the other more general as you say, rather they are equal, so every messenger is a prophet and every prophet is a messenger, and this is the opinion of some verifiers, and it is possible that this answer is among what the commentator indicated.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage begins by defining the term 'Rabb' (Lord), explaining that it can mean master or owner, and that it is originally a verbal noun from 'tarbiyah' (upbringing), which is the gradual bringing of something to its intended limit. Then the commentator discusses the plural 'Rusul' and its pronunciation, noting that the author made the sin sukun for metrical reasons, which is permissible. The main discussion addresses an objection: the author's phrase 'li-rusuli Rabbihi' (for the messengers of his Lord) seems to imply the Prophet is only the seal of messengers, not of prophets. But since the Prophet is the seal of both, the commentator explains that the objection is repelled by the statement 'i.e., for all the prophets' (meaning the prophets are intended by 'messengers'). Because 'messengers' is more specific than 'prophets', proving he is seal of messengers does not automatically make him seal of prophets. However, if he had said 'seal of prophets', it would have been clearer because it would entail he is seal of messengers. The first answer given is that 'messengers' is used by way of majaz mursal (a type of figurative language) where the specific term (messengers) is used but the general (prophets) is intended. The commentator then provides two additional answers: (1) The speech contains an ellipsis, such that the intended meaning is 'the last for the messengers and prophets of his Lord', similar to the Quranic verse where 'heat' is mentioned but 'cold' is understood. (2) The terms 'Rasul' and 'Nabi' are synonymous, so there is no distinction; every prophet is a messenger and vice versa. This is an opinion of some verifiers.

Key Terms
الرب
الرسول
التربية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the original meaning of 'Rabb' according to the commentator?
A) Master and owner
B) Creator and sustainer
C) A verbal noun meaning upbringing (tarbiyah)
D) Lord and king

Key Takeaway
‘Rabb’ has a deep linguistic root in gradual nurturing, and ‘Rusul’ appears with a sukun due to poetic necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as if the One who nurtures you gradually knows exactly what you need at every stage, or do you sometimes demand instant growth from yourself and from others?
?
The word 'Rabb' implies a Master who owns you completely. Are there areas of your life where you still resist His ownership, acting as if you are your own master?
?
If God's lordship means He brings you to perfection step by step, what is one area where you have been impatient with His process, and what would it look like to surrender to His timing today?

Prophethood
intermediate
6 min
Three Answers to the Objection on the Seal of Prophethood
Three answers to an objection about the seal of messengers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the three answers given to the objection that 'seal of messengers' does not prove 'seal of prophets'.

Core Idea
Three linguistic and theological arguments show the Prophet is seal of both prophets and messengers.

The commentator encounters a potential objection: the poem's phrase 'seal of the messengers of his Lord' only explicitly mentions messengers, not prophets. Since the Prophet is clearly the seal of both, this needs explanation. The commentator provides three distinct answers, each drawing on different principles of Arabic rhetoric and theology. This lesson unpacks those answers so you can appreciate the depth of Islamic scholarly reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the passage that sets up the objection: 'he is the seal of the prophets just as he is the seal of the messengers...'. Identify why proving he is seal of messengers does not automatically make him seal of prophets.
The key is that 'messengers' is more specific than 'prophets'.
2
Examine the first answer: majaz mursal
The first answer is that 'messengers' is used figuratively for 'prophets' (general term intended by specific term). This is a type of figurative language called majaz mursal.
This is the answer the commentator says is indicated by the phrase 'i.e., for all the prophets'.
3
Study the second answer: iktifa' (ellipsis)
The second answer posits an ellipsis: the intended meaning is 'seal of the messengers and prophets', but the word 'and prophets' is omitted. This is similar to a Quranic verse where 'heat' is mentioned but 'cold' is understood.
Grammarians call this 'iktifa' – deletion of the conjunction and its conjunct.
4
Analyze the third answer: synonymy
The third answer claims that 'Rasul' and 'Nabi' are synonymous, meaning every prophet is a messenger and vice versa. This is the opinion of some verifiers.
This answer would make the objection moot because the terms are identical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the terms 'Rasul' and 'Nabi' are always distinct and that the Prophet being seal of messengers does not entail being seal of prophets.
Correction: The passage provides three valid interpretations that all confirm the Prophet is seal of both, using rhetorical and linguistic principles.

Checkpoint
According to the first answer, why does 'seal of the messengers' include the prophets?
A) Because every messenger is a prophet
B) Because 'messengers' is used figuratively for 'prophets' (majaz mursal)
C) Because the word 'prophets' is omitted but understood
D) Because the terms are synonymous

Key Takeaway
The objection that 'seal of messengers' does not cover prophets is answered by three distinct linguistic arguments, all affirming the Prophet's comprehensive seal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of the three answers strengthens your conviction that the Prophet ﷺ is the final prophet? How does that answer affect the way you receive his sunnah as the last divine guidance?
?
If someone objected that the Qur'an says 'khatam an-nabiyyin' (seal of the prophets) but the lesson discusses 'khatam al-mursalin', does this passage resolve your own doubt? What would you say to someone who uses this as a point of confusion?
?
The third answer equates 'rasul' and 'nabi'—a view that changes how we understand prophethood. Are you willing to hold that position if it means affirming the Prophet's finality without qualification? Why or why not?

Prophethood

The Finality of Prophethood: Jesus Descends as a Follower
The commentator refutes the idea that Jesus' descent implies a new prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the descent of Jesus at the end of time does not contradict the belief that Muhammad is the final prophet.

Core Idea
Jesus descends as a follower of Muhammad's law, not as a new prophet.

This passage addresses an important objection: if Jesus will descend with Muhammad's law, does that make him a new prophet? The commentator explains that Jesus' prophethood began before Muhammad, so his descent is a continuation, not a new beginning. This preserves the finality of Muhammad's prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on Jesus' descent
Read the Arabic passage beginning with 'بعده نبي تُبتدأُ نبوته' and its translation, focusing on the footnote about Jesus.
Notice the phrase 'فلا تبتدأ نبوته بعد نبينا' – his prophethood does not begin after our Prophet.
2
Identify the objection and refutation
The objection claimed that Jesus' coming with Muhammad's law is like the coming of previous prophets with Musa's law, who were considered independent prophets. The refutation states that for those prophets, their coming was the start of their prophethood, but for Jesus, his prophethood already existed.
The key difference is the timing of the beginning of prophethood.
3
Understand the answer to the jizyah objection
A secondary objection about jizyah being terminated by Jesus' descent is answered by saying that Jesus implements the existing law, not a new one.
This reinforces that Jesus acts within Muhammad's law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان معنى الدين، وخلوه عن التوحيد قبل بعثته .
بعده نبي تُبتدأُ نبوته (١) ، فهو بمعنى الخاتم بعثه وإرساله (الرسل ربِّه) أي: لجميع الأنبياء (٢)
والرَّبُّ يُقال لمعانٍ ، منها : السَّيِّدُ والمَالِكُ، وهو في الأصل مصدر بمعنى التربية ، وهي : تبليغ الشَّيء شيئًا فشيئًا إلى الحد الذي أراده المُربِّي .
(١) قوله تبتدأ نبوته وأما السيد عيسى وإن كان ينزل آخر الزمان ، فلا تبتدأ نبوته بعد نبينا ، بل ينزل حاكما بشرع نبينا محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم ، وبقوله: تبتدأ نبوته يسقط ما قيل: مجيء عيسى بشرعنا كمجيء أنبياء بني إسرائيل بشرع موسى، وقد عدُّوا أنبياء مستقلين، لقولهم: لا يشترط في الرسول أن ينسخ شرع من قبله ، ووجه السقوط أن أنبياء بني إسرائيل مجيئهم هذا هو بدء نبوتهم . إن قلت: ينافي التبعية للنبي رد الجزية التي قبلها النبي .
أجيب : بأنه تنفيذ الشرع النبي الله ، فإنه أفاد أنها مغيّاة بنزول عيسى . الشنواني . (٢) الرُّسُل : جمع رسول ، وأصله بضم الراء والسين المهملتين، كما قالوا: صُبر ، وغفر - بضم أولهما وثانيهما ـ في جمع صَبور وغَفور ، إلا أنَّ المصنف سَكَنَ سين الرُّسل لإقامة الوزن،
وهذا سائغ في المفرد والجمع ، وقد نطقوا في عنق) ونحوه بسكون الثَّاني . وقول الشارح: «أي: لجميع الأنبياء إشارة إلى دفع اعتراض ورد على عبارة المصنف، وحاصله : أنه الله خاتم للأنبياء كما أنه خاتم للرسل، وإثبات كونه خاتما لرسل الله لا يستلزم ا إثبات كونه خاتماً للأنبياء ؛ لأن الرُّسل أخصُّ من الأنبياء ، ولا يلزم من كونه خاتما للأخص كونه خاتما للأعم، ولو أنه قال: «الخاتم الأنبياء (به لكان أحسنَ وأصرح ؛ إذ يلزم من كونه خاتما للأنبياء كونه خاتما للرُّسل ، لأنَّه ليس لنا رسولٌ إِلَّا وهو نبي ، وحاصل هذا الجواب أن المراد بالرُّسل : الأنبياء ، من باب إطلاق العام وإرادة الخاص ، فهو مجاز مرسل ، ولهذا الاعتراض جوابان آخران غير هذا الجواب ؛ أولها أن في الكلام اكتفاء - و وهو ما يذكره النحاة بعنوان حذف الواو مع ما عطفت ـ وتقدير الكلام: العاقب لرسل وأنبياء ربه، على حد قوله تعالى: (سَرَابِيلَ تَقِيكُمُ الخَرَ) [النحل: ٨١] فإنَّ التقدير : - والله أعلم - تقيكم الحر والبرد، وثانيهما: أنا ندعي أنّ الرَّسول والنَّبيَّ بمعنى واحد ، وليس أحدهما خاصاً والآخر عاما كما تقولون ، بل هما متساويان، فكل رسول نبي، وكل نبي رسول ، وهو رأي بعض المحققين ، ويجوز أن يكون هذا الجواب ممَّا أشار إليه الشارح . محي.
Explanation of the meaning of religion, and its emptiness of tawhid before his mission. After him a prophet whose prophethood begins (1), so it means the seal of his sending and his mission (the messengers of his Lord) i.e., for all the prophets (2). And 'Rabb' is said for meanings, among them: the master and the owner, and it is originally a verbal noun meaning tarbiyah, which is: conveying the thing gradually to the limit that the educator intended. (1) His saying: 'whose prophethood begins' — and as for Sayyid 'Isa, even if he descends at the end of time, his prophethood does not begin after our Prophet, rather he descends as a ruler by the law of our Prophet Muhammad (SAW), and by his saying: 'whose prophethood begins' the objection is refuted that the coming of 'Isa with our law is like the coming of the prophets of Bani Isra'il with the law of Musa, and they were counted as independent prophets, because they say: it is not required for a messenger to abrogate the law of those before him. And the point of refutation is that the prophets of Bani Isra'il had their coming this as the beginning of their prophethood. If you say: it contradicts the following of the Prophet the refusal of jizyah that the Prophet accepted. The answer: it is the implementation of the law of the Prophet of Allah, for it indicates that it is terminated by the descent of 'Isa. al-Shanwani. (2) 'al-Rusul': plural of 'Rasul', and its origin is with dammah on the ra' and the sin, as they said 'subur' and 'ghufur' with dammah on the first and second in the plural of 'sabur' and 'ghafur', except that the author made the sin of 'al-Rusul' silent to maintain the meter, and this is permissible in the singular and plural, and they pronounced in 'unuq' and similar with sukun on the second. And the commentator's saying: 'i.e., for all the prophets' is an indication to repel an objection that occurred on the author's expression, and its summary: that He, Allah, is the seal of the prophets just as He is the seal of the messengers, and proving that He is the seal of the messengers of Allah does not entail proving that He is the seal of the prophets, because messengers are more specific than prophets, and it does not follow from His being the seal of the more specific that He is the seal of the more general. And if he had said 'the seal of the prophets' it would have been better and clearer, for from His being the seal of the prophets it follows that He is the seal of the messengers, because we have no messenger except that he is a prophet. And the summary of this answer is that by 'messengers' is meant 'prophets', from the category of using the general and intending the specific, so it is a metonymy. And there are two other answers to this objection besides this answer: the first is that in the speech there is iqtifa' (sufficiency by ellipsis)—and it is what the grammarians mention under the topic of deleting the waw with what it coordinates—and the estimation of the speech is: 'the successor to the messengers and prophets of his Lord,' analogous to His saying: 'garments that protect you from the heat' [16:81], for the estimation is—and Allah knows best—'protect you from the heat and cold.' And the second: we claim that the messenger and the prophet are one meaning, and one is not specific and the other general as you say, rather they are coextensive, so every messenger is a prophet and every prophet is a messenger, and this is the view of some verifiers, and it is possible that this answer is what the commentator indicated. Muhyi.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a line from the poem 'Jawharat al-Tawhid' that describes the Prophet Muhammad as the seal of the messengers. It begins by stating the topic: explanation of the meaning of 'religion' and its lack of tawhid before his mission, but quickly focuses on the phrase 'after him a prophet whose prophethood begins' to clarify that no new prophet comes after Muhammad, including Jesus, who will descend but not as a new prophet. The first footnote defends this by arguing that Jesus descends as a follower of Muhammad's law, not as a new prophet. The second footnote discusses the plural 'rusul' and addresses an objection: if the poem only says 'seal of the messengers,' does that guarantee he is also the seal of the prophets? The commentator explains that some hold messengers are a subset of prophets, so being seal of the subset doesn't entail seal of the larger set. Three answers are given: 1) the intended meaning is 'prophets' through metonymy; 2) there is an ellipsis and the full phrase is 'successor to the messengers and prophets'; 3) the terms 'messenger' and 'prophet' are coextensive. The passage preserves the theological nuance that Muhammad is the final prophet, and any coming of Jesus does not constitute a new prophethood.

Correction: The passage clarifies that Jesus descends as a follower of Muhammad's law, not as a new prophet.

A) By saying Jesus will not descend
B) By explaining that Jesus' prophethood began before Muhammad, so his descent does not start a new prophethood

?

Defending the Seal: Three Answers to the Objection
The commentator provides three linguistic and logical defenses for the phrase 'seal of the messengers' implying seal of all prophets.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the phrase 'seal of the messengers' is sufficient to affirm Muhammad as the seal of all prophets, through three linguistic and logical arguments.

Core Idea
The poet's phrase 'seal of the messengers' implies seal of all prophets via metonymy, ellipsis, or equality of terms.

An objection arises: if messengers are a subset of prophets, then calling Muhammad the 'seal of the messengers' does not necessarily mean he is the seal of all prophets. The commentator presents three powerful answers: metonymy (using 'messengers' to mean 'prophets'), ellipsis (the full phrase includes both), and the view that 'messenger' and 'prophet' are actually coextensive. These defenses preserve the unqualified finality of Muhammad's prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and first answer
Read the Arabic passage that presents the objection and the first answer (metonymy). Understand why the objection arises and how using 'messengers' to mean 'prophets' solves it.
The first answer is a form of مجاز مرسل.
2
Explore the second answer (ellipsis)
Read the second answer which claims there is an ellipsis (اكتفاء) in the original text, and the full phrase would include both messengers and prophets.
This is similar to the Qur'anic example of 'garments that protect you from the heat' implying also from the cold.
3
Analyze the third answer (coextensive terms)
Read the third answer that equates 'messenger' and 'prophet' in meaning. Understand that this is a valid scholarly opinion.
This eliminates the subset issue entirely.

Source Text

بيان معنى الدين، وخلوه عن التوحيد قبل بعثته .
بعده نبي تُبتدأُ نبوته (١) ، فهو بمعنى الخاتم بعثه وإرساله (الرسل ربِّه) أي: لجميع الأنبياء (٢)
والرَّبُّ يُقال لمعانٍ ، منها : السَّيِّدُ والمَالِكُ، وهو في الأصل مصدر بمعنى التربية ، وهي : تبليغ الشَّيء شيئًا فشيئًا إلى الحد الذي أراده المُربِّي .
(١) قوله تبتدأ نبوته وأما السيد عيسى وإن كان ينزل آخر الزمان ، فلا تبتدأ نبوته بعد نبينا ، بل ينزل حاكما بشرع نبينا محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم ، وبقوله: تبتدأ نبوته يسقط ما قيل: مجيء عيسى بشرعنا كمجيء أنبياء بني إسرائيل بشرع موسى، وقد عدُّوا أنبياء مستقلين، لقولهم: لا يشترط في الرسول أن ينسخ شرع من قبله ، ووجه السقوط أن أنبياء بني إسرائيل مجيئهم هذا هو بدء نبوتهم . إن قلت: ينافي التبعية للنبي رد الجزية التي قبلها النبي .
أجيب : بأنه تنفيذ الشرع النبي الله ، فإنه أفاد أنها مغيّاة بنزول عيسى . الشنواني . (٢) الرُّسُل : جمع رسول ، وأصله بضم الراء والسين المهملتين، كما قالوا: صُبر ، وغفر - بضم أولهما وثانيهما ـ في جمع صَبور وغَفور ، إلا أنَّ المصنف سَكَنَ سين الرُّسل لإقامة الوزن،
وهذا سائغ في المفرد والجمع ، وقد نطقوا في عنق) ونحوه بسكون الثَّاني . وقول الشارح: «أي: لجميع الأنبياء إشارة إلى دفع اعتراض ورد على عبارة المصنف، وحاصله : أنه الله خاتم للأنبياء كما أنه خاتم للرسل، وإثبات كونه خاتما لرسل الله لا يستلزم ا إثبات كونه خاتماً للأنبياء ؛ لأن الرُّسل أخصُّ من الأنبياء ، ولا يلزم من كونه خاتما للأخص كونه خاتما للأعم، ولو أنه قال: «الخاتم الأنبياء (به لكان أحسنَ وأصرح ؛ إذ يلزم من كونه خاتما للأنبياء كونه خاتما للرُّسل ، لأنَّه ليس لنا رسولٌ إِلَّا وهو نبي ، وحاصل هذا الجواب أن المراد بالرُّسل : الأنبياء ، من باب إطلاق العام وإرادة الخاص ، فهو مجاز مرسل ، ولهذا الاعتراض جوابان آخران غير هذا الجواب ؛ أولها أن في الكلام اكتفاء - و وهو ما يذكره النحاة بعنوان حذف الواو مع ما عطفت ـ وتقدير الكلام: العاقب لرسل وأنبياء ربه، على حد قوله تعالى: (سَرَابِيلَ تَقِيكُمُ الخَرَ) [النحل: ٨١] فإنَّ التقدير : - والله أعلم - تقيكم الحر والبرد، وثانيهما: أنا ندعي أنّ الرَّسول والنَّبيَّ بمعنى واحد ، وليس أحدهما خاصاً والآخر عاما كما تقولون ، بل هما متساويان، فكل رسول نبي، وكل نبي رسول ، وهو رأي بعض المحققين ، ويجوز أن يكون هذا الجواب ممَّا أشار إليه الشارح . محي.
Explanation of the meaning of religion, and its emptiness of tawhid before his mission. After him a prophet whose prophethood begins (1), so it means the seal of his sending and his mission (the messengers of his Lord) i.e., for all the prophets (2). And 'Rabb' is said for meanings, among them: the master and the owner, and it is originally a verbal noun meaning tarbiyah, which is: conveying the thing gradually to the limit that the educator intended. (1) His saying: 'whose prophethood begins' — and as for Sayyid 'Isa, even if he descends at the end of time, his prophethood does not begin after our Prophet, rather he descends as a ruler by the law of our Prophet Muhammad (SAW), and by his saying: 'whose prophethood begins' the objection is refuted that the coming of 'Isa with our law is like the coming of the prophets of Bani Isra'il with the law of Musa, and they were counted as independent prophets, because they say: it is not required for a messenger to abrogate the law of those before him. And the point of refutation is that the prophets of Bani Isra'il had their coming this as the beginning of their prophethood. If you say: it contradicts the following of the Prophet the refusal of jizyah that the Prophet accepted. The answer: it is the implementation of the law of the Prophet of Allah, for it indicates that it is terminated by the descent of 'Isa. al-Shanwani. And if he had said 'the seal of the prophets' it would have been better and clearer, for from His being the seal of the prophets it follows that He is the seal of the messengers, because we have no messenger except that he is a prophet. And the summary of this answer is that by 'messengers' is meant 'prophets', from the category of using the general and intending the specific, so it is a metonymy. Defending Ahl al-Fatrah: Answering the Objection
The author presents an objection and four answers defending Ahl al-Fatrah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four rational answers given by Sunni scholars to the objection that the Prophet's father is in Hell, and why these answers preserve the doctrine that Ahl al-Fatrah are saved.

Core Idea
Hadiths about the Prophet's father being in Hell are singular and cannot overturn decisive Quranic evidence.

Sunni theologians vigorously defend the salvation of those who lived in the 'interval' between prophets (Ahl al-Fatrah). A key objection is a hadith that the Prophet said his father was in Hell. This lesson examines the four responses that uphold the principle while explaining the apparent contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and first answer
Read the passage where the objection is raised and the first answer is given: the hadith is ahad and cannot override the decisive Quranic verse.
Note the distinction between ahad and mutawatir.
2
Understand the second answer
The second answer suggests that punishment of specific individuals is known only to Allah and His messenger.
3
Examine the third answer
The third answer limits punishment to those who changed religion without excuse, but the author notes scholars disagree with this.
This answer is weaker and not universally accepted.
4
Consider the fourth answer
The fourth answer posits that the punished might be followers of a previous prophet, like Jesus, whose law still applied.
5
Reflect on the overall strategy
Think about how each answer defends Ahl al-Fatrah by undermining the objection's force.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت: كيف هذا أخبر بأن جماعة من أهل الفترة من آباء النبي الصحابة في النار ، حيث سئل وهو على المنبر فقيل له أين أبي ؟ فقال: في النار . أجيب: بأن أحاديثهم آحاد لا تعارض القطعي وهو الآية ، وبأنه يجوز أن يكون تعذيب من صح تعذيبه منهم . الأمر يخص به علم الله ورسوله ، وبأن التعذيب مقصور على من غير وبدل من أهل الفترة بما لا يعذر فيه كعبادة الأوثان ، ولكن هذا لا يوافق إطلاق الأئمة ، ولا القول بأنه لا وجوب إلا بالشرع، وبأنه يمكن أن يكون من ثبت تعذيبه من أتباع من بقي شرعه إذ ذاك كعيسى واعلم أن المذهب الحق أن أهل الفترة على التحقيق ناجون، وأن منهم آباءه وكذا أمهاته ، من عبد الله إلى آدم ممن لم تبلغهم دعوة نبي ولا رسول، فكلهم ناجون وفي الجنة، ومحكوم بإيمانهم ولم يدخلهم كفر ولا رجس ولا عيب، ولا شيء مما كان عليه الجاهلية بأدلة نقلية منها قوله تعالى: ﴿وَتَقَلُّبَكَ فِي السَّاجِدِينَ ﴾ [الشعراء: ۲۱۹] ومنها قوله : «لم أزل أنتقل من الأصلاب الطاهرات إلى الأرحام الزاكيات إلى آخر الأحاديث البالغة مبلغ التواتر والقطع، وأما آزر فكان عم إبراهيم، وإنما دعاه بالأب على عادة العرب ولا يعول على غيرها ولا يلتفت إليه أصلا لضعفه أو نسخه ، هذا هو الحق الذي نلقى الله به. الحاصل أن أهل الفترة فيهم ثلاثة أقوال: أولها: وهو الصحيح أنهم لا يعذبون وإن غيروا وبدلوا أو عبدوا الأصنام.
If you say: How is this, when it is reported that a group of the People of the Interval from the Prophet's fathers are in Hell? He was asked while on the pulpit: 'Where is my father?' He said: 'In Hell.' The answer: It is that their hadiths are singular (ahad) and do not oppose the decisive (qat'i) which is the verse. And also, perhaps the punishment of those whose punishment is established among them is a matter specific to the knowledge of Allah and His messenger. And also, punishment is confined to those among ahl al-fatrah who changed and altered in a way for which there is no excuse, such as idol worship. But this does not align with the unrestricted statements of the imams, nor with the statement that there is no obligation except by the law. And also, it is possible that the one whose punishment is proven is from the followers of one whose law remained at that time, like Jesus. And know that the true doctrine is that, upon investigation, ahl al-fatrah are saved; and among them are his forefathers and likewise his mothers, from Abdullah to Adam, those who were not reached by the call of a prophet or messenger. All of them are saved and in Paradise, and they are ruled to have faith; no disbelief, impurity, defect, or anything of the Jahiliyyah entered them, due to transmitted evidences, among them His statement the Exalted: 'And your movement among those who prostrate' (al-Shu'ara: 219), and among them his statement: 'I have always been transferred from pure loins to pure wombs' and other hadiths that reach the level of tawatur and certainty. As for Azar, he was the uncle of Abraham; he called him 'father' as was the Arab custom, and no attention is paid to other reports due to their weakness or abrogation. This is the truth with which we meet Allah. The conclusion is that concerning ahl al-fatrah, there are three opinions: The first, which is correct: they are not punished even if they changed, altered, or worshipped idols.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer section. The objector points to reports that the Prophet's father and other ancestors are in Hell, contradicting the salvation of ahl al-fatrah. The author provides multiple answers: (1) These hadiths are ahad (singular) and cannot override the decisive Quranic verse. (2) Perhaps those specifically known to be punished are a special case known only to Allah and the Prophet. (3) Punishment is only for those who deliberately changed religion with no excuse like idol worship, but this contradicts other scholars. (4) Perhaps those punished were followers of a previous prophet whose law still applied (e.g., Jesus). The author then states the correct doctrine: ahl al-fatrah are saved, including the Prophet's forefathers from Abdullah to Adam, based on the Quranic verse 'turn among those who prostrate' and mutawatir hadiths about the Prophet's pure lineage. Regarding Azar (Abraham's father), he was actually his uncle; calling him 'father' was an Arab custom. The passage concludes by listing the first of three scholarly opinions on ahl al-fatrah: they are not punished even if they changed or worshipped idols.

Key Terms
أهل الفترة
آحاد
قطع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith about the Prophet's father being in Hell proves that Ahl al-Fatrah are punished.
Correction: The hadith is ahad and does not override the decisive Quranic evidence; the answers show it does not disprove the salvation of Ahl al-Fatrah.

Checkpoint
According to the first answer, why does the hadith about the Prophet's father not contradict the salvation of Ahl al-Fatrah?
A) The hadith is mutawatir.
B) The hadith is ahad and cannot override the decisive Quranic verse.
C) The hadith is about a different person.
D) The hadith was abrogated.

Key Takeaway
The four answers together show that the hadith about the Prophet's father is not evidence against the salvation of Ahl al-Fatrah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a hadith that seems to contradict a clear Quranic principle, do you suspend judgment until you seek scholarly clarification? Or do you accept it without question? This lesson challenges you to weigh evidence carefully—are you willing to do that even when it is uncomfortable?
?
The author refutes an objection using four distinct arguments. When you face doubts about your faith, do you seek comprehensive, multi-faceted answers, or are you satisfied with the first response you hear? Commit today to exploring your faith's intellectual defenses more deeply.
?
This lesson reminds us that final judgment belongs to Allah alone, especially for those who never received clear guidance. Is there anyone in your life—a family member, a friend, or even a historical figure—whose spiritual fate you have assumed without evidence? How will you leave judgment to Allah and instead focus on your own responsibility?

The True Doctrine: Ahl al-Fatrah Are Saved
The author states the true doctrine of Ahl al-Fatrah's salvation and provides evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitive Sunni doctrine that Ahl al-Fatrah are saved, based on Quranic and hadith evidence, and the clarification regarding Azar.

Core Idea
Ahl al-Fatrah, including the Prophet's ancestors to Adam, are saved and in Paradise.

After refuting the objection, the author lays down the correct doctrine: all those who lived in the interval between prophets, including the Prophet's own forefathers, are saved. This lesson presents the evidence and addresses the case of Azar, Abraham's uncle.

Guided Path
1
Read the positive doctrine statement
Read the passage where the author states that Ahl al-Fatrah are saved, including the Prophet's forefathers from Abdullah to Adam.
2
Examine the Quranic evidence
The verse 'And your movement among those who prostrate' is cited as proof of the Prophet's pure lineage.
Reflect on how this verse indicates the Prophet was always among believers.
3
Examine the hadith evidence
The hadith 'I have always been transferred from pure loins to pure wombs' is presented as mutawatir evidence.
4
Understand the clarification on Azar
The author clarifies that Azar was Abraham's uncle, not father, and the term 'father' was an Arab custom.
This resolves confusion about a potential exception.
5
Note the first opinion
The passage concludes that the correct opinion is that Ahl al-Fatrah are not punished even if they changed religion or worshipped idols.

فإن قلت: كيف هذا أخبر بأن جماعة من أهل الفترة من آباء النبي الصحابة في النار ، حيث سئل وهو على المنبر فقيل له أين أبي ؟ فقال: في النار . أجيب: بأن أحاديثهم آحاد لا تعارض القطعي وهو الآية ، وبأنه يجوز أن يكون تعذيب من صح تعذيبه منهم . الأمر يخص به علم الله ورسوله ، وبأن التعذيب مقصور على من غير وبدل من أهل الفترة بما لا يعذر فيه كعبادة الأوثان ، ولكن هذا لا يوافق إطلاق الأئمة ، ولا القول بأنه لا وجوب إلا بالشرع، وبأنه يمكن أن يكون من ثبت تعذيبه من أتباع من بقي شرعه إذ ذاك كعيسى واعلم أن المذهب الحق أن أهل الفترة على التحقيق ناجون، وأن منهم آباءه وكذا أمهاته ، من عبد الله إلى آدم ممن لم تبلغهم دعوة نبي ولا رسول، فكلهم ناجون وفي الجنة، ومحكوم بإيمانهم ولم يدخلهم كفر ولا رجس ولا عيب، ولا شيء مما كان عليه الجاهلية بأدلة نقلية منها قوله تعالى: ﴿وَتَقَلُّبَكَ فِي السَّاجِدِينَ ﴾ [الشعراء: ۲۱۹] ومنها قوله : «لم أزل أنتقل من الأصلاب الطاهرات إلى الأرحام الزاكيات إلى آخر الأحاديث البالغة مبلغ التواتر والقطع، وأما آزر فكان عم إبراهيم، وإنما دعاه بالأب على عادة العرب ولا يعول على غيرها ولا يلتفت إليه أصلا لضعفه أو نسخه ، هذا هو الحق الذي نلقى الله به. الحاصل أن أهل الفترة فيهم ثلاثة أقوال: أولها: وهو الصحيح أنهم لا يعذبون وإن غيروا وبدلوا أو عبدوا الأصنام.
If you say: How is this, when it is reported that a group of the People of the Interval from the Prophet's fathers are in Hell? He was asked while on the pulpit: 'Where is my father?' He said: 'In Hell.' The answer: It is that their hadiths are singular (ahad) and do not oppose the decisive (qat'i) which is the verse. And also, perhaps the punishment of those whose punishment is established among them is a matter specific to the knowledge of Allah and His messenger. And also, punishment is confined to those among ahl al-fatrah who changed and altered in a way for which there is no excuse, such as idol worship. But this does not align with the unrestricted statements of the imams, nor with the statement that there is no obligation except by the law. And also, it is possible that the one whose punishment is proven is from the followers of one whose law remained at that time, like Jesus. And know that the true doctrine is that, upon investigation, ahl al-fatrah are saved; and among them are his forefathers and likewise his mothers, from Abdullah to Adam, those who were not reached by the call of a prophet or messenger. All of them are saved and in Paradise, and they are ruled to have faith; no disbelief, impurity, defect, or anything of the Jahiliyyah entered them, due to transmitted evidences, among them His statement the Exalted: 'And your movement among those who prostrate' (al-Shu'ara: 219), and among them his statement: 'I have always been transferred from pure loins to pure wombs' and other hadiths that reach the level of tawatur and certainty. As for Azar, he was the uncle of Abraham; he called him 'father' as was the Arab custom, and no attention is paid to other reports due to their weakness or abrogation. This is the truth with which we meet Allah. The conclusion is that concerning ahl al-fatrah, there are three opinions: The first, which is correct: they are not punished even if they changed, altered, or worshipped idols.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer section. The objector points to reports that the Prophet's father and other ancestors are in Hell, contradicting the salvation of ahl al-fatrah. The author provides multiple answers: (1) These hadiths are ahad (singular) and cannot override the decisive Quranic verse. (2) Perhaps those specifically known to be punished are a special case known only to Allah and the Prophet. (3) Punishment is only for those who deliberately changed religion with no excuse like idol worship, but this contradicts other scholars. (4) Perhaps those punished were followers of a previous prophet whose law still applied (e.g., Jesus). The author then states the correct doctrine: ahl al-fatrah are saved, including the Prophet's forefathers from Abdullah to Adam, based on the Quranic verse 'turn among those who prostrate' and mutawatir hadiths about the Prophet's pure lineage. Regarding Azar (Abraham's father), he was actually his uncle; calling him 'father' was an Arab custom. The passage concludes by listing the first of three scholarly opinions on ahl al-fatrah: they are not punished even if they changed or worshipped idols.

Key Terms
أهل الفترة
نجاة
آزر
تواتر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Azar was the biological father of Prophet Abraham.
Correction: Azar was Abraham's uncle; 'father' was an Arab custom. The report to the contrary is weak or abrogated.

Checkpoint
How does the author explain the Quranic evidence for the salvation of Ahl al-Fatrah?
A) By citing the verse 'And your movement among those who prostrate' as proof of pure lineage.
B) By citing the verse 'We sent no messenger but with the language of his people'.
C) By citing the verse 'Every soul is held in pledge for its deeds'.
D) By citing the verse 'And we have not destroyed any town unless it had warners'.

Key Takeaway
The saved Ahl al-Fatrah include the Prophet's lineage, and Azar was not his father but his uncle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The Prophet's ancestors from Abdullah to Adam are all saved because they never received a call. But you have received the call through the Quran and Sunnah. If you die tonight, will you meet Allah with the same purity of faith as those forefathers? What one habit or belief needs to change right now?
?
The author says, 'This is the truth with which we meet Allah.' Can you truly say that about every aspect of your aqeedah? Where might you be following weak or abrogated reports instead of the clear truth? What will you do today to anchor your belief in certainty?

The Faith of the Prophet's Parents: Arguments and Refutations
Al-Amir argues for the salvation of the Prophet's parents and refutes objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key arguments used to affirm the faith of the Prophet's parents and the refutations of opposing claims.

Core Idea
The Prophet's parents are saved because nobility cannot coexist with disbelief.

This lesson presents a classical argument for the faith of the Prophet Muhammad's parents, a topic of theological importance. Al-Amir al-Kabir uses several lines of reasoning: the principle of nobility, the fatrah exception, and reinterpretation of problematic texts. We will examine the evidence and see how apparent objections are answered.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the density of arguments in a short text.
2
Identify the main arguments
Find the three main positive arguments: the fatrah analogy, the nobility principle, and the clarification about Azar.
These establish the parents' innocence.
3
Understand handling of counter-evidence
See how the author addresses the report from al-Fiqh al-Akbar and the hadith about seeking forgiveness.
The author offers multiple interpretations to reconcile.
4
Appreciate supportive sources
Note the use of poetry from al-Busiri and the weak hadith about revival as additional support.
These are not core proofs but reinforce the position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا كان هذا في أهل الفترة عموما فأولى نجاة والديه فإنه لا يحل إلا في شريف عند الله تعالى ، والشرف لا يجامع كفرا ، قال المحققون : ليس له أب كافر ، وأما آزر فكان عم إبراهيم فدعاه بالأب على عادة العرب ، وما في الفقه الأكبر لأبي حنيفة أنهما ماتا على الكفر ؛ فإما مدسوس عليه، بل نُوزع في نسبة الكتاب من أصله له ، أو يؤول بأنهما ماتا في زمن الكفر بمعنى الجاهلية وإن كانوا ناجينَ ، وغلط ملا علي القاري يغفر الله له، ومن العجائب ما نُسب له مع ذلك من إيمان فرعون اغترارا بالظواهر في ذلك ، ويرحم الله البوصيري حيث يقول: لَمْ تَزَلْ فِي ضَمَائِرِ الكَوْنِ تُخْتَا ولكَ الأُمَّهَاتُ وَالآباء وما وَردَ مِن نهيه عن استغفاره لهما أو نحو ذلك ؛ فمحمول على أنه قبل إخباره بحالهما ، أو لئلا يقتدي به أولاد من مضى من الكفار الإسرائيليين ونحوهم، على أنه قيل: أحياهما الله تعالى زيادة في الفضل، وآمنا به أنشد الغيطي في المولد للحافظ الشمس بن ناصر الدين الدمشقي : حبا الله النبي مزيد فضل على فضل وكان به رؤوفا فأحيا أُمه وكذا أبــــاه لإيمــان بـــه فضلا منيفـــا فسلم بالقديم بذا قدير وإن كان الحديث به ضعيفا.
If this is the case for the people of the fatrah generally, then more so will be the salvation of his parents. For he (the Prophet) does not descend except from a noble one in the sight of Allah, and nobility cannot coexist with disbelief. The verifiers (muhaqqiqun) said: He has no disbelieving father. As for Azar, he was Ibrahim's uncle, and Ibrahim called him 'father' according to Arab custom. And what is in al-Fiqh al-Akbar of Abu Hanifah that both died in disbelief: either it is interpolated into him, rather the attribution of the book to him is disputed, or it is interpreted to mean that they died in the time of disbelief (i.e. Jahiliyyah) while still being saved. Mulla 'Ali al-Qari made an error – may Allah forgive him. Among the wonders is what was attributed to him of the faith of Pharaoh, being deceived by external appearances. May Allah have mercy on al-Busiri where he says: 'You have never ceased in the consciences of the universe to be hidden, and the mothers and fathers are yours.' And what is reported of his prohibition of seeking forgiveness for them or similar: it is interpreted that this was before he was informed of their state, or lest the children of past disbelievers among the Israelites and others imitate him. Moreover, it is said that Allah revived them (the parents) as additional honor and they believed in him. Al-Ghayti recited in al-Mawlid from al-Hafiz Shams ibn Nasir al-Din al-Dimashqi: 'Allah gifted the Prophet with abundant favor upon favor, and He was kind to him, so He revived his mother and also his father for faith in him, a lofty favor. So submit that the Eternal is capable of that, even if the hadith is weak.'
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument for the faith of the Prophet's parents, based on the principle of nobility and the fatrah argument. The commentator begins with an analogy: if ahl al-fatrah are saved, then the Prophet's parents are even more deserving of salvation because the Prophet only comes from noble lineage, and nobility cannot coexist with kufr. He refutes the claim that Azar (Ibrahim's father) was a disbeliever, explaining that Azar was actually his uncle, called 'father' by custom. He then addresses the attribution to Abu Hanifah in al-Fiqh al-Akbar that the parents died in disbelief: this is either an interpolation, the book's attribution is doubted, or it means they died in Jahiliyyah (time of ignorance) while still being saved. He criticizes Mulla 'Ali al-Qari for errors, including a claim about Pharaoh's faith. He cites al-Busiri's poetry affirming the parents' faith. Regarding the hadith forbidding seeking forgiveness for the parents, he interprets it as either before being informed of their fate, or to prevent imitating disbelieving parents. Finally, he mentions that some say Allah revived them to believe as an honor, citing a weak hadith from al-Ghayti. The overall argument is that the parents are believers and saved.

والدي النبي
آزر
الفقه الأكبر
الجاهلية
ملا علي القاري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some claim the Prophet's parents died in disbelief based on a report from al-Fiqh al-Akbar.
Correction: The passage argues that report is either interpolated, the book's attribution is disputed, or it means they died in Jahiliyyah but are saved.

Checkpoint

B) By claiming they were never born

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's parents are affirmed as believers through reasoning from nobility, fatrah, and careful interpretation of opposing texts.

?
Mulla 'Ali al-Qari was deceived by external appearances into thinking Pharaoh believed. Where in your own life have you been deceived by externals—whether about people's faith, the strength of a proof, or your own spiritual state? Will you commit to testing your assumptions against the Quran and the way of the scholars, or will you remain content with surface-level understanding?
?
The passage offers multiple interpretations to defend the parents' faith, including the possibility of a weak hadith. Applying the Three Judgments to the Prophets
Explains the analogical extension of rational judgments to prophets.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the three rational judgments (wajib, mustahil, ja'iz) apply to prophets analogically rather than identically with God, and why the author specifies 'messengers' in this context.

Core Idea
The judgments for prophets are like those for God — but not the same.

The previous lesson established that every legally responsible person must know the three rational judgments concerning God. Now, the author extends this framework to the prophets. But he uses the word 'like' (mithl) to warn us: the similarity is in category, not in exact content. This lesson unpacks that careful distinction and explains why he speaks of messengers specifically.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and footnote
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the word 'mithl' (like) which is the key term.
The footnote is part of the author's original text.
2
Identify the core distinction
Understand that the author uses 'mithl' to indicate that the judgments for prophets are similar in category but not identical to those for God. The specifics and evidence differ.
3
Analyze the choice of 'messengers'
Note that the author specifies 'rusul' (messengers) rather than 'anbiya' (prophets). The footnote explains this is because some obligations (like delivering the message) are unique to messengers.
This is a precise technical choice.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: How does this prevent confusion between the Creator and His creatures? The analogy preserves distinction while extending the rational framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وَمِثْل ذَا) (۲) أي: ويجب بالشَّرع أيضاً على كلِّ مكلَّفٍ أَنْ يَعرِفَ مثل
(٢) أشار المصنف الله بلفظ : «مثل» إلى أنّ الواجب في حقّ الأنبياء عليهم الصلاة والسلام، والمستحيل والجائز ليست هي عين الواجب في حقه ، والجائز والمستحيل، فالمراد المثلية في مطلق واجب ومستحيل وجائز ، وإن اختلفت الأفراد والأدلة ، وإنما خص المصنف الرُّسلَ ، ولم يذكر الأنبياء ؛ لأنَّ بعض ما سيأتي ذكره من الواجب لهم خاص بالرسل مثل التبليغ . محي.
('And like this') (2) i.e., it is also obligatory by the Law upon every legally responsible person to know the like...
(2) The author—may God [bless him]—indicated by the word 'like' (mithl) that the obligatory, the impossible, and the possible concerning the prophets—peace and blessings be upon them—are not the very same as the obligatory concerning Him, and the possible and the impossible; rather the intent is similarity (al-mithliyya) in the absolute obligatory, impossible, and possible, even if the individuals and the evidences differ. And the author specified the messengers and did not mention the prophets, because some of what will come later concerning the obligatory for them is specific to messengers, such as delivering the message. End.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage addresses the extension of the three rational judgments (wājib, mustaḥīl, jāʾiz) to the prophets. The phrase 'wa-mithl dhā' (and like this) indicates that every legally responsible person must know a similar set of categories concerning the prophets. The footnote explains that the author used the word 'like' (mithl) deliberately: the obligatory, impossible, and possible concerning the prophets are not exactly identical to those concerning God; rather, they are analogous (al-mithliyya) in a general sense. The specific content may differ. For example, while it is obligatory for God to be truthful, it is also obligatory for prophets to be truthful, but the basis and details differ. The footnote also clarifies why the author specifically mentioned 'messengers' (rusul) rather than 'prophets' (anbiyāʾ): some obligations unique to messengers (like delivering the message) will be listed later. Thus, the lesson shows that the conceptual framework applies to both God and prophets, but with due recognition of the difference between Creator and creatures.

Key Terms
المثلية (al-mithliyya)
الأنبياء (al-anbiyāʾ)
الرسل (al-rusul)
التبليغ (al-tablīgh)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The three rational judgments apply identically to God and prophets.
Correction: They are similar in category but differ in specifics and evidence.

Checkpoint
Why did the author specify 'messengers' (rusul) rather than 'prophets' (anbiyā') in this context?
A) Because prophets are not obligated to know these judgments
B) Because some obligations are unique to messengers, like delivering the message
C) Because messengers are higher in rank
D) Because the author made a mistake

Key Takeaway
The three rational judgments for prophets are analogous to those for God, not identical, and the term 'messengers' is used because some obligations are unique to them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that prophets are truthful and trustworthy, how does that challenge your willingness to accept their teachings even when they contradict your desires? What one teaching are you most resistant to submitting to right now?

The passage says the judgments for prophets are 'like' those for God but not identical. Where in your life have you substituted your own judgment for the prophetic message, treating yourself as if you had the same authority as a prophet? How can you realign your actions to reflect that you are not a prophet but must follow them?
?
Prophets bear the unique obligation of delivering the message. How have you taken up your own obligation to convey what you know of the truth to others? Where have you remained silent out of fear or laziness, and what will you do this week to fulfill that duty in a small but concrete way?

The Necessity of Believing in Qada' and Qadr
Summarizes the Ash'ari/Maturidi position on qada' and qadr with a proof from Hadith Jibril.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that belief in qada' and qadr is an essential part of iman, based on the Ash'ari/Maturidi position and the Hadith of Jibril.

Core Idea
Belief in qada' and qadr is not optional; it's a pillar of iman itself.

This lesson presents the core Ash'ari/Maturidi doctrine on divine decree. It establishes that a responsible believer must affirm that God's eternal knowledge encompasses all actions and that He creates them according to that knowledge. The key proof comes directly from the Prophet's explanation of iman to Jibril, where qada' and qadr are explicitly listed among its pillars.

Guided Path
1
Read the doctrinal summary
The passage opens with the obligatory belief: God knew all actions eternally and created them according to that knowledge. Grasp this foundational statement.
2
Understand why it's essential for iman
Notice the phrase 'without which faith cannot be realized.' This shows the centrality of qada' and qadr.
3
Examine the prophetic proof
The Hadith of Jibril is quoted as evidence. Consider how the Prophet listed qada' and qadr among the items of iman.
4
Reflect on the scholarly nuance
The passage mentions a debate about whether the Prophet was defining iman's reality or its characteristics. This nuance doesn't affect the inclusion of qada' and qadr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخلاصة ما ذهب إليه أهل السنة والجماعة في هذه المسألة: أنَّه يجب على المُكلَّف أنْ يؤمن بأنَّ الله سبحانه عَلِم أزلا بجميع أفعال العباد ، وأنه أوجَدَها ـ حين أوجدها فيما لا يزال ـ على القَدْرِ المَخصُوص والوَجْه المُعيَّن الذي سبق العلم به ، بل إنَّ ذلك ممَّا لا يتحقَّقُ الإيمان إلا به ، والدليل على ذلك أنَّ النبيَّ الله لما بيَّن لجبريل الإيمان ـ سواء قلنا إنه بين حقيقة الإيمان بناءً على أنَّ الأعمال جزء منه، أو قلنا إنَّه إنَّما بيَّن خِصال الإيمان، أي: مُتعلّق الإيمان - ذَكَرَ الإيمان بالقضاء والقدر في ضمن ذلك، وذلك قوله: الإيمان أن تؤمن بالله وملائكته وكتبه ورسله، والقضاء والقدر، خيره وشره، وحلوه ومره.
The summary of what Ahl al-Sunna wal-Jama'a hold in this matter: that it is obligatory upon the responsible person (mukallaf) to believe that God, exalted, knew eternally all the actions of the servants, and that He brought them into existence—when He brings them into existence in what is yet to come—according to the specific measure and the particular way that preceded in knowledge. Indeed, this is something without which faith (iman) cannot be realized. The proof of that is that the Prophet, when he explained iman to Jibril—whether we say he explained the reality of iman based on the view that actions are part of it, or we say he only explained the characteristics of iman, i.e., the objects of iman—he mentioned belief in al-qada' wa al-qadr within that. That is his statement: 'Iman is that you believe in God, His angels, His books, His messengers, and al-qada' wa al-qadr, its good and its evil, its sweet and its bitter.'
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'ari/Maturidi position on qada' and qadr. It states that a mukallaf must believe God eternally knew all actions and created them according to His prior knowledge. This belief is essential for iman. The proof is from the Hadith of Jibril, where the Prophet listed belief in qada' and qadr as a pillar of iman. The passage notes a scholarly nuance: whether the Prophet was defining iman's essence or its characteristics, the inclusion of qada' and qadr is clear.

Key Terms
القضاء والقدر
الإيمان
المكلَّف
علم أزلا
حديث جبريل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think iman is only belief in God, angels, books, messengers, and the Last Day, without including qada' and qadr.
Correction: This passage shows that belief in qada' and qadr is explicitly mentioned by the Prophet as part of iman, and without it iman is not realized.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the proof that belief in qada' and qadr is part of iman?
A) A rational argument that actions cannot happen without God's knowledge.
B) The Hadith of Jibril where the Prophet mentions it.
C) The consensus of scholars.
D) The Quranic verse on predestination.

Key Takeaway
Belief in qada' and qadr is an essential component of iman, as proven by the Hadith of Jibril.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face hardships, do you truly believe that God's decree encompasses both its good and its evil, its sweet and its bitter? Or do you complain against the qadr when it is bitter? How can you train yourself to accept both with iman?
?
The Prophet listed belief in qada' and qadr as part of iman. Is there any area of your life where you act as if your own planning or effort is independent of God's decree? What would change if you internalized that every outcome is exactly as God predetermined?
?
The passage says iman cannot be realized without belief in qada' and qadr. Do you ever doubt that your sins or mistakes were within God's knowledge and creation? How would embracing this fully affect your repentance and trust in God's mercy?
