Two Scholarly Views on Seeing Allah in This World
Sanusi states two Ahl al-Sunnah positions on ru'ya in dunya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two major positions among Ahl al-Sunnah on whether Allah can be seen in this world, with special reference to the Prophet's Mi'raj.

Core Idea
Ahl al-Sunnah differ on ru'ya in dunya; the majority affirm it is possible for whom Allah wills.

Sanusi begins by framing the third aspect of the discussion: does the transmitted evidence allow seeing Allah in this world or only in the Hereafter? He then presents two positions: a minority who restrict ru'ya to the akhira, and a majority who affirm its possibility in dunya, citing the Prophet's Mi'raj as a key proof. This lesson focuses on these two views and their scriptural basis.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully to understand the two positions presented by Sanusi.
Notice the scriptural references used by each group.
2
Identify the two positions
Identify the minority view (ru'ya only in akhira) and the majority view (ru'ya possible in dunya). Note how each interprets verses like 'لا تدركه الأبصار' and the story of Moses.
3
Examine the majority's evidence
Focus on the majority's claim that the Prophet saw his Lord with his physical eyes during Mi'raj, and that this view is transmitted from many Companions including Ibn Abbas.
This sets up the later debate about the nature of that vision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما عن الوجه الثالث: ـ وهو هل في السمع ما يدلُّ على جواز رؤية الله تعالى في الدُّنيا، أو هو إن دل على جواز الرؤية خاص بالآخرة ؟ - فنقول : اختلف أهل السنة والجماعة في هذه المسألة ؛ فمنهم من قال: الذي ورد في السمع مما يدل على جواز الرؤية خاص بالآخرة ، وعلى هذا تُحمّل الآياتُ التى تنفى جواز الرؤية على رؤية الدُّنيا ، فقوله تعالى: ﴿لَّا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَرُ﴾ [الأنعام: ۱۰۳] إنْ سلَّمنا أنَّ الإدراك المنفي هو الرُّؤية فنقول: هذا في الدُّنيا، أما الآخرة فقد ثبت بدليل آخر أنه يُرى، وقوله سبحانه لموسى: قَالَ لَن تَرَينِي الأعراف: ١٤٣] أي: في الدُّنيا .. .. إلخ. وذهب الأكثرون إلى أنَّ في السَّمْع ما يدلُّ على جواز رؤية الله تعالى في الدُّنيا لمن أرادَ اللهُ له ذلك ، ومن ذلك قصة معراجه الله ، وهذا الفريق يقول : إنَّ النَّبِيَّ صلى الله عليه وسلم رأى رَبَّهُ بِعَيْنَيْ رأسه وهما في مكانهما الخَلْقِيِّ، ولم يحوّلهما الله تعالى إلى قلبه كما زعم بعض الناس، قالوا: وكان النبيصلى الله عليه وسلم يرى ربه النَّبِيُّ في كل مرة من مرات المراجعة التي كان يسأل فيها ربَّه تخفيف الصلوات المفروضة، وهذا الرّأيُ منقول عن جمهرة الصحابة، ومنهم ابن عباس رضي الله تعالى عنه، وكانت عائشة رضي الله تعالى عنها ومعاوية بن أبي سفيان الله رضي تعالى عنه يقولان: كانت رؤيا النَّبي ربَّه ليلة المعراج رؤيا منام، ولم تكن يقظة، ولعلهما قالا ذلك بناءً على اجتهاد منهما ، وقد يوجه بعض النَّاس قولهما بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا جَعَلْنَا الرُّيَا الَّتِي أَرَيْنَكَ إِلَّا فِتْنَةً لِلنَّاسِ ﴾ [الإسراء: ٦٠] ووجه ذلك أن (الرُّؤيا) بالألف المقصورة تطلق على رؤيا النوم؛ فإن أريد رؤية اليقظة قيل: رؤية بالتّاء ، والجواب على هذا الكلام من ثلاثة وجوه:
الأول: أنَّ عائشةَ رضي الله تعالى عنها لم تكن في بيت النبي ، بل لم تكن تميز إن سمعت في وقت الحادث ، بل الرّاجح أنها لم تكن وُلدت ورأت الدُّنيا ؛ لأن المعراج حدث في أول البعثة ، وعائشة يوم الهجرة لم تكن قد بلغت العاشرة على الأرجح ، ومعاوية بن أبي سفيان رضي الله تعالى عنه لم يكن أسلم يوم هذا الحادث ، بل إنه لم يُسْلِمْ إلَّا بعده بقريب من عشرين سنة .
والوجه الثاني : أنَّ الرُّؤيا بالألف المقصورة كما تطلق على رُؤيا النوم تُطلق على ما يكون في اليقظة أيضا، ومن ذلك قول الراعي:
فَكَبَّرَ لِلرُّؤْيَا وَهَ فُؤَادُهُ وَبَشِّرَ قَلْبًا كَانَ جَمًّا بَلَابِلُهْ يصفُ راعيًا وأنه رأى العُشْبَ والكَلاَ فاستبشر به وطَمْأَنَ نَفْسَهُ ، أو يَصِفُ صيَّادًا رأى صيدا ، ولا يُعقل أنَّ ذلك كان في النَّوم.
والوجه الثالث: أنَّه تعالى يقول عن هذه الرُّؤيا: إنَّها كانت فتنةً للناس، يعني ابتلاء لهم واختبارا ، ليرى مَن مِن النَّاس يَثبتُ على إيمانه ، ومَن منهم يرتد عن دينه لعدم تصديقه ذلك وقد كان ذلك فعلا؛ فإن النَّبيَّ لما أصبحَ وأخبرَ أهلَ مكَّةَ بما كان من سرَاهُ إلى بيت المقدس وعُروجه إلى السَّماوات وما فوقها، سَخروا منه وكذبوه وشنّعُوا عليه حتى ارتد ضعاف القلوب ، وثَبَتَ أهلُ التَّقوى وأهل المغفرة ، ومنهم أبو بكر الذي قال حين سمع ما يَتَنَدَّرُونَ على النبي به: «إن كان قد قال ذلك فقد صَدَقَ» وليس من المعقول أن يكون حديث الإنسان عن رؤيا رآها في المنام يكون فتنة وبلاء واختبارًا ، ويُصدِّق قوم به وينكره آخرون، فإنَّ كلَّ أحدٍ مهما يكن شأنه لو قال لأقل النَّاس عقلا : لقد رأيت الليلة فيما يرى النائم أَنَّني أطيرُ في السَّماء وأسير مع الملائكة وأكلَّم الله تعالى لم يستغربه السَّامع ؛ لأنَّ رؤيا النَّوم خيالات في حسبان أكثرِ النَّاسِ ، وأي إنسان يحجر على أي إنسان أن يتخيَّل في يقظته ما شاء، فضلا أن يكون هذا الخيال في النوم؟
As for the third aspect — whether there is in the transmitted evidence what indicates the permissibility of seeing Allah Almighty in this world, or if it indicates permissibility only for the Hereafter? — we say: Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah differed in this matter. Some said: what has come in the transmitted evidence that indicates the permissibility of seeing is specific to the Hereafter, and upon this the verses that negate the permissibility of seeing are interpreted as referring to seeing in this world. So His statement: 'Vision comprehends Him not' [al-An'am 103] — if we concede that the negated perception is vision, we say: this is in this world; as for the Hereafter, it is established by another proof that He is seen. And His statement to Moses: 'He said: You shall not see Me' [al-A'raf 143] means in this world, etc. And the majority went to the view that there is in the transmitted evidence what indicates the permissibility of seeing Allah Almighty in this world for whom Allah wills that, among which is the story of His ascension (Mi'raj). This group says: the Prophet (peace be upon him) saw his Lord with the eyes of his head while they were in their created place, and Allah did not transfer them to his heart as some people claimed. They said: The Prophet (peace be upon him) used to see his Lord each time during the multiple returns when he asked his Lord for reduction of the obligatory prayers. And this opinion is transmitted from a multitude of the Companions, among them Ibn Abbas (may Allah be pleased with him). And Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) and Muawiya ibn Abi Sufyan (may Allah be pleased with him) used to say: The Prophet's vision of his Lord on the night of Mi'raj was a dream vision, not a waking one, and perhaps they said that based on their ijtihad. And some people may justify their statement by His saying: 'And We made not the vision which We showed you except as a trial for mankind' [al-Isra 60], and the justification is that 'ru'ya' (with alif maqsura) is used for dream vision; if waking vision is intended, 'ru'ya' (with ta) is said. The answer to this statement is from three aspects:
First: That Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) was not in the Prophet's house; rather she could not have heard at the time of the event; indeed the more likely is that she had not been born and seen the world, because the Mi'raj occurred at the beginning of the mission, and Aisha at the time of the Hijra had not yet reached the age of ten on the most likely estimate, and Muawiya ibn Abi Sufyan (may Allah be pleased with him) had not accepted Islam at the time of this event; rather he did not accept Islam until nearly twenty years after it.
Second: That 'ru'ya' (with alif maqsura) is used for dream vision but is also used for what occurs while awake. Among that is the saying of the shepherd: 'fa-kabbara lil-ru'ya wa ha fu'aduhu wa bashshara qalban kana jamman balabiluh' describing a shepherd who saw greenery and grass and rejoiced and calmed himself, or describing a hunter who saw game, and it is inconceivable that that was in sleep.
Third: That Allah says concerning this vision: it was a fitna (trial) for people, meaning a test and an ordeal, to see who from among the people would remain firm in his faith and who would apostatize from his religion due to not believing it. And that indeed occurred, for when the Prophet became morning and informed the people of Mecca of what happened of his night journey to Jerusalem and his ascension to the heavens and what is above them, they mocked him, denied him, and reviled him until the weak-hearted apostatized, while the people of piety and forgiveness remained firm, among them Abu Bakr who said when he heard what they were taunting the Prophet with: 'If he said that, then he has spoken truth.' And it is not reasonable that a person's speech about a dream he saw in his sleep would be a fitna, trial, and test, with some people believing it and others denying it. For anyone, no matter his status, if he said to the least intelligent of people: 'Last night I saw in a dream that I am flying in the sky, walking with angels, and speaking with Allah,' the listener would not find it strange, because dreams are fantasies in the estimation of most people. And what person would restrict another from imagining in his wakefulness whatever he wishes, let alone such imagination in sleep?
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the third aspect concerning the transmitted evidence for seeing Allah. The author presents the disagreement among Ahl al-Sunnah: some hold that the evidence for ru'ya is specific to the Hereafter, interpreting verses like 'لا تدركه الأبصار' and the statement to Moses as referring to this world. The majority, however, affirm the possibility of seeing Allah in this world, as in the Prophet's Mi'raj. They assert that the Prophet saw his Lord with his physical eyes, and this view is attributed to many Companions, including Ibn Abbas.

Then the author addresses the objection based on Aisha and Muawiya's opinion that the vision was a dream, using the verse 'وَمَا جَعَلْنَا الرُّيَا...' as support. The objectors argue that the word 'ru'ya' (with alif maqsura) typically means dream vision. The author provides three counters:
1. Aisha and Muawiya lacked firsthand knowledge; Aisha was not yet born or very young, and Muawiya was not a Muslim at the time. Thus their opinion carries less weight.
2. The word 'ru'ya' (with alif maqsura) is used for both dream and waking vision, as shown by a poetic example where a shepherd sees something while awake.
3. The event being a fitna (trial) implies it was a real occurrence that people chose to believe or reject. Dream visions are not subject to such disbelief and are generally accepted as fantasies. The strong reaction from the Quraysh and the steadfastness of Abu Bakr indicate it was a waking event.

Key Terms
الرؤية
المعراج
السمع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that all of Ahl al-Sunnah deny the possibility of seeing Allah in this world.
Correction: While some scholars restrict ru'ya to the Hereafter, the majority—including many Companions—affirm its possibility in this world, as in the Prophet's Mi'raj.

Checkpoint
According to the majority view in this passage, what did the Prophet see during the Mi'raj?
A) A dream vision of his Lord
B) His Lord with the eyes of his head
C) A light only
D) Nothing; the Mi'raj did not involve seeing Allah

Key Takeaway
The majority of Ahl al-Sunnah affirm that the Prophet saw Allah with his physical eyes during the Mi'raj, based on transmitted evidence and the testimony of many Companions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the Prophet saw Allah with his physical eyes during the Mi'raj, why do you not feel a burning desire to witness that sight yourself? What worldly attachments hold you back from longing for that vision?
?
The majority of Companions believed the Prophet saw his Lord. Do you submit to their testimony, or does your heart waver? What would it take for you to be certain that seeing Allah in this life is possible?
?
You have just read about two scholarly views. Which view stirs more awe in your heart? And are you willing to live as if the majority view is true—expecting that Allah can manifest His beauty to His chosen ones even now?

The Objection: Was the Prophet's Vision a Dream?
Sanusi presents the dream interpretation objection with its scriptural proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection raised by Aisha and Muawiya that the Prophet's vision during Mi'raj was a dream, and their scriptural evidence from the Quran.

Core Idea
Aisha and Muawiya argued the vision was a dream, citing the word 'ru'ya' and the verse about fitna.

After presenting the majority view that the Prophet saw Allah physically, Sanusi introduces an opposing opinion from Aisha and Muawiya. They claimed the vision was a dream, not a waking experience, and supported their view with the Quranic verse 'وَمَا جَعَلْنَا الرُّيَا الَّتِي أَرَيْنَكَ إِلَّا فِتْنَةً لِلنَّاسِ'. This lesson examines their rationale and the linguistic argument they used.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic text and translation that presents the view of Aisha and Muawiya.
Notice that they base their opinion on ijtihad.
2
Identify their proof
Identify the Quranic verse they used and the linguistic distinction between 'ru'ya' (dream) and 'ru'ya' (waking vision).
3
Reflect on the strength of their position
Consider why Sanusi might consider this a valid objection, even though he will later refute it.
The objection is based on respected Companions' views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما عن الوجه الثالث: ـ وهو هل في السمع ما يدلُّ على جواز رؤية الله تعالى في الدُّنيا، أو هو إن دل على جواز الرؤية خاص بالآخرة ؟ - فنقول : اختلف أهل السنة والجماعة في هذه المسألة ؛ فمنهم من قال: الذي ورد في السمع مما يدل على جواز الرؤية خاص بالآخرة ، وعلى هذا تُحمّل الآياتُ التى تنفى جواز الرؤية على رؤية الدُّنيا ، فقوله تعالى: ﴿لَّا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَرُ﴾ [الأنعام: ۱۰۳] إنْ سلَّمنا أنَّ الإدراك المنفي هو الرُّؤية فنقول: هذا في الدُّنيا، أما الآخرة فقد ثبت بدليل آخر أنه يُرى، وقوله سبحانه لموسى: قَالَ لَن تَرَينِي الأعراف: ١٤٣] أي: في الدُّنيا .. .. إلخ. وذهب الأكثرون إلى أنَّ في السَّمْع ما يدلُّ على جواز رؤية الله تعالى في الدُّنيا لمن أرادَ اللهُ له ذلك ، ومن ذلك قصة معراجه الله ، وهذا الفريق يقول : إنَّ النَّبِيَّ صلى الله عليه وسلم رأى رَبَّهُ بِعَيْنَيْ رأسه وهما في مكانهما الخَلْقِيِّ، ولم يحوّلهما الله تعالى إلى قلبه كما زعم بعض الناس، قالوا: وكان النبيصلى الله عليه وسلم يرى ربه النَّبِيُّ في كل مرة من مرات المراجعة التي كان يسأل فيها ربَّه تخفيف الصلوات المفروضة، وهذا الرّأيُ منقول عن جمهرة الصحابة، ومنهم ابن عباس رضي الله تعالى عنه، وكانت عائشة رضي الله تعالى عنها ومعاوية بن أبي سفيان الله رضي تعالى عنه يقولان: كانت رؤيا النَّبي ربَّه ليلة المعراج رؤيا منام، ولم تكن يقظة، ولعلهما قالا ذلك بناءً على اجتهاد منهما ، وقد يوجه بعض النَّاس قولهما بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا جَعَلْنَا الرُّيَا الَّتِي أَرَيْنَكَ إِلَّا فِتْنَةً لِلنَّاسِ ﴾ [الإسراء: ٦٠] ووجه ذلك أن (الرُّؤيا) بالألف المقصورة تطلق على رؤيا النوم؛ فإن أريد رؤية اليقظة قيل: رؤية بالتّاء ، والجواب على هذا الكلام من ثلاثة وجوه:
الأول: أنَّ عائشةَ رضي الله تعالى عنها لم تكن في بيت النبي ، بل لم تكن تميز إن سمعت في وقت الحادث ، بل الرّاجح أنها لم تكن وُلدت ورأت الدُّنيا ؛ لأن المعراج حدث في أول البعثة ، وعائشة يوم الهجرة لم تكن قد بلغت العاشرة على الأرجح ، ومعاوية بن أبي سفيان رضي الله تعالى عنه لم يكن أسلم يوم هذا الحادث ، بل إنه لم يُسْلِمْ إلَّا بعده بقريب من عشرين سنة .
والوجه الثاني : أنَّ الرُّؤيا بالألف المقصورة كما تطلق على رُؤيا النوم تُطلق على ما يكون في اليقظة أيضا، ومن ذلك قول الراعي:
فَكَبَّرَ لِلرُّؤْيَا وَهَ فُؤَادُهُ وَبَشِّرَ قَلْبًا كَانَ جَمًّا بَلَابِلُهْ يصفُ راعيًا وأنه رأى العُشْبَ والكَلاَ فاستبشر به وطَمْأَنَ نَفْسَهُ ، أو يَصِفُ صيَّادًا رأى صيدا ، ولا يُعقل أنَّ ذلك كان في النَّوم.
والوجه الثالث: أنَّه تعالى يقول عن هذه الرُّؤيا: إنَّها كانت فتنةً للناس، يعني ابتلاء لهم واختبارا ، ليرى مَن مِن النَّاس يَثبتُ على إيمانه ، ومَن منهم يرتد عن دينه لعدم تصديقه ذلك وقد كان ذلك فعلا؛ فإن النَّبيَّ لما أصبحَ وأخبرَ أهلَ مكَّةَ بما كان من سرَاهُ إلى بيت المقدس وعُروجه إلى السَّماوات وما فوقها، سَخروا منه وكذبوه وشنّعُوا عليه حتى ارتد ضعاف القلوب ، وثَبَتَ أهلُ التَّقوى وأهل المغفرة ، ومنهم أبو بكر الذي قال حين سمع ما يَتَنَدَّرُونَ على النبي به: «إن كان قد قال ذلك فقد صَدَقَ» وليس من المعقول أن يكون حديث الإنسان عن رؤيا رآها في المنام يكون فتنة وبلاء واختبارًا ، ويُصدِّق قوم به وينكره آخرون، فإنَّ كلَّ أحدٍ مهما يكن شأنه لو قال لأقل النَّاس عقلا : لقد رأيت الليلة فيما يرى النائم أَنَّني أطيرُ في السَّماء وأسير مع الملائكة وأكلَّم الله تعالى لم يستغربه السَّامع ؛ لأنَّ رؤيا النَّوم خيالات في حسبان أكثرِ النَّاسِ ، وأي إنسان يحجر على أي إنسان أن يتخيَّل في يقظته ما شاء، فضلا أن يكون هذا الخيال في النوم؟
As for the third aspect — whether there is in the transmitted evidence what indicates the permissibility of seeing Allah Almighty in this world, or if it indicates permissibility only for the Hereafter? — we say: Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah differed in this matter. Some said: what has come in the transmitted evidence that indicates the permissibility of seeing is specific to the Hereafter, and upon this the verses that negate the permissibility of seeing are interpreted as referring to seeing in this world. So His statement: 'Vision comprehends Him not' [al-An'am 103] — if we concede that the negated perception is vision, we say: this is in this world; as for the Hereafter, it is established by another proof that He is seen. And His statement to Moses: 'He said: You shall not see Me' [al-A'raf 143] means in this world, etc. And the majority went to the view that there is in the transmitted evidence what indicates the permissibility of seeing Allah Almighty in this world for whom Allah wills that, among which is the story of His ascension (Mi'raj). This group says: the Prophet (peace be upon him) saw his Lord with the eyes of his head while they were in their created place, and Allah did not transfer them to his heart as some people claimed. They said: The Prophet (peace be upon him) used to see his Lord each time during the multiple returns when he asked his Lord for reduction of the obligatory prayers. And this opinion is transmitted from a multitude of the Companions, among them Ibn Abbas (may Allah be pleased with him). And Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) and Muawiya ibn Abi Sufyan (may Allah be pleased with him) used to say: The Prophet's vision of his Lord on the night of Mi'raj was a dream vision, not a waking one, and perhaps they said that based on their ijtihad. And some people may justify their statement by His saying: 'And We made not the vision which We showed you except as a trial for mankind' [al-Isra 60], and the justification is that 'ru'ya' (with alif maqsura) is used for dream vision; if waking vision is intended, 'ru'ya' (with ta) is said. The answer to this statement is from three aspects:
First: That Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) was not in the Prophet's house; rather she could not have heard at the time of the event; indeed the more likely is that she had not been born and seen the world, because the Mi'raj occurred at the beginning of the mission, and Aisha at the time of the Hijra had not yet reached the age of ten on the most likely estimate, and Muawiya ibn Abi Sufyan (may Allah be pleased with him) had not accepted Islam at the time of this event; rather he did not accept Islam until nearly twenty years after it.
Second: That 'ru'ya' (with alif maqsura) is used for dream vision but is also used for what occurs while awake. Among that is the saying of the shepherd: 'fa-kabbara lil-ru'ya wa ha fu'aduhu wa bashshara qalban kana jamman balabiluh' describing a shepherd who saw greenery and grass and rejoiced and calmed himself, or describing a hunter who saw game, and it is inconceivable that that was in sleep.
Third: That Allah says concerning this vision: it was a fitna (trial) for people, meaning a test and an ordeal, to see who from among the people would remain firm in his faith and who would apostatize from his religion due to not believing it. And that indeed occurred, for when the Prophet became morning and informed the people of Mecca of what happened of his night journey to Jerusalem and his ascension to the heavens and what is above them, they mocked him, denied him, and reviled him until the weak-hearted apostatized, while the people of piety and forgiveness remained firm, among them Abu Bakr who said when he heard what they were taunting the Prophet with: 'If he said that, then he has spoken truth.' And it is not reasonable that a person's speech about a dream he saw in his sleep would be a fitna, trial, and test, with some people believing it and others denying it. For anyone, no matter his status, if he said to the least intelligent of people: 'Last night I saw in a dream that I am flying in the sky, walking with angels, and speaking with Allah,' the listener would not find it strange, because dreams are fantasies in the estimation of most people. And what person would restrict another from imagining in his wakefulness whatever he wishes, let alone such imagination in sleep?
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the third aspect concerning the transmitted evidence for seeing Allah. The author presents the disagreement among Ahl al-Sunnah: some hold that the evidence for ru'ya is specific to the Hereafter, interpreting verses like 'لا تدركه الأبصار' and the statement to Moses as referring to this world. The majority, however, affirm the possibility of seeing Allah in this world, as in the Prophet's Mi'raj. They assert that the Prophet saw his Lord with his physical eyes, and this view is attributed to many Companions, including Ibn Abbas.

Then the author addresses the objection based on Aisha and Muawiya's opinion that the vision was a dream, using the verse 'وَمَا جَعَلْنَا الرُّيَا...' as support. The objectors argue that the word 'ru'ya' (with alif maqsura) typically means dream vision. The author provides three counters:
1. Aisha and Muawiya lacked firsthand knowledge; Aisha was not yet born or very young, and Muawiya was not a Muslim at the time. Thus their opinion carries less weight.
2. The word 'ru'ya' (with alif maqsura) is used for both dream and waking vision, as shown by a poetic example where a shepherd sees something while awake.
3. The event being a fitna (trial) implies it was a real occurrence that people chose to believe or reject. Dream visions are not subject to such disbelief and are generally accepted as fantasies. The strong reaction from the Quraysh and the steadfastness of Abu Bakr indicate it was a waking event.

Key Terms
الرؤيا
الفتنة
السمع

Checkpoint
What linguistic argument did Aisha and Muawiya use to support their view that the Prophet's vision was a dream?
A) The word 'ru'ya' in the Quran always means a waking vision
B) The word 'ru'ya' (with alif maqsura) is used for dreams, while 'ru'ya' (with ta) is for waking visions
C) The verse uses 'fitna' which implies doubt
D) They did not use a linguistic argument

Key Takeaway
Aisha and Muawiya held that the Prophet's vision was a dream, using the Quranic word 'ru'ya' as evidence for their ijtihad.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter an opinion from a respected Companion like Aisha that contradicts the majority, do you instinctively defend your own view or do you pause to examine their reasoning? What does your reaction reveal about your trust in the Companions' knowledge and your own humility?
?
The passage says they spoke 'based on their ijtihad'. Are you willing to admit that your own most cherished beliefs about Islam might be based on ijtihad rather than definitive texts? How would you feel if you discovered you were wrong?
?
The linguistic distinction between 'ru'ya' forms is subtle. Do you rely more on surface-level meanings of Quranic words or do you seek deeper context? What is one area where you might need to re-evaluate your understanding due to a linguistic nuance?

Three Refutations of the Dream Interpretation
Sanusi provides three counterarguments against the dream interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three arguments Sanusi uses to refute the claim that the Prophet's vision during Mi'raj was merely a dream.

Core Idea
Sanusi refutes the dream interpretation with historical, linguistic, and logical arguments.

Having presented the dream interpretation objection, Sanusi now offers a threefold refutation. He argues that Aisha and Muawiya lacked firsthand knowledge, that the word 'ru'ya' can also mean waking vision (supported by poetry), and that the event being a 'fitna' (trial) proves it was a real occurrence that caused disbelief—something a mere dream could not do. This lesson unpacks these three powerful counterarguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the three refutations
Read the Arabic text and translation of the three answers Sanusi provides.
Notice how each refutation targets a different aspect: historical context, linguistics, and logical consequence.
2
Analyze the historical refutation
Understand why Aisha and Muawiya's testimony is weakened by their absence at the time of the event.
This is a common method in Islamic scholarship: evaluating the witness's proximity.
3
Analyze the linguistic refutation
Examine the poetic evidence showing 'ru'ya' can refer to waking vision.
The poetry example is from a shepherd seeing greenery.
4
Analyze the logical refutation
Grasp the argument that a dream vision would not cause such a strong fitna (trial) leading to apostasy.
This is the strongest refutation: the severe reaction of Quraysh and the steadfastness of Abu Bakr indicate a real event.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما عن الوجه الثالث: ـ وهو هل في السمع ما يدلُّ على جواز رؤية الله تعالى في الدُّنيا، أو هو إن دل على جواز الرؤية خاص بالآخرة ؟ - فنقول : اختلف أهل السنة والجماعة في هذه المسألة ؛ فمنهم من قال: الذي ورد في السمع مما يدل على جواز الرؤية خاص بالآخرة ، وعلى هذا تُحمّل الآياتُ التى تنفى جواز الرؤية على رؤية الدُّنيا ، فقوله تعالى: ﴿لَّا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَرُ﴾ [الأنعام: ۱۰۳] إنْ سلَّمنا أنَّ الإدراك المنفي هو الرُّؤية فنقول: هذا في الدُّنيا، أما الآخرة فقد ثبت بدليل آخر أنه يُرى، وقوله سبحانه لموسى: قَالَ لَن تَرَينِي الأعراف: ١٤٣] أي: في الدُّنيا .. .. إلخ. وذهب الأكثرون إلى أنَّ في السَّمْع ما يدلُّ على جواز رؤية الله تعالى في الدُّنيا لمن أرادَ اللهُ له ذلك ، ومن ذلك قصة معراجه الله ، وهذا الفريق يقول : إنَّ النَّبِيَّ صلى الله عليه وسلم رأى رَبَّهُ بِعَيْنَيْ رأسه وهما في مكانهما الخَلْقِيِّ، ولم يحوّلهما الله تعالى إلى قلبه كما زعم بعض الناس، قالوا: وكان النبيصلى الله عليه وسلم يرى ربه النَّبِيُّ في كل مرة من مرات المراجعة التي كان يسأل فيها ربَّه تخفيف الصلوات المفروضة، وهذا الرّأيُ منقول عن جمهرة الصحابة، ومنهم ابن عباس رضي الله تعالى عنه، وكانت عائشة رضي الله تعالى عنها ومعاوية بن أبي سفيان الله رضي تعالى عنه يقولان: كانت رؤيا النَّبي ربَّه ليلة المعراج رؤيا منام، ولم تكن يقظة، ولعلهما قالا ذلك بناءً على اجتهاد منهما ، وقد يوجه بعض النَّاس قولهما بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا جَعَلْنَا الرُّيَا الَّتِي أَرَيْنَكَ إِلَّا فِتْنَةً لِلنَّاسِ ﴾ [الإسراء: ٦٠] ووجه ذلك أن (الرُّؤيا) بالألف المقصورة تطلق على رؤيا النوم؛ فإن أريد رؤية اليقظة قيل: رؤية بالتّاء ، والجواب على هذا الكلام من ثلاثة وجوه:
الأول: أنَّ عائشةَ رضي الله تعالى عنها لم تكن في بيت النبي ، بل لم تكن تميز إن سمعت في وقت الحادث ، بل الرّاجح أنها لم تكن وُلدت ورأت الدُّنيا ؛ لأن المعراج حدث في أول البعثة ، وعائشة يوم الهجرة لم تكن قد بلغت العاشرة على الأرجح ، ومعاوية بن أبي سفيان رضي الله تعالى عنه لم يكن أسلم يوم هذا الحادث ، بل إنه لم يُسْلِمْ إلَّا بعده بقريب من عشرين سنة .
والوجه الثاني : أنَّ الرُّؤيا بالألف المقصورة كما تطلق على رُؤيا النوم تُطلق على ما يكون في اليقظة أيضا، ومن ذلك قول الراعي:
فَكَبَّرَ لِلرُّؤْيَا وَهَ فُؤَادُهُ وَبَشِّرَ قَلْبًا كَانَ جَمًّا بَلَابِلُهْ يصفُ راعيًا وأنه رأى العُشْبَ والكَلاَ فاستبشر به وطَمْأَنَ نَفْسَهُ ، أو يَصِفُ صيَّادًا رأى صيدا ، ولا يُعقل أنَّ ذلك كان في النَّوم.
والوجه الثالث: أنَّه تعالى يقول عن هذه الرُّؤيا: إنَّها كانت فتنةً للناس، يعني ابتلاء لهم واختبارا ، ليرى مَن مِن النَّاس يَثبتُ على إيمانه ، ومَن منهم يرتد عن دينه لعدم تصديقه ذلك وقد كان ذلك فعلا؛ فإن النَّبيَّ لما أصبحَ وأخبرَ أهلَ مكَّةَ بما كان من سرَاهُ إلى بيت المقدس وعُروجه إلى السَّماوات وما فوقها، سَخروا منه وكذبوه وشنّعُوا عليه حتى ارتد ضعاف القلوب ، وثَبَتَ أهلُ التَّقوى وأهل المغفرة ، ومنهم أبو بكر الذي قال حين سمع ما يَتَنَدَّرُونَ على النبي به: «إن كان قد قال ذلك فقد صَدَقَ» وليس من المعقول أن يكون حديث الإنسان عن رؤيا رآها في المنام يكون فتنة وبلاء واختبارًا ، ويُصدِّق قوم به وينكره آخرون، فإنَّ كلَّ أحدٍ مهما يكن شأنه لو قال لأقل النَّاس عقلا : لقد رأيت الليلة فيما يرى النائم أَنَّني أطيرُ في السَّماء وأسير مع الملائكة وأكلَّم الله تعالى لم يستغربه السَّامع ؛ لأنَّ رؤيا النَّوم خيالات في حسبان أكثرِ النَّاسِ ، وأي إنسان يحجر على أي إنسان أن يتخيَّل في يقظته ما شاء، فضلا أن يكون هذا الخيال في النوم؟
As for the third aspect — whether there is in the transmitted evidence what indicates the permissibility of seeing Allah Almighty in this world, or if it indicates permissibility only for the Hereafter? — we say: Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah differed in this matter. Some said: what has come in the transmitted evidence that indicates the permissibility of seeing is specific to the Hereafter, and upon this the verses that negate the permissibility of seeing are interpreted as referring to seeing in this world. So His statement: 'Vision comprehends Him not' [al-An'am 103] — if we concede that the negated perception is vision, we say: this is in this world; as for the Hereafter, it is established by another proof that He is seen. And His statement to Moses: 'He said: You shall not see Me' [al-A'raf 143] means in this world, etc. And the majority went to the view that there is in the transmitted evidence what indicates the permissibility of seeing Allah Almighty in this world for whom Allah wills that, among which is the story of His ascension (Mi'raj). This group says: the Prophet (peace be upon him) saw his Lord with the eyes of his head while they were in their created place, and Allah did not transfer them to his heart as some people claimed. They said: The Prophet (peace be upon him) used to see his Lord each time during the multiple returns when he asked his Lord for reduction of the obligatory prayers. And this opinion is transmitted from a multitude of the Companions, among them Ibn Abbas (may Allah be pleased with him). And Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) and Muawiya ibn Abi Sufyan (may Allah be pleased with him) used to say: The Prophet's vision of his Lord on the night of Mi'raj was a dream vision, not a waking one, and perhaps they said that based on their ijtihad. And some people may justify their statement by His saying: 'And We made not the vision which We showed you except as a trial for mankind' [al-Isra 60], and the justification is that 'ru'ya' (with alif maqsura) is used for dream vision; if waking vision is intended, 'ru'ya' (with ta) is said. The answer to this statement is from three aspects:
First: That Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) was not in the Prophet's house; rather she could not have heard at the time of the event; indeed the more likely is that she had not been born and seen the world, because the Mi'raj occurred at the beginning of the mission, and Aisha at the time of the Hijra had not yet reached the age of ten on the most likely estimate, and Muawiya ibn Abi Sufyan (may Allah be pleased with him) had not accepted Islam at the time of this event; rather he did not accept Islam until nearly twenty years after it.
Second: That 'ru'ya' (with alif maqsura) is used for dream vision but is also used for what occurs while awake. Among that is the saying of the shepherd: 'fa-kabbara lil-ru'ya wa ha fu'aduhu wa bashshara qalban kana jamman balabiluh' describing a shepherd who saw greenery and grass and rejoiced and calmed himself, or describing a hunter who saw game, and it is inconceivable that that was in sleep.
Third: That Allah says concerning this vision: it was a fitna (trial) for people, meaning a test and an ordeal, to see who from among the people would remain firm in his faith and who would apostatize from his religion due to not believing it. And that indeed occurred, for when the Prophet became morning and informed the people of Mecca of what happened of his night journey to Jerusalem and his ascension to the heavens and what is above them, they mocked him, denied him, and reviled him until the weak-hearted apostatized, while the people of piety and forgiveness remained firm, among them Abu Bakr who said when he heard what they were taunting the Prophet with: 'If he said that, then he has spoken truth.' And it is not reasonable that a person's speech about a dream he saw in his sleep would be a fitna, trial, and test, with some people believing it and others denying it. For anyone, no matter his status, if he said to the least intelligent of people: 'Last night I saw in a dream that I am flying in the sky, walking with angels, and speaking with Allah,' the listener would not find it strange, because dreams are fantasies in the estimation of most people. And what person would restrict another from imagining in his wakefulness whatever he wishes, let alone such imagination in sleep?
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the third aspect concerning the transmitted evidence for seeing Allah. The author presents the disagreement among Ahl al-Sunnah: some hold that the evidence for ru'ya is specific to the Hereafter, interpreting verses like 'لا تدركه الأبصار' and the statement to Moses as referring to this world. The majority, however, affirm the possibility of seeing Allah in this world, as in the Prophet's Mi'raj. They assert that the Prophet saw his Lord with his physical eyes, and this view is attributed to many Companions, including Ibn Abbas.

Then the author addresses the objection based on Aisha and Muawiya's opinion that the vision was a dream, using the verse 'وَمَا جَعَلْنَا الرُّيَا...' as support. The objectors argue that the word 'ru'ya' (with alif maqsura) typically means dream vision. The author provides three counters:
1. Aisha and Muawiya lacked firsthand knowledge; Aisha was not yet born or very young, and Muawiya was not a Muslim at the time. Thus their opinion carries less weight.
2. The word 'ru'ya' (with alif maqsura) is used for both dream and waking vision, as shown by a poetic example where a shepherd sees something while awake.
3. The event being a fitna (trial) implies it was a real occurrence that people chose to believe or reject. Dream visions are not subject to such disbelief and are generally accepted as fantasies. The strong reaction from the Quraysh and the steadfastness of Abu Bakr indicate it was a waking event.

Key Terms
الرؤيا
الفتنة
السمع
الاجتهاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the word 'ru'ya' in the Quran always means a dream, and that Aisha and Muawiya's view is definitive.
Correction: Sanusi shows that 'ru'ya' can mean waking vision (with poetic proof), and that Aisha and Muawiya's testimony is weakened by their absence. The strongest refutation is that the event caused a fitna, which dreams do not.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the third refutation?
A) Aisha was not born yet
B) The word 'ru'ya' can mean waking vision
C) A dream would not cause a major trial (fitna)
D) Muawiya was not a Muslim

Key Takeaway
Sanusi refutes the dream interpretation by showing the objectors' lack of firsthand knowledge, the linguistic flexibility of 'ru'ya', and the logical necessity that the vision was a real event because it caused a major trial.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear about the Mi'raj, do you truly believe it as a real event, or does your heart harbor any doubt? What would you have done if you were in Mecca that morning?
?
Abu Bakr said 'If he said that, then he has spoken truth' without hesitation. Is your faith in the Prophet's words that immediate and unwavering, or do you need proof?
?
The Quraysh mocked and some apostatized. In your life, are there aspects of Islamic belief that you are embarrassed to affirm in front of others, fearing their reaction?

The Reality of the Prophet's Vision in the World
Sanusi establishes the occurrence of the Prophet's vision in the world and its nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Prophet's vision of Allah in the worldly life was a unique occurrence, its reality as a clear vision like seeing the moon, and the refutation of the anthropomorphist misunderstanding.

Core Idea
The Prophet saw Allah with certainty, unlike any other in this world.

This lesson begins the discussion of the Prophet's vision of Allah during the Night Journey. Sanusi clarifies that this vision was exclusive to the Prophet and not for anyone else, including Moses. He then explains the meaning of 'world' and describes the nature of the vision as crystal clear, drawing a comparison to seeing the full moon. Finally, he corrects the mistaken interpretation of some anthropomorphists.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the vision's exclusivity
Focus on the statement that the vision occurred only for the Chosen Prophet, not for others. Notice the reason given.
This establishes the special status of the Prophet.
2
Understand the meaning of 'world'
The author defines 'dunya' as the earthly realm before the Hereafter, indicating the vision happened in this temporal world.
This avoids confusion about place versus time.
3
Analyze the description of the vision
The vision is likened to seeing the moon without doubt. Reflect on why this comparison is used and how it refutes the anthropomorphist error.
The kaaf is for clarity, not for likening the seen.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا [رؤية النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم لربّه سبحانه في الدنيا ليلة الإسراء والمعراج] (هَذَا) كما علمتَ (وَ) رؤيته سبحانه (لِلْمُخْتَارِ (۲)) وهو نبينا محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم لأنه خيرُ البَرايَا فلم تقع لغيره، ولا لموسى في الـ (دنيا) مِن الدُّنْو ؛ السبقها للآخرة (۳) ، أو لدنوّها مِن الزَّوال ، وحقيقتها : ما على الأرض من الهواء والجوّ ممَّا قبل الآخرة (٤) ، ومراده : الإشارة إلى وجه أخص من جواز الوقوع، = الرائي، ومعناه: أنها رؤية يزاح عنها الشك مثل رؤيتكم القمر ليلة البدر، فهو تشبيه في عدم الخفاء، وقد توهم بعض الناس من المجسمة أن الكاف لتشبيه المرئي، وهو غلط فاحش. وبيانه أن رؤية النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم لربه في الدنيا ليلة الإسراء والمعراج معنی (ثَبَتَتْ) أي: حصلتْ ووقعت لنبينا في الدُّنيا ليلة الإسراء، والوقوع يستلزم الإمكان، بخلاف العكس، والراجح عند أكثر العلماء أنه رأى ربه بعيني رأسه (۱) ؛ لحديث ابن عباس وغيره ، وهذا لا يؤخذ إلا بالسَّماع منه ؛ فلا ينبغي أن يتشكك فيه، ولما نفت عائشة وقوعها له لا قدم ابن عباس عليها ؛ لأنَّه مُثبِتُ ، حَتَّى قال مَعْمَر بن راشد(۲): ما عائشةُ عندنا بأعلم من ابن عباس، وأما حديث «واعلموا أَنَّكُم لَنْ تَروا رَبَّكُم حتّى تموتوا (۳) فإنَّه وإن أفاد أنَّ الرُّؤية في الدُّنيا وإنْ جازت عقلا فقد امتنعث سمعاً ، لكن من أثبتها للنَّبيِّ صلى الله عليه وسلم له أن يقول: إِنَّ = ها المتكلّم لا يدخل في عموم كلامه . بد من وهذا بيان لزمانها ، والأول مكانها ، والآخرة من النفخة على ما يأتي. وعلى هذا لا تقدير مضاف عند قوله: (للمختار دنيا ثبتت أي: للمختار زمن الدنيا ثبتت ، فالمراد: أنه رآه زمن وجود الدنيا لا في مكانها الأمير والشنواني .
No [the vision of the Prophet (peace be upon him) of his Lord (Glory be to Him) in the world on the night of the Isra and Mi'raj]. (This) as you have learned, and (and) His vision (Glory be to Him) (for the Chosen One (2)) – meaning our Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) because he is the best of creation – did not occur for anyone else, nor for Moses in the (world) from the nearness; its precedence is for the Hereafter (3), or because of its nearness to passing away. And its reality: that which is on the earth of air and atmosphere from what is before the Hereafter (4). And his intent: indication to a more specific aspect than the possibility of occurrence... = the seer, meaning: it is a vision from which doubt is removed, like your seeing the moon on the night of the full moon; so it is a likeness in the absence of hiddenness. And some of the Mujassimah mistakenly thought that the kaaf is for likening the seen, which is a grave error. And its explanation: that the vision of the Prophet (peace be upon him) of his Lord in the world on the night of the Isra and Mi'raj meaning (it is established) i.e., it occurred and happened for our Prophet in the world on the night of the Isra, and occurrence necessitates possibility, contrary to the converse. And the predominant view according to most scholars is that he saw his Lord with the eyes of his head (1); due to the hadith of Ibn Abbas and others, and this is not taken except by transmission from him; so no doubt should be entertained about it. And when Aisha denied its occurrence for him, Ibn Abbas gave precedence over her because he is an affirmant (muthbit). So much so that Ma'mar ibn Rashid (2) said: 'Aisha, in our view, is not more knowledgeable than Ibn Abbas.' As for the hadith 'Know that you will not see your Lord until you die' (3), it indicates that the vision in the world, even if intellectually possible, is prohibited by transmitted evidence. However, one who affirms it for the Prophet (peace be upon him) may say that 'the speaker does not enter into the generality of his speech.' There is no alternative? And this is an explanation of its time, and the first is its place, and the Hereafter is from the blowing (of the Trumpet) as will come. Based on this, there is no implied word according to his statement: 'for the Chosen One, in the world it is established' meaning 'for the Chosen One in the time of the world it is established,' so the meaning is that he saw Him in the time of the existence of the world, not in its place. (Al-Amir and Al-Shanwani).
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the specific case of the Prophet's vision of Allah in the worldly life on the Night of Isra and Mi'raj. It begins by stating that this vision occurred for the Prophet alone, not for anyone else, and explains the meaning of 'dunya' (world) as the earthly realm before the Hereafter. The author then clarifies that the vision is like seeing the moon without doubt, and refutes the anthropomorphist misinterpretation. The evidence for its occurrence is given: the hadith of Ibn Abbas, and that it is accepted by transmission. The author then addresses the objection of Aisha, giving precedence to Ibn Abbas as an affirmant, supported by Ma'mar ibn Rashid. He also responds to the hadith 'you will not see your Lord until you die' by noting that the speaker (the Prophet) may be excluded from its generality. Finally, he explains that the vision occurred in the time of the world, not in its place.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The kaaf in 'like your seeing the moon' means Allah is similar to the moon.
Correction: The comparison is in the clarity of vision, not in the nature of the seen object. The author refutes this as a grave error.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's vision of Allah in this world was a unique, clear vision, and the comparison to the moon is for certainty, not for similarity.

?

Evidence for the Prophet's Vision and Refutation of Denials
Sanusi presents the evidence, addresses counterarguments, and clarifies the ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the evidence for the occurrence of the Prophet's vision, the response to Aisha's denial, and the interpretation of the hadith 'you will not see your Lord until you die.'

Core Idea
The Prophet saw Allah in this world, and the evidence from transmission outweighs denial.

This lesson continues from the previous one, now focusing on the proof that the vision actually occurred. The author cites the hadith of Ibn Abbas and other reports, establishing that the predominant view is that the Prophet saw Allah with his physical eyes. He then addresses the objection from Aisha, giving precedence to Ibn Abbas as an affirmant. Finally, he explains how to understand the hadith that seems to deny vision in this world, noting that the Prophet himself may be excluded from its generality.

Guided Path
1
Examine the evidence from Ibn Abbas
Read the passage that states the predominant view and the reliance on transmission. Notice that this is a sam'iyyat matter.
The author emphasizes that this knowledge comes only from transmission, not rational proof.
2
Understand the response to Aisha's denial
The author explains why Ibn Abbas is given precedence: because he affirms it. Consider the principle that affirmation outweighs denial in such matters.
Ma'mar ibn Rashid supports this view.
3
Interpret the hadith about not seeing until death
The author acknowledges the hadith but explains that the Prophet may be an exception, as the speaker does not include himself in the generality.
This resolves the apparent contradiction.

Dreams of the Prophet and the Authority of Tawātur
A fā'ida about a dream of the Prophet and the ruling on khums.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a dream of the Prophet cannot override a ruling established by mass transmission (tawātur), and how Islamic legal theory distinguishes between certain and probable evidence.

Core Idea
A dream of the Prophet is not an independent source of law when a ruling is already proven by tawātur.

This lesson presents a fā'ida (benefit) from the commentary on Jawharat al-Tawḥīd, illustrating a key legal principle: that certain knowledge from tawātur cannot be overturned by a singular report (āḥād), even if it comes through a dream of the Prophet. The example of a man who dreamed of a treasure without khums demonstrates this rule.

Guided Path
1
Read the entire passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then read the translation. Notice that it begins with a general observation about visions before presenting a specific story.
The passage is a single continuous fā'ida from the commentary.
2
Identify the core story and ruling
Find the story of the man who dreamed of the Prophet telling him about a treasure. Note what al-Izz ibn Abd al-Salam ruled and the reasoning he gave.
The key phrase is 'أخرج الخُمْسَ فقد ثبت بالتواتر'.
3
Understand the legal principle
The ruling is based on the hierarchy of evidence: tawātur (mass transmission) gives certainty, while a dream (even of the Prophet) is only āḥād (single report) and cannot override a certain ruling.
This principle applies beyond this story, as the analogy with Eid/Ramadan shows.
4
Reflect on the analogy
The passage extends the principle: if a person dreams that tomorrow is Eid, he should rely on the established lunar sighting methods, not the dream. Ask yourself why this follows the same logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رؤية النبي لربه في الدنيا ليلة الإسراء والمعراج . الأحوال تجعل الغائب كالشاهد ، حتى إذا كثر اشتغال الشخص بشيء واستحضاره له صار كأنه حاضر بين يديه كما هو معلوم بالوجدان لكل أحد الشنواني . فائدة: حكي أن رجلا رأى النبي الله في المنام يقول له : في المكان الفلاني ركاز ذهب، خذه ولا خُمس عليك ، فذهب وأخذهنَّ ثم استفتى العلماء؛ فقال العز بن عبد السلام: أخرج الخُمْسَ فقد ثبت بالتواتر ، وقصارى رؤيتك الآحاد ، ومنه أن يقول له: غدا العيد أو رمضان ، فيعوّل على العلامات المُقررة . الشنواني .
The vision of the Prophet of his Lord in this world on the night of Isra and Mi'raj. The states make the absent like the present, so when a person's engagement with something and his bringing it to mind becomes frequent, it becomes as if it is before his eyes, as is known by experience for everyone – al-Shanawani. A benefit: It was narrated that a man saw the Prophet (peace be upon him) in a dream saying to him: In such-and-such a place there is a treasure of gold; take it and there is no khums (one-fifth tax) due on you. So he went and took it, then sought a legal opinion from the scholars. Al-Izz ibn Abd al-Salam said: Pay the khums, for it has been established by tawatur (mass transmission), and at most your seeing is singly-reported (ahad). Similarly, if he says to him: Tomorrow is Eid or Ramadan, one relies on the established signs. al-Shanawani.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage is a fā'ida (benefit) that appears to be a side note in the commentary. It first mentions the vision of the Prophet seeing his Lord during the Isra and Mi'raj, then explains that frequent mental engagement with something makes it seem present, a known experiential fact (wijdān). The core of the benefit is a story: a man dreams that the Prophet tells him to take a buried treasure (rikāz) without paying khums. The man takes the treasure and then consults scholars. Al-Izz ibn Abd al-Salam rules that he must pay khums. The reasoning: the obligation of khums is established by mass transmission (tawātur), which is certain, whereas a dream is only an individual report (āḥād) and cannot override a certain obligation. The passage then extends the analogy: if a person dreams that tomorrow is Eid or Ramadan, he should still rely on the established lunar sighting methods, not the dream. This highlights the principle in Islamic legal theory that mutawātir evidence takes precedence over āḥād, even if the āḥād comes from a dream of the Prophet (since dreams are not infallible sources of law). The passage ends with 'al-Shanawani', likely indicating the author of the commentary from which this benefit is taken.

Key Terms
ru'ya
tawātur
āḥād
khums
rikāz

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think a dream of the Prophet can abrogate an established obligation or create a new ruling.
Correction: The passage shows that a dream (āḥād) cannot override a ruling established by tawātur. Dreams are not infallible sources of law when certainty is already present.

Checkpoint
Why did al-Izz ibn Abd al-Salam rule that the man must pay khums despite the dream saying otherwise?
A) Because the dream was unreliable
B) Because the obligation of khums is established by tawātur, which is stronger than an āḥād dream
C) Because the treasure was not actually gold
D) Because the man forgot the dream details

Key Takeaway
A mutawātir proof (like the obligation of khums) cannot be opposed by an āḥād report (like a dream).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever relied on a personal spiritual experience—like a dream or feeling—to justify neglecting a clear obligation of Islam? What would the story of the man and al-Izz ibn Abd al-Salam say to you about your priorities? Take five minutes to identify one such situation and commit to correcting it.
?
The passage says frequent engagement makes the absent present. Is there a pillar of Islam or a teaching of the Prophet that you have made 'absent' from your daily awareness? What would it take to make it so present that it becomes as if before your eyes? Write one concrete habit you will start this week.
?
If a dream came to you commanding something that contradicts a mass-transmitted teaching, would you have the courage to go to a true scholar for guidance, or would you follow the dream? Examine your heart: is your attachment to your own experiences stronger than your submission to the Prophet's established Sunnah? What change must you make?

Sending Messengers: Intellectually Possible, Not Obligatory nor Impossible
Defines the Ahl al-Sunna position on sending messengers as intellectually possible and refutes two opposing groups.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ahl al-Sunna classify sending messengers as intellectually possible (ja'iz) rather than obligatory or impossible, and how this refutes the positions of the Mu'tazila/philosophers and the Sumaniyya/Barahima.

Core Idea
Sending messengers is an act of divine grace, not a duty or an impossibility.

In this passage, the author establishes the Ahl al-Sunna position on prophethood by clarifying the rational status of sending messengers. He defines 'intellectually possible' and then refutes two opposing groups who err by either making it obligatory or impossible. This lesson unpacks that argument and shows why the middle path is the correct one.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ahl al-Sunna claim
Read the passage carefully to find the main claim: that sending messengers is intellectually possible for God, meaning it is neither obligatory nor impossible. Note the definition of 'ja'iz 'aqli'.
The author states this right after explaining the scope of the poem.
2
Understand the definition of 'ja'iz'
The passage explains that 'intellectually possible' means the intellect can conceive of both the act and its omission. If the intellect permits the act, its omission is not obligatory, and vice versa. Grasping this is key to the whole argument.
3
Contrast the two opposing views
Identify the two groups refuted: the Mu'tazila/philosophers (who say it's obligatory) and the Sumaniyya/Barahima (who say it's impossible). Note the author's refutations: against the Mu'tazila he mentions the principle of 'beneficial and most beneficial' which was previously refuted; against the philosophers he mentions that God acts by choice; against the Sumaniyya/Barahima he begins a refutation but the text cuts off.
The passage ends mid-sentence, but the contrast is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان جواز إرسال الرسل عقلا ووجوبه شرعا
وَمِنْهُ إِرْسَالُ جَمِيعِ الرُّسْلِ فَلَا وُجُوبَ بَلْ بِمَحْضِ الفَضْلِ
ولما فرغ من الإلهيَّات شرَع في النُّبوَّات (١) فقال: (وَمِنْهُ) أي: ومن أفراد (1) يريد المصنف أنْ يُقرِّرَ أنَّ مذهب أهل السُّنَّة والجماعة: أنَّ مِن أنواع الجائز العقلي على الله تعالى إرساله لجميع الرُّسل مِن لَدُن آدمَ أبي البشر إلى خاتمهم وسيدهم محمد صلى الله عليه وعليهم أجمعين ، ومعنى ذلك أنَّ الإرسال مما هو ذلك أنَّ الإرسال مما هو جائز عقلا على الله تعالى عند أهل السُّنَّة أي: أنه لا يجب على الله تعالى إرسالهم ، ولا يستحيل عليه إرسالهم ؛ إذ الجائز العقلي هو ما يُجَوِّزُ العقل فعله وتركه ، ومتى جوّز فعله لم يكن تركه واجباً، ومتى جوز تركه لم يكن فعله واجبا، وفي هذا الكلام ردُّ على طائفتين من أهل البحث: الطائفة الأولى : المعتزلة والفلاسفة؛ فقد اتَّفق هذان الفريقان على أنَّه يجب على الله تعالى أن يبعث الرُّسُلَ إلى الخلق ليدلُّوهم على ما يريده منهم ، ومبنى كلام المعتزلة في هذه المسألة ما أَصَّلُوه عند أنفسهم وجعلوه قاعدةً بَنَوْا عليها كثيرًا من الأحكام، وهو أنَّه يجب على الله تعالى فعلُ الصَّلاح والأصلح لعباده ، وقد مضى الكلام على هذا الأصل وردّ ما ذهبوا إليه، قالوا: النّظام المؤدِّي إلى صلاح حال النوع الإنساني على وجه العموم في معاشه ومعاده لا يتم إلا ببعثة الرُّسل ، وكلُّ ما هو كذلك فهو واجب على الله تعالى ، وأنت خبير بعد ما تقدم في مبحث الصلاح والأصلح بطريق هدم هذه المقدمات التي لا ثبات لها عند النظر، ومبنى كلام الفلاسفة في هذه المسألة ما ذهَبوا إليه وجعلوه من قواعدهم التي بنوا عليها كثيرًا من الأحكام ، وهو: القول بالتعليل أو الطبيعة، قالوا: يلزم من وجود الله تعالى وجود العالم بالتعليل : أي: بكون الله تعالى علَّةً ، أو بالطَّبع ، ويلزم من وجود العالم وجود مَن يُصْلِحُهُ : وقد بيّنا لك فيما سبق أنَّ الله تعالى فاعل باختيار ، لا بطريق الإجبار . والطائفة الثانية : السُّمَّنِيَّة والبَرَاهِمَةُ ، فقد اتَّفق هذان الفريقان على أنه يستحيل على الله تعالى عقلا أنْ يُرسل الرُّسل ، قالوا : إِنَّ إرسال الرُّسُلِ عَبَتْ ؛ لأنَّه يُستغنى عنه بالعقل ، بأن يُجعل =
Statement of the permissibility of sending messengers intellectually and its obligation legally. And from it (i.e., from the intellectually possible acts) is the sending of all messengers; so there is no obligation, rather it is by pure grace. And when he finished from the divine matters, he began on prophecies (1) and said: 'And from it' meaning from the individuals (1) the author intends to establish that the doctrine of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a is that among the types of the intellectually possible (al-ja'iz al-'aqli) for God Almighty is His sending of all messengers from Adam, the father of humanity, to their seal and master Muhammad, peace be upon him and upon them all. And the meaning of that is that sending is something that is intellectually possible for God Almighty according to Ahl al-Sunna, i.e., it is not obligatory for God Almighty to send them, nor is it impossible for Him to send them; for the intellectually possible is that which the intellect permits doing and leaving. And whenever it permits doing it, its leaving is not obligatory, and whenever it permits leaving it, its doing is not obligatory. And in this statement is a refutation of two groups of researchers: The first group: the Mu'tazila and the philosophers; indeed these two parties agreed that it is obligatory for God Almighty to send messengers to creation to guide them to what He wants from them. The basis of the Mu'tazila's speech on this issue is what they established among themselves and made a principle upon which they built many rulings, namely that it is obligatory for God Almighty to do what is beneficial and most beneficial for His servants. And the discussion on this principle and the refutation of what they went to has passed. They said: The system leading to the welfare of the human species in general in its livelihood and afterlife is not completed except by the sending of messengers. And everything that is like that is obligatory upon God Almighty. And you are aware after what preceded in the investigation of the beneficial and most beneficial of the way of demolishing these premises which have no stability upon consideration. And the basis of the philosophers' speech on this issue is what they went to and made among their principles upon which they built many rulings, namely the assertion of causation (al-ta'lil) or nature (al-tabi'a). They said: It necessarily follows from the existence of God Almighty the existence of the world by causation: i.e., by God Almighty being a cause, or by nature; and it necessarily follows from the existence of the world the existence of one who will reform it. And we have shown to you previously that God Almighty is an agent by choice, not by way of compulsion. And the second group: al-Sumaniyya and al-Barahima; indeed these two parties agreed that it is intellectually impossible for God Almighty to send messengers. They said: indeed sending messengers is nonsense; because it is dispensed with by the intellect, by making =
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage defines the Ahl al-Sunna position on the sending of messengers and refutes two opposing groups. The author begins by stating that among the intellectually possible acts for God is sending all messengers—this is the doctrine of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a. He explains that 'intellectually possible' means the mind can conceive of both the act and its omission; thus, sending is neither obligatory nor impossible for God. This is a refutation of the first group: the Mu'tazila and philosophers. The Mu'tazila argue that God must do what is most beneficial for His servants, and sending messengers is necessary for human well-being in this life and the next, making it obligatory upon God. The author dismisses this by referring to a previous refutation of the 'beneficial and most beneficial' principle. The philosophers base their argument on causation or nature, claiming that existence of God necessarily entails existence of the world and thus a need for a reformer—messengers. The author counters that God acts by choice, not by compulsion. The second group: al-Sumaniyya and al-Barahima hold that sending messengers is intellectually impossible because reason alone suffices, making messengers superfluous. The passage cuts off mid-argument, but the author clearly rejects both extremes, affirming the middle position of permissibility.

جائز عقلي

استحالة

تعليل
طبيعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that sending messengers is either obligatory upon God (Mu'tazila and philosophers) or impossible (Sumaniyya and Barahima).
Correction: Ahl al-Sunna holds that sending messengers is intellectually possible — a free act of God's grace — neither required nor prohibited by reason.

Checkpoint

B) Intellectually impossible
C) Intellectually possible (ja'iz)
D) Legally obligatory but intellectually impossible

Key Takeaway
The sending of messengers is intellectually possible for God, neither obligatory nor impossible, reflecting His free will and grace.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that God owes you something, like answers to your prayers or a certain outcome? How does the truth that sending messengers is pure grace (not an obligation) challenge that expectation in your life? Will you let this reshape how you approach God's commands today?
?
The Barahima claimed that human intellect is enough, making prophets unnecessary. In what areas of your life do you silently rely on your own reasoning over the guidance of the prophets? Are you willing to submit to revelation even when it contradicts your logic?
?
God's freedom to send or not send messengers means He gave you this gift by choice, not duty. Does your heart respond with gratitude and humility? Or do you take prophethood for granted? What specific change in your daily routine will reflect thankfulness for this grace?

Refuting the Mu'tazila's Claim of Obligation
Al-Malali presents and refutes the Mu'tazila argument for obligation of messengers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's argument from al-salāh wa al-aslah and why it is insufficient to prove the obligation of sending messengers.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila's principle of al-salāh wa al-aslah is rationally unsound as a basis for obligation upon Allah.

The Mu'tazila argue that Allah must send messengers because the well-being of humanity depends on it, and they consider that obligatory under their principle of al-salāh wa al-aslah. This lesson examines their reasoning and the refutation that exposes its fragility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how the Mu'tazila's argument is presented.
The passage is part of a larger refutation.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila's principle
Find the principle of al-salāh wa al-aslah. Understand that they claim Allah is obliged to do what is beneficial for His servants.
3
Understand the refutation
The author references a previous refutation of this principle, stating that its premises are unstable. Ask yourself why this principle might be problematic.
This connects to broader discussions of divine obligation.

The first group: the Mu'tazila and the philosophers; these two groups agreed that it is obligatory upon Allah to send messengers to creation to guide them to what He wants from them. The basis of the Mu'tazila's speech in this matter is what they originally established for themselves and made a principle upon which they built many rulings, which is that it is obligatory upon Allah to do what is beneficial and most beneficial (al-salāh wa al-aslah) for His servants. Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the first group who claim that sending messengers is obligatory upon Allah. It first names them: Mu'tazila and philosophers. It then explains the basis of the Mu'tazila: their principle of al-salāh wa al-aslah (what is beneficial and most beneficial), which they hold is obligatory upon Allah. Refuting the Philosophers' Claim of Obligation through Causation
Al-Malali presents and refutes the philosophers' argument from causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that the existence of the world necessitates a messenger, and why it is invalid because Allah acts by choice.

Core Idea
The philosophers' deterministic causation contradicts Allah's free will and choice.

The philosophers argue that from Allah's existence necessarily follows the world, and the world requires a rectifier—the messenger. This lesson examines their argument and the decisive refutation: Allah is an agent by choice, not by compulsion.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the philosophers base their claim on causation or nature.
2
Understand causation and nature
The philosophers say that Allah's existence necessitates the world's existence through causation or nature. This is a deterministic worldview.
3
Grasp the refutation
The author refutes by stating a prior principle: Allah acts by choice, not compulsion. This undermines the entire necessity chain.
This is a key difference between Islamic theology and philosophical determinism.

Refuting the Barahima's Argument Against Prophethood
Al-Malali refutes the Barahima and Summaniyya claim of impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument of the Barahima and Summaniyya against the possibility of sending messengers, and how al-Malali's matn implicitly refutes it through the category of the intellectually permissible.

Core Idea
Sending messengers is intellectually permissible, not impossible, and its actual occurrence disproves claims of impossibility.

This passage takes on a direct challenge to prophethood: the claim that it is rationally impossible for Allah to send messengers. The Barahima and Summaniyya argue that reason alone suffices, making messengers futile. Al-Malali's matn subtly refutes them by classifying sending as 'jā'iz 'aqlī' (intellectually permissible), which negates both the Mu'tazilite claim of obligation and the Barahima claim of impossibility. The commentator explains why the actual sending of messengers makes the impossibility claim clearly false.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Pay attention to the flow from the Barahima argument to the commentator's explanation.
The passage includes both the opponents' argument and the commentary on the matn.
2
Identify the Barahima/Summaniyya argument
Find their core claim: that sending messengers is futile (عبث) because the intellect can guide people without messengers. Note their reliance on the intellect's ability to judge good and evil (تحسين وتقبيح).
Futility on Allah is impossible, so they conclude sending messengers is impossible.
3
Understand the author's implicit refutation
The commentator says the author's phrase 'فلا وجوب' and classification as 'جائز عقلي' suffices to negate both obligation (Mu'tazila) and impossibility (Barahima). Realize that 'jā'iz' means it is not necessary and not impossible.
4
Note the role of observation
The commentator points out that the actual sending of messengers is an observed fact, so claiming impossibility is 'مكابرة للحس' (obstinacy against the senses). This is a direct refutation.
5
Reflect on the final line
The last line of the source says 'لكن بذا إيماننا قد وجبا' — faith in the messengers is obligatory even though the act is permissible. Consider why this clarification is necessary.
This prevents the misunderstanding that permissibility means optional belief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والطائفة الثانية : السُّمَّنِيَّة والبَرَاهِمَةُ ، فقد اتَّفق هذان الفريقان على أنه يستحيل على الله تعالى عقلا أنْ يُرسل الرُّسل ، قالوا : إِنَّ إرسال الرُّسُلِ عَبَتْ ; لأنَّه يُستغنى عنه بالعقل ، بأن يُجعل =
قسم النبوات
مناط فعل الشيء تحسينُ العقل إيَّاه ، ومناط تركه تقبيح العقل إيَّاه، والعبث على الله تعالى محالٌ ; فيكون ما أدى إليه وهو تجويز إرسال الرسل محالاً ، إذا علمت هذا الكلام علمت أن قول المصنف: «فلا وجوب» تصريح بنفي مذهب المعتزلة والفلاسفة، ولم يصرح بنفي مذهب البراهمة والسُّمنية إما من باب الاكتفاء، وكأنه قد قال: فلا وجوب ولا استحالة ، لأنَّ هذا يُنْبِئُ عنه قوله: (وَمِنْهُ أي من الجائز العقلي ، وإما لكون مذهبهم ظاهر البطلان ; لأنَّ الرُّسل قد أُرسلوا فعلا، فإرسالهم واقع ثابت بالمشاهَدَةِ والعيان؛ فادعاء استحالته مكابرة للحس ومعاندةٌ للمُشاهَدِ ، فأَمَّا مذهب الفلاسفة والمعتزلة فلا يدلّ على بطلانه بَعْتُهم عليهم الصلاة والسلام بالفعل ; لجواز أن يقولوا : إنّ الله تعالى قد فعل ذلك لكونه واجبًا عليه ، فلما اخْتَلَفَ شأنُ المَذْهَبَيْنِ ذلكَ الاختلاف صرَّح بنفي أقواهما بحسب الظاهر، وإن كان كل منهما ضعيفًا في الحقيقة ، وقوله فيما بعد : لكنْ بِذَا إيماننا قَدْ وَجَبَا دفع لما قد يُتوهم مِن كون إرسال الرُّسل جائزا عقليًا أنّ الإيمان بوقوعه ليس واجباً
And the second group: the Summaniyya and the Barahima; these two groups agreed that it is intellectually impossible for Allah to send messengers. They said: The sending of messengers is futility (ʿabath), because it is dispensed with by the intellect, by making the criterion for doing a thing the intellect's approval of it, and the criterion for leaving it the intellect's disapproval of it. And futility upon Allah is impossible; so what leads to it, which is the permissibility of sending messengers, is impossible. When you have understood this speech, you know that the author's saying 'So there is no obligation' is an explicit negation of the doctrine of the Mu'tazila and philosophers, and he did not explicitly negate the doctrine of the Barahima and Summaniyya, either by way of sufficing, as if he had said: 'So there is no obligation and no impossibility,' because this is indicated by his saying '(And from it) meaning from the intellectually permissible', or because their doctrine is clearly false, because messengers have indeed been sent actually, so their sending is established by observation and eyewitness; so claiming its impossibility is obstinacy against the senses and opposition to the observed. As for the doctrine of the philosophers and Mu'tazila, their actual sending – peace be upon them – does not indicate its falsehood, because it is possible for them to say: Allah did that because it is obligatory upon Him. So since the matter of the two doctrines differed in that difference, he explicitly negated the stronger of the two in appearance, although each is weak in reality. And his subsequent saying: 'But by this, our faith has become obligatory' is a dafʿ (repulsion) of what might be imagined from the fact that sending messengers is intellectually permissible – that belief in its occurrence is not obligatory.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the second group: the Barahima and Summaniyya, who claim that sending messengers is intellectually impossible. Their argument is that sending messengers would be futile (ʿabath) because reason alone suffices; the criterion for action is the intellect's approval (taḥsīn) and for leaving is its disapproval (taqbīḥ). Since futility is impossible for Allah, the permissibility of sending messengers becomes impossible. The commentator then explains that the author's matn implicitly refutes this doctrine. He did not explicitly mention impossibility but his statement 'فلا وجوب' and the fact that sending is from the jā'iz 'aqlī indicates both that it is not obligatory and not impossible. The commentator also notes that the actual occurrence of messengers makes the claim of impossibility clearly false, as it is observed. He then contrasts this with the Mu'tazila and philosophers, whose claim of obligation is not disproven by actual occurrence because they could say Allah did it out of obligation. Hence the author explicitly negated obligation (stronger in appearance) while the negation of impossibility is understood. Finally, the author's line 'لكن بذا إيماننا قد وجبا' clarifies that believing in the occurrence of the messengers is obligatory even though the act itself is intellectually permissible.

Key Terms
السمنية
البراهمة
عبث
تحسين
تقبيح
مشاهدة
مكابرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that because sending messengers is intellectually permissible, believing in the messengers is also optional (not obligatory).
Correction: The author's line 'لكن بذا إيماننا قد وجبا' clarifies that faith in the actual messengers is obligatory, even though the act of sending is permissible.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator say the author did not explicitly refute the Barahima's claim of impossibility?
A) Because their claim is too weak to need refutation
B) Because the matn's statement 'فلا وجوب' and classifying it as jā'iz 'aqlī suffices to negate both obligation and impossibility
C) Because the author agreed with them
D) Because the refutation was given in a previous section

Key Takeaway
The actual sending of messengers is an empirical fact that refutes any claim of rational impossibility; the act is intellectually permissible, but belief in it is obligatory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you rely on your intellect alone to guide your life, or do you submit to the messages sent by Allah? What area of your life do you think you are most in need of divine guidance?
?
The Barahima claimed that sending messengers is futility because intellect suffices. Where in your life have you acted as if you have no need of prophets, and what has been the consequence?
?
The author says that belief in the actual messengers is obligatory even though sending them is intellectually permissible. Have you ever treated the obligation of faith as optional? What would it mean for you to fully believe that Allah sent messengers out of His wisdom, not out of obligation?

The Benefits of Sending Messengers
Al-Malali enumerates six benefits of sending messengers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the six benefits of sending messengers as taught by Ahl al-Sunna, and why they are a divine grace and necessity.

Core Idea
Messengers are a divine grace that perfects reason and reveals what reason alone cannot know.

This passage from al-Malali summarizes the Sunni position on prophethood: sending messengers is permissible for Allah, not obligatory or impossible. The core of the lesson is the enumeration of six distinct benefits that highlight why messengers are a mercy and a completion of reason. Let's explore each benefit in order.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the list structure starting with 'ومنها'.
The passage begins with a summary of the Sunni position before listing benefits.
2
Identify the six benefits
Count the benefits as they appear. Each is introduced with 'ومنها' (and among them). Write them down in your own words.
3
Connect each benefit to the role of messengers
For each benefit, ask: how does sending messengers accomplish this? For example, the first benefit assists reason; the second provides rulings beyond reason.
4
Reflect on the overall purpose
Consider that the benefits are not random but cover intellectual, moral, spiritual, and social dimensions. This shows the comprehensiveness of prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخلاصة القول في هذه المسألة: أنَّ بعثة الله تعالى الرُّسُلَ إلى خلقه عند أهل السُّنَّة جائز في حقه سبحانه ; فليس واجبًا عليه ، ولا مستحيلا ، وأنه واقع منه سبحانه لطفا منه بعباده ورحمةً لما فيها من الحِكَم والمصالح التي لا تُحصى... ومنها : مُعاضدة العقل فيما يمكنه أن يستقل بمعرفته ، مثل : وجودِ الباري ، وعلمه، وقدرته ، لئلا يكون للناس على الله حجة بعد الرسل. ومنها: استفادةُ الحُكم من الأنبياء فيما لا يستقل العقل به، مثل: مبحث الكلام، ورؤيته تعالى، والمعاد الجسماني. ومنها: إزالة الخوف الحاصل عند إتيان العبد بالحسناتِ ، لكون إتيانه بها تصرفًا في ملك الله بغير إذنه ; فلو لم يعلم أنَّ الله راض عنها طالب لها لم يأمَنْ أن يكون آتيا بغير ما يرضاه سبحانه ، ولا سبيل إلى علم رضاه سبحانه بفعل ذلك إلَّا مِن طريق الرُّسل. ومنها: بيان حال الأفعال التي تحسنُ تارةً وتقبح تارةً أخرى من غير اهتداء العقل إلى مواقعها. ومنها: تكميل النفوس البشرية بحسب استعداداتهم المُختلفة في العلميَّات والعمليات . ومنها: تبيين الأخلاق الفاضلة والراجعة إلى الأشخاص، والسياسات الكاملة العائدة إلى الجماعات
And the summary of the matter in this issue: that the sending of messengers by Allah to His creation according to Ahl al-Sunna is permissible in His right, exalted is He; so it is not obligatory upon Him, nor impossible, and that it occurs from Him, exalted, as a kindness (luṭf) from Him to His servants and mercy due to the wisdoms and benefits that cannot be counted... And among them: assisting the intellect in what it can independently know, such as the existence of the Creator, His knowledge, and His power, lest people have an argument against Allah after the messengers. And among them: deriving rulings from the prophets in what the intellect cannot independently know, such as the topic of speech (al-kalām), seeing Him (ru'yatuhu taʿālā), and bodily resurrection (al-maʿād al-jismānī). And among them: removing the fear that occurs when a servant does good deeds, because his doing them is a disposition in the kingdom of Allah without His permission; if he did not know that Allah is pleased with them and seeks them, he would not be safe from doing what displeases Him, and there is no way to know His pleasure with that action except through the messengers. And among them: explaining the state of actions that are sometimes good and sometimes evil without the intellect guiding to their occasions. And among them: perfecting human souls according to their various dispositions in theoretical and practical matters. And among them: clarifying virtuous morals pertaining to individuals, and perfect policies pertaining to communities.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage is a summary and enumeration of benefits. It first restates the position of Ahl al-Sunna: sending messengers is permissible (jā'iz), not obligatory nor impossible, and it is a divine grace (luṭf) and mercy containing countless wisdoms. Then it lists six benefits: 1) Assisting the intellect in what it can know independently, such as the existence of Allah, His knowledge and power, to remove any excuse after the messengers. 2) Providing rulings in matters the intellect cannot know independently, like the nature of divine speech, the beatific vision, and bodily resurrection. 3) Removing the fear that comes from performing good deeds, because a person might worry that acting in Allah's dominion without explicit permission could displease Him; the messengers assure that such acts are pleasing. 4) Clarifying the moral status of actions that are sometimes good and sometimes evil, as the intellect alone may not discern the relevant factors. 5) Perfecting human souls according to their varied capacities in both theoretical and practical domains. 6) Outlining virtuous morals for individuals and complete policies for communities.
