رحمة
حكم
مصالح
معاضدة العقل
استفادة الحكم
إزالة الخوف
تكميل النفوس
الأخلاق الفاضلة
السياسات الكاملة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Messengers are only needed for matters beyond reason; reason is sufficient for everything else.
Correction: The first benefit shows that messengers also assist and confirm what reason can know, and they are needed to remove excuses and provide certainty.

Checkpoint
Which benefit of sending messengers specifically addresses the fear of acting in Allah's dominion without permission?
A) Assisting the intellect
B) Deriving rulings that reason cannot know
C) Removing fear when doing good deeds
D) Perfecting souls

Key Takeaway
The sending of messengers is a divine kindness that assists reason, teaches what reason cannot know, removes fear, clarifies morality, perfects souls, and establishes social order.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform a good deed, do you ever secretly fear that Allah might not accept it because you acted without His explicit permission? How does the third benefit—removing that fear through messengers—change the way you approach obedience? Will you now serve Him with more confidence, knowing He has declared His pleasure through the messengers?
?
You have a mind that can reason about God's existence and attributes. But are you truly humble enough to admit that your intellect is limited, especially regarding matters like divine speech and resurrection? The second benefit confronts you: do you rely on revelation for these truths, or do you try to reduce them to what your mind can accept? Which areas of your faith are you tempted to rationalize away?
?
The fifth benefit speaks of perfecting human souls 'according to their various dispositions.' Have you ever considered that your unique strengths and weaknesses are part of Allah's plan for your spiritual growth? Instead of comparing yourself to others, will you commit to seeking the prophetic guidance that specifically addresses your theoretical understanding and practical conduct—starting today?

Rational Permissibility of Messengers and Negation of Obligation
Commentator explains the rational status of sending messengers and the author's negation of obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author explicitly negated the obligation of sending messengers while not explicitly negating impossibility, based on the rational judgment of permissibility.

Core Idea
Sending messengers is rationally permissible, negating both obligation and impossibility.

In this passage, the commentator explains the author's statement in the matn. He shows that the rational basis for actions—the intellect's judgment of good and bad—leads to the conclusion that sending messengers is permissible, not obligatory or impossible. The author's explicit negation of obligation targets the Muʿtazila and philosophers, while the negation of impossibility is either implied or obvious from reality.

Guided Path
1
Understand the rational framework
Read the opening sentence where the commentator establishes that the basis for doing or refraining from an action lies in the intellect's judgment of good or bad. Note that futility (ʿabath) is impossible for God.
This is a foundational Ashʿarī principle: the intellect can discern good and bad, but God is not subject to obligation.
2
Apply to the sending of messengers
See how the commentator applies this principle: if futility is impossible, then deeming the sending of messengers permissible (jāʾiz) is also necessary (since it avoids futility). This establishes that the act is neither obligatory nor impossible.
3
Identify the two erroneous doctrines
The passage discusses two opposing wrong views: the Muʿtazila and philosophers who say sending messengers is obligatory upon God, and the Brahmans and Sumaniyya who say it is impossible. Understand why each is false.
The Brahmans' view is refuted by observable reality; the Muʿtazila's view is refuted by rational arguments against obligation.
4
Analyze the author's rhetorical strategy
Notice that the author explicitly negates only obligation, not impossibility. The commentator explains why: either because impossibility is already negated by mentioning 'al-jāʾiz al-ʿaqli' or because the impossibility claim is obviously false. The negation of obligation is directed at the stronger apparent argument.
This shows the author's scholarly precision and consideration of the audience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم النبوات
مناط فعل الشيء تحسينُ العقل إيَّاه ، ومناط تركه تقبيح العقل إيَّاه، والعبث على الله تعالى محالٌ ؛ فيكون ما أدى إليه وهو تجويز إرسال الرسل محالاً ، إذا علمت هذا الكلام علمت أن قول المصنف: «فلا وجوب» تصريح بنفي مذهب المعتزلة والفلاسفة، ولم يصرح بنفي مذهب البراهمة والسُّمنية إما من باب الاكتفاء، وكأنه قد قال: فلا وجوب ولا استحالة ، لأنَّ هذا يُنْبِئُ عنه قوله: (وَمِنْهُ أي من الجائز العقلي ، وإما لكون مذهبهم ظاهر البطلان ؛ لأنَّ الرُّسل قد أُرسلوا فعلا، فإرسالهم واقع ثابت بالمشاهَدَةِ والعيان؛ فادعاء استحالته مكابرة للحس ومعاندةٌ للمُشاهَدِ ، فأَمَّا مذهب الفلاسفة والمعتزلة فلا يدلّ على بطلانه بَعْتُهم عليهم الصلاة والسلام بالفعل ؛ لجواز أن يقولوا : إنّ الله تعالى قد فعل ذلك لكونه واجبًا عليه ، فلما اخْتَلَفَ شأنُ المَذْهَبَيْنِ ذلكَ الاختلاف صرَّح بنفي أقواهما بحسب الظاهر، وإن كان كل منهما ضعيفًا في الحقيقة
Section of Prophethood. The basis for doing an action is the intellect's deeming it good, and the basis for refraining is the intellect's deeming it bad. Futility (al-ʿabath) regarding God Most High is impossible. Therefore, what leads to it—which is deeming the sending of messengers permissible as a rational possibility—is impossible. If you have understood this statement, you know that the author's saying: 'Thus no obligation (fa-lā wujūb)' is an explicit negation of the doctrine of the Muʿtazila and the philosophers, and he did not explicitly negate the doctrine of the Brahmans and the Sumaniyya, either out of sufficiency, as if he had said: 'no obligation and no impossibility', because this is indicated by his saying: 'and from it (wa-minhu) i.e. from the rationally permissible (al-jāʾiz al-ʿaqli),' or because their doctrine is manifestly false; for the messengers have indeed been sent, so their sending is an established reality witnessed and seen; thus claiming its impossibility is obstinacy against sense and defiance of the observed. As for the doctrine of the philosophers and the Muʿtazila, their sending (peace and blessings upon them) in actuality does not prove its falsehood; because they may say that God Most High did that because it is obligatory upon Him. So when the two doctrines differed in that manner, he explicitly negated the stronger of them apparently, even though both are weak in reality.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage begins with an introduction to the rational basis for actions (taḥsīn and taqbīḥ) and applies it to the issue of sending messengers. The commentator explains the statement of the author (Sanusi) 'fa-lā wujūb' (thus no obligation) from the matn. He argues that the author's explicit negation of obligation is a refutation of the Muʿtazila and philosophers, who hold that sending messengers is obligatory upon God. The author did not explicitly deny impossibility, which is the view of the Brahmans and Sumaniyya, either because his mention of 'wa-minhu' (and from it) sufficiently indicates that it is rationally permissible (al-jāʾiz al-ʿaqli), thus negating both extremes, or because their claim of impossibility is manifestly false: messengers have been sent in observable reality. For the Muʿtazila and philosophers, their claim of obligation cannot be disproven by the mere fact of sending, since they could say God did so because it is obligatory. Therefore, the author explicitly denied the stronger erroneous view (obligation) in appearance, although both are weak. This shows the author's rhetorical strategy and the rational status of prophethood according to Ahl al-Sunna.

Key Terms
جائز عقلي
وجوب
استحالة
العبث
البراهمة
السمنية
المعتزلة
الفلاسفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the commentator considered the Brahmans' view possibly valid.
Correction: The commentator explicitly states their doctrine is manifestly false, as messengers have been sent in observable reality.

Checkpoint
Why did the author explicitly negate obligation (wujūb) but not explicitly negate impossibility (istiḥāla)?
A) Because impossibility is more obvious.
B) Because the Muʿtazila's view appears stronger in outward argument.
C) Because he forgot to mention it.
D) Because the Brahmans' view is also explicitly denied.

Key Takeaway
The rational permissibility of sending messengers is the correct stance of Ahl al-Sunna, negating both obligation and impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever thought that God is obligated to send you guidance in a specific way? This lesson shows that sending messengers is purely gracious. Does your heart truly thank Him for the guidance you have, or do you secretly demand more?
?
The Brahmans denied the possibility of messengers despite clear historical evidence. Where in your life do you stubbornly reject truth simply because it challenges your assumptions or comfort?
?
The author negates both extremes: obligation and impossibility. Are there beliefs you hold about God that fall into either extreme—expecting something from Him that you have no right to, or doubting what He has clearly done? Will you search your soul and correct them?

The Ahl al-Sunna Position on Prophethood: Permissibility and Benefits
Summary of Ahl al-Sunna doctrine on prophethood with list of benefits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ahl al-Sunna position that sending messengers is rationally permissible and actually occurred as a grace from God, and you will be able to list several benefits of prophethood.

Core Idea
Prophethood is a rational possibility and a divine grace, not a necessity.

The passage begins by clarifying a potential misunderstanding: because sending messengers is rationally permissible, one might think belief in it is not obligatory. But the matn affirms that faith in prophets is obligatory. Then the commentator summarizes the Ahl al-Sunna doctrine: sending messengers is jāʾiz (permissible) in God's right—not wajib (obligatory) nor mustaḥīl (impossible)—and it has occurred as a luṭf (grace) and raḥma (mercy). He then lists several wisdoms and benefits (ḥikam) that show why God sent messengers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core stance
Read the first part of the passage to understand that Ahl al-Sunna say sending messengers is jāʾiz ʿaqlī, not wajib or mustaḥīl, and that it has occurred as luṭf.
The matn line 'lākin bi-dhā īmānunā qad wajabā' is being explained.
2
Work through the list of benefits
There are six benefits listed. Note each one: (1) supporting reason, (2) informing about unreachable matters, (3) removing fear from good deeds, (4) clarifying context-dependent morals, (5) perfecting souls, (6) teaching ethics and politics.
These benefits show the wisdom behind sending messengers.
3
Reflect on human need
Ask yourself why each benefit addresses a specific human limitation or need. This will help you see prophethood as a mercy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله فيما بعد : لكنْ بِذَا إيماننا قَدْ وَجَبَا دَفع لما قد يُتوهم مِن كون إرسال الرُّسل جائزًا عقليًا أنّ الإيمان بوقوعه ليس واجباً .
وخلاصة القول في هذه المسألة: أنَّ بعثة الله تعالى الرُّسلَ إلى خلقه عند أهل السُّنَّة جائز في حقه سبحانه ؛ فليس واجبًا عليه ، ولا مستحيلا ، وأنه واقع منه سبحانه لطفا منه بعباده ورحمةً لما فيها من الحِكَم والمصالح التي لا تُحصى... ومنها : مُعاضدة العقل فيما يمكنه أن يستقل بمعرفته ، مثل : وجودِ الباري ، وعلمه، وقدرته ، لئلا يكون للناس على الله حجة بعد الرسل. ومنها: استفادةُ الحُكم من الأنبياء فيما لا يستقل العقل به، مثل: مبحث الكلام، ورؤيته تعالى، والمعاد الجسماني. ومنها: إزالة الخوف الحاصل عند إتيان العبد بالحسناتِ ، لكون إتيانه بها تصرفًا في ملك الله بغير إذنه ؛ فلو لم يعلم أنَّ الله راض عنها طالب لها لم يَأْمَنْ أن يكون آتيا بغير ما يرضاه سبحانه ، ولا سبيل إلى علم رضاه سبحانه بفعل ذلك إلَّا مِن طريق الرُّسل. ومنها: بيان حال الأفعال التي تحسنُ تارةً وتقبح تارةً أخرى من غير اهتداء العقل إلى مواقعها.
ومنها: تكميل النفوس البشرية بحسب استعداداتهم المُختلفة في العلميَّات والعمليات . ومنها: تبيين الأخلاق الفاضلة والراجعة إلى الأشخاص، والسياسات الكاملة العائدة إلى الجماعات .
بيان جواز إرسال الرسل عقلا ووجوبه شرعا
الجائز العقلي (إِرْسَالُ) الله تعالى (جَمِيعِ الرُّسُلِ) أي: رُسلَ البَشر مِن آدمَ إلى محمد عليهم الصَّلاة والسَّلام إلى المُكلَّفين من الثقلين، ليُبلِّغُوهم أمرَهُ ونهيه ووعده ووعيده ، ويُبيّنوا لهم عنه ما يحتاجون إليه من أمور الدينا والدِّين ممَّا جاءوا به ، حتّى تقومَ الحُجَّة عليهم بالبينات ، وتنقطع عنهم سائر العلل . ومنها: الإخبار بتفاصيل ثواب المُطيع وعقاب العاصي، ترغيباً في الحسنات وتحذيرًا عن السَّيِّئات ، ... إلى غير ذلك من الفوائد. وأنَّ الذين قالوا إنها مستحيلة تمسكوا بِشُبَه أَوْهَى مِن بيوت العنكبوت، منها: أنَّها تتوقف على علم المَبعُوث بأن الباعث هو الله تعالى ، ولا سبيل إلى معرفته ذلك. ورُدَّ : بأنَّه يجوز أن يَنْصُبَ الله تعالى دليلا يدلُّ على ذلك ، أو أن يخلق فيه علما ضروريًا. ومن شبههم الواهية: أنها عبث ؛ لأنَّ العقل يستقل بفَهم ما يجب وما يستحيل وما يجوز ، فما يراه حسناً يجب فعله وما يراه قبيحاً يجب تركه، وما يتوقف فيه يجوز فعله إن اقتضته المصلحة . ويُرَدُّ على هذا: بأَنَّا لا نُسلّم أنها سَفَةٌ ؛ لأنَّ الأحوال إن انحصرت فيما ذكرتُم فالبعثة تكون معاضِدَةً للعقل ، وإن لم تنحصر - وهو الواقع - فإنها تفيد حكم ما لا يستطيع العقل الاستقلال به . ومِن شبههم الفاسدة: أنَّ مبنى البعثة على التكاليف، وليس في التكاليف فائدة، لا للآمر بها ، وهو ظاهر ؛ لتعاليه عن أن ينتفع بعمل عبده ، ولا للمأمور بها ، وهو العبد ؛ لأنه يتضرّر باحتمال ما يَشُقُ عليه. وهذا كلام ظاهر البطلان ، بل نقول: فيها نفع للعبد عظيم جدًّا، وكلُّ واحدٍ منَّا يتحمل كثيرًا المَشَاقُ في سبيل تحصيل منفعة لا تُقاس أبدا ممَّا من ثواب عبادة الله . وأما المعتزلة الذين أوجبوها على الله تعالى، فالرَّدُّ عليهم إنَّما يكون بإبطال أصلهم الذي بنوا عليه هذه المسألة، وهو قولهم: (فعل الصلاح والأصلح واجب على الله)، وقد قدمنا ما فيه الكفاية في إبطاله ، فارجع إليه إن شئت، والله ينفعك به، وينفعني بدعوة أخ صالح يدعو لي بظهر الغيب . اللهم آمين . والله أعلى وأعلم ، وصلى الله على سيدنا محمد وآله وصحبه وسلّم. محي. إلى هنا نهاية حاشية الشيخ محمد محي الدين عبد الحميد وجزاه عنا كل خير
And his statement later: 'but by this our faith has become obligatory (lākin bi-dhā īmānunā qad wajabā)' is a refutation of what might be imagined—that because sending messengers is rationally permissible, belief in its occurrence is not obligatory. And the summary of the discourse on this issue: that the sending of messengers by God Most High to His creation, according to Ahl al-Sunna, is permissible (jāʾiz) in His right, the Exalted; it is not obligatory upon Him, nor impossible, and it has occurred from Him, the Exalted, as a grace (luṭf) from Him to His servants and mercy, because of the wisdoms and interests that cannot be counted... And among them: supporting the intellect in what it can know independently, such as the existence of the Creator, His knowledge, and His power, so that people may have no argument against God after the messengers. And among them: deriving judgment from the prophets in what the intellect cannot know independently, such as the topic of speech (al-kalām), seeing Him the Exalted, and bodily resurrection. And among them: removing the fear that occurs when the servant performs good deeds, because his performance of them is a disposition in God's kingdom without His permission; so if he did not know that God is pleased with them and desires them, he would not be safe from doing what displeases Him the Exalted. And there is no way to know His pleasure, the Exalted, with doing that except through the messengers. And among them: clarifying the status of actions that are good sometimes and bad at other times without the intellect's guidance to their contexts. And among them: perfecting human souls according to their different aptitudes in theoretical and practical matters. And among them: explaining virtuous ethics pertaining to individuals, and complete policies pertaining to communities. Statement of the rational permissibility of sending messengers and its legal obligation. The rationally permissible (al-jāʾiz al-ʿaqli) is the sending by God Most High of all messengers—i.e., human messengers from Adam to Muhammad, peace and blessings be upon them, to the morally responsible from humankind and jinn—so that they may convey to them His command, His prohibition, His promise, and His threat, and explain to them on His behalf what they need of the matters of this world and religion from what they brought, so that the proof may be established against them with clear signs, and all excuses may be cut off from them. And among them: informing about the details of the reward of the obedient and the punishment of the disobedient, as an incentive for good deeds and a deterrent against evil deeds... and other such benefits. And those who said that it is impossible clung to arguments weaker than spider webs. Among them: that it depends on the sent one knowing that the sender is God, and there is no way for him to know that. And it is refuted: that it is permissible for God to appoint a proof indicating that, or to create necessary knowledge in him. And among their weak arguments: that it is futility (ʿabath); because the intellect independently understands what is obligatory, impossible, and permissible, so what it sees as good must be done, what it sees as bad must be abandoned, and what it is uncertain about may be done if maslaḥa requires it. And this is refuted: that we do not concede that it is foolishness; because if the situations are limited to what you mentioned, then the sending would be supportive of the intellect, and if they are not limited—which is the reality—then it provides a ruling for what the intellect cannot independently grasp. And among their corrupt arguments: that the basis of sending is obligations (takālīf), and there is no benefit in obligations, neither for the commander—which is obvious due to His transcendence above benefiting from His servant's action—nor for the commanded—the servant—because he is harmed by enduring what is difficult for him. This is clearly false; rather we say: in them there is a great benefit for the servant, and each of us endures many hardships in pursuit of a benefit that is incomparable to what comes from the reward of worshipping God. As for the Muʿtazila who made it obligatory upon God, the refutation against them is only by invalidating the principle upon which they built this issue, which is their statement: 'doing what is best and most fitting (fiʿl al-ṣalāḥ wa al-aṣlaḥ) is obligatory upon God.' We have already presented what is sufficient in invalidating it, so refer to it if you wish, and may God benefit you with it, and benefit me with the supplication of a righteous brother who prays for me in secret. O Allah, amen. And God is Higher and more Knowing. And may God bless our master Muhammad, his family, and his companions and grant them peace. Muhyī. Up to here is the end of the gloss of Shaykh Muḥammad Muḥyī al-Dīn ʿAbd al-Ḥamīd, may God reward him with all good.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage serves as both a summary of the Ahl al-Sunna position on the sending of messengers and a refutation of opposing views. It begins by explaining the matn line 'lākin bi-dhā īmānunā qad wajabā', which counters a potential misconception: because sending messengers is rationally permissible, one might think belief in its occurrence is not obligatory; but the matn affirms that it is obligatory. Then the commentator summarizes the Ahl al-Sunna doctrine: sending messengers is rationally permissible (jāʾiz ʿaqlī) in God's right—not obligatory and not impossible—and it has actually occurred as a grace (luṭf) and mercy from God, containing countless wisdoms and interests. He then lists several benefits (ḥikam) of sending messengers: (1) supporting reason in matters it can know independently (e.g., God's existence, knowledge, power) so people have no excuse; (2) providing knowledge about matters reason cannot know (e.g., God's speech, vision of God, bodily resurrection); (3) removing fear from doing good deeds, since without prophetic guidance one cannot know God's pleasure; (4) clarifying the moral status of actions that vary with context; (5) perfecting human souls in knowledge and practice; (6) explaining virtuous ethics and just policies. He then defines the rationally permissible sending as encompassing all messengers from Adam to Muhammad to both humans and jinn, for conveying commands, prohibitions, promises, threats, and establishing proof, and adds another benefit: detailing reward and punishment. Next, he refutes those who deem it impossible (the Brahmans and similar groups), citing their weak arguments: (a) the messenger cannot know he is sent by God—refuted by God's ability to provide proof or create necessary knowledge; (b) it is futile because reason suffices—refuted by the fact that reason may not cover all situations, and even if it does, the sending supports it; in reality reason cannot cover all; (c) obligations have no benefit—refuted because the servant gains immense benefit, including reward. Finally, he refutes the Muʿtazila's view that sending messengers is obligatory upon God, stating that this is based on their principle that God must do what is best (al-ṣalāḥ wa al-aṣlaḥ), which has already been refuted earlier. The passage ends with a prayer and a note that this is the end of the gloss.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Because sending messengers is rationally permissible, belief in prophets is optional.
Correction: The matn affirms that faith has become obligatory (wajaba) through revelation; rational permissibility does not negate legal obligation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Ahl al-Sunna view on sending messengers?
A) It is obligatory upon God.
B) It is rationally permissible and actually occurred as grace.
C) It is impossible.
D) It is rationally permissible but did not occur.

Key Takeaway
Ahl al-Sunna affirm that prophethood is a rationally possible divine grace with numerous benefits for humanity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform a good deed, do you ever fear that it might not be accepted because you are acting in God's dominion without explicit permission? How does the knowledge that prophets have confirmed God's pleasure change that fear in your daily life? Will you thank God today for sending them?
?
The passage says prophethood perfects souls according to different aptitudes. In the past week, have you actively sought to deepen your understanding of God's attributes (theoretical) or improve your character (practical) through the teachings of the Prophet ﷺ? If not, what one step will you take today to do so?

Prophethood
intermediate
8 min
Refuting Objections to Prophethood
Refutation of three objections to prophethood and of the Mu'tazila position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand and be able to refute common objections to prophethood raised by skeptics, the Brahmans, and the Muʿtazila.

Core Idea
Objections to prophethood are weak and collapse upon scrutiny; prophethood is both possible and beneficial.

After establishing the rational permissibility and benefits of sending messengers, the commentator addresses those who reject it. He refutes three main objections: (1) the messenger cannot know he is sent by God, (2) prophethood is futile because reason suffices, and (3) obligations have no benefit. He ends by refuting the Muʿtazila who make it obligatory upon God. Each refutation is concise but decisive.

Guided Path
1
Read the three objections
Identify each objection: (a) messenger cannot know sender, (b) prophethood is futile (ʿabath), (c) obligations have no benefit.
These are raised by different groups—Brahmans, skeptics, etc.
2
Study the refutations
For each objection, note the refutation: (a) God can provide proof or create necessary knowledge; (b) reason is not sufficient in all matters; (c) the servant gains immense reward.
The refutations are based on reason and divine attributes.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila refutation
The Mu'tazila claim it is obligatory on God to send prophets; the commentator says this rests on their principle of 'al-ṣalāḥ wa al-aṣlaḥ', which has already been refuted.
Refer back to earlier discussion on that principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله فيما بعد : لكنْ بِذَا إيماننا قَدْ وَجَبَا دَفع لما قد يُتوهم مِن كون إرسال الرُّسل جائزًا عقليًا أنّ الإيمان بوقوعه ليس واجباً .
وخلاصة القول في هذه المسألة: أنَّ بعثة الله تعالى الرُّسلَ إلى خلقه عند أهل السُّنَّة جائز في حقه سبحانه ؛ فليس واجبًا عليه ، ولا مستحيلا ، وأنه واقع منه سبحانه لطفا منه بعباده ورحمةً لما فيها من الحِكَم والمصالح التي لا تُحصى... ومنها : مُعاضدة العقل فيما يمكنه أن يستقل بمعرفته ، مثل : وجودِ الباري ، وعلمه، وقدرته ، لئلا يكون للناس على الله حجة بعد الرسل. ومنها: استفادةُ الحُكم من الأنبياء فيما لا يستقل العقل به، مثل: مبحث الكلام، ورؤيته تعالى، والمعاد الجسماني. ومنها: إزالة الخوف الحاصل عند إتيان العبد بالحسناتِ ، لكون إتيانه بها تصرفًا في ملك الله بغير إذنه ؛ فلو لم يعلم أنَّ الله راض عنها طالب لها لم يَأْمَنْ أن يكون آتيا بغير ما يرضاه سبحانه ، ولا سبيل إلى علم رضاه سبحانه بفعل ذلك إلَّا مِن طريق الرُّسل. ومنها: بيان حال الأفعال التي تحسنُ تارةً وتقبح تارةً أخرى من غير اهتداء العقل إلى مواقعها.
ومنها: تكميل النفوس البشرية بحسب استعداداتهم المُختلفة في العلميَّات والعمليات . ومنها: تبيين الأخلاق الفاضلة والراجعة إلى الأشخاص، والسياسات الكاملة العائدة إلى الجماعات .
بيان جواز إرسال الرسل عقلا ووجوبه شرعا
الجائز العقلي (إِرْسَالُ) الله تعالى (جَمِيعِ الرُّسُلِ) أي: رُسلَ البَشر مِن آدمَ إلى محمد عليهم الصَّلاة والسَّلام إلى المُكلَّفين من الثقلين، ليُبلِّغُوهم أمرَهُ ونهيه ووعده ووعيده ، ويُبيّنوا لهم عنه ما يحتاجون إليه من أمور الدينا والدِّين ممَّا جاءوا به ، حتّى تقومَ الحُجَّة عليهم بالبينات ، وتنقطع عنهم سائر العلل . ومنها: الإخبار بتفاصيل ثواب المُطيع وعقاب العاصي، ترغيباً في الحسنات وتحذيرًا عن السَّيِّئات ، ... إلى غير ذلك من الفوائد. وأنَّ الذين قالوا إنها مستحيلة تمسكوا بِشُبَه أَوْهَى مِن بيوت العنكبوت، منها: أنَّها تتوقف على علم المَبعُوث بأن الباعث هو الله تعالى ، ولا سبيل إلى معرفته ذلك. ورُدَّ : بأنَّه يجوز أن يَنْصُبَ الله تعالى دليلا يدلُّ على ذلك ، أو أن يخلق فيه علما ضروريًا. ومن شبههم الواهية: أنها عبث ؛ لأنَّ العقل يستقل بفَهم ما يجب وما يستحيل وما يجوز ، فما يراه حسناً يجب فعله وما يراه قبيحاً يجب تركه، وما يتوقف فيه يجوز فعله إن اقتضته المصلحة . ويُرَدُّ على هذا: بأَنَّا لا نُسلّم أنها سَفَةٌ ؛ لأنَّ الأحوال إن انحصرت فيما ذكرتُم فالبعثة تكون معاضِدَةً للعقل ، وإن لم تنحصر - وهو الواقع - فإنها تفيد حكم ما لا يستطيع العقل الاستقلال به . ومِن شبههم الفاسدة: أنَّ مبنى البعثة على التكاليف، وليس في التكاليف فائدة، لا للآمر بها ، وهو ظاهر ؛ لتعاليه عن أن ينتفع بعمل عبده ، ولا للمأمور بها ، وهو العبد ؛ لأنه يتضرّر باحتمال ما يَشُقُ عليه. وهذا كلام ظاهر البطلان ، بل نقول: فيها نفع للعبد عظيم جدًّا، وكلُّ واحدٍ منَّا يتحمل كثيرًا المَشَاقُ في سبيل تحصيل منفعة لا تُقاس أبدا ممَّا من ثواب عبادة الله . وأما المعتزلة الذين أوجبوها على الله تعالى، فالرَّدُّ عليهم إنَّما يكون بإبطال أصلهم الذي بنوا عليه هذه المسألة، وهو قولهم: (فعل الصلاح والأصلح واجب على الله)، وقد قدمنا ما فيه الكفاية في إبطاله ، فارجع إليه إن شئت، والله ينفعك به، وينفعني بدعوة أخ صالح يدعو لي بظهر الغيب . اللهم آمين . والله أعلى وأعلم ، وصلى الله على سيدنا محمد وآله وصحبه وسلّم. محي. إلى هنا نهاية حاشية الشيخ محمد محي الدين عبد الحميد وجزاه عنا كل خير
And his statement later: 'but by this our faith has become obligatory (lākin bi-dhā īmānunā qad wajabā)' is a refutation of what might be imagined—that because sending messengers is rationally permissible, belief in its occurrence is not obligatory. And the summary of the discourse on this issue: that the sending of messengers by God Most High to His creation, according to Ahl al-Sunna, is permissible (jāʾiz) in His right, the Exalted; it is not obligatory upon Him, nor impossible, and it has occurred from Him, the Exalted, as a grace (luṭf) from Him to His servants and mercy, because of the wisdoms and interests that cannot be counted... And among them: supporting the intellect in what it can know independently, such as the existence of the Creator, His knowledge, and His power, so that people may have no argument against God after the messengers. And among them: deriving judgment from the prophets in what the intellect cannot know independently, such as the topic of speech (al-kalām), seeing Him the Exalted, and bodily resurrection. And among them: removing the fear that occurs when the servant performs good deeds, because his performance of them is a disposition in God's kingdom without His permission; so if he did not know that God is pleased with them and desires them, he would not be safe from doing what displeases Him the Exalted. And there is no way to know His pleasure, the Exalted, with doing that except through the messengers. And among them: clarifying the status of actions that are good sometimes and bad at other times without the intellect's guidance to their contexts. And among them: perfecting human souls according to their different aptitudes in theoretical and practical matters. And among them: explaining virtuous ethics pertaining to individuals, and complete policies pertaining to communities. Statement of the rational permissibility of sending messengers and its legal obligation. The rationally permissible (al-jāʾiz al-ʿaqli) is the sending by God Most High of all messengers—i.e., human messengers from Adam to Muhammad, peace and blessings be upon them, to the morally responsible from humankind and jinn—so that they may convey to them His command, His prohibition, His promise, and His threat, and explain to them on His behalf what they need of the matters of this world and religion from what they brought, so that the proof may be established against them with clear signs, and all excuses may be cut off from them. And among them: informing about the details of the reward of the obedient and the punishment of the disobedient, as an incentive for good deeds and a deterrent against evil deeds... and other such benefits. And those who said that it is impossible clung to arguments weaker than spider webs. Among them: that it depends on the sent one knowing that the sender is God, and there is no way for him to know that. And it is refuted: that it is permissible for God to appoint a proof indicating that, or to create necessary knowledge in him. And among their weak arguments: that it is futility (ʿabath); because the intellect independently understands what is obligatory, impossible, and permissible, so what it sees as good must be done, what it sees as bad must be abandoned, and what it is uncertain about may be done if maslaḥa requires it. And this is refuted: that we do not concede that it is foolishness; because if the situations are limited to what you mentioned, then the sending would be supportive of the intellect, and if they are not limited—which is the reality—then it provides a ruling for what the intellect cannot independently grasp. And among their corrupt arguments: that the basis of sending is obligations (takālīf), and there is no benefit in obligations, neither for the commander—which is obvious due to His transcendence above benefiting from His servant's action—nor for the commanded—the servant—because he is harmed by enduring what is difficult for him. This is clearly false; rather we say: in them there is a great benefit for the servant, and each of us endures many hardships in pursuit of a benefit that is incomparable to what comes from the reward of worshipping God. As for the Muʿtazila who made it obligatory upon God, the refutation against them is only by invalidating the principle upon which they built this issue, which is their statement: 'doing what is best and most fitting (fiʿl al-ṣalāḥ wa al-aṣlaḥ) is obligatory upon God.' We have already presented what is sufficient in invalidating it, so refer to it if you wish, and may God benefit you with it, and benefit me with the supplication of a righteous brother who prays for me in secret. O Allah, amen. And God is Higher and more Knowing. And may God bless our master Muhammad, his family, and his companions and grant them peace. Muhyī. Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage serves as both a summary of the Ahl al-Sunna position on the sending of messengers and a refutation of opposing views. It begins by explaining the matn line 'lākin bi-dhā īmānunā qad wajabā', which counters a potential misconception: because sending messengers is rationally permissible, one might think belief in its occurrence is not obligatory; but the matn affirms that it is obligatory. Then the commentator summarizes the Ahl al-Sunna doctrine: sending messengers is rationally permissible (jāʾiz ʿaqlī) in God's right—not obligatory and not impossible—and it has actually occurred as a grace (luṭf) and mercy from God, containing countless wisdoms and interests. He then lists several benefits (ḥikam) of sending messengers: (1) supporting reason in matters it can know independently (e.g., God's existence, knowledge, power) so people have no excuse; (2) providing knowledge about matters reason cannot know (e.g., God's speech, vision of God, bodily resurrection); (3) removing fear from doing good deeds, since without prophetic guidance one cannot know God's pleasure; (4) clarifying the moral status of actions that vary with context; (5) perfecting human souls in knowledge and practice; (6) explaining virtuous ethics and just policies. He then defines the rationally permissible sending as encompassing all messengers from Adam to Muhammad to both humans and jinn, for conveying commands, prohibitions, promises, threats, and establishing proof, and adds another benefit: detailing reward and punishment. Next, he refutes those who deem it impossible (the Brahmans and similar groups), citing their weak arguments: (a) the messenger cannot know he is sent by God—refuted by God's ability to provide proof or create necessary knowledge; (b) it is futile because reason suffices—refuted by the fact that reason may not cover all situations, and even if it does, the sending supports it; in reality reason cannot cover all; (c) obligations have no benefit—refuted because the servant gains immense benefit, including reward. Finally, he refutes the Muʿtazila's view that sending messengers is obligatory upon God, stating that this is based on their principle that God must do what is best (al-ṣalāḥ wa al-aṣlaḥ), which has already been refuted earlier. The passage ends with a prayer and a note that this is the end of the gloss.

Key Terms
عبث
تكاليف
الصلاح والأصلح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophethood is impossible because reason suffices or obligations are pointless.
Correction: Reason does not cover all matters, and obligations bring great benefit to the servant.

Checkpoint
How does the passage refute the objection that prophethood is futile (ʿabath) because reason suffices?
A) By saying that reason can never know God.
B) By saying that if reason sufficed, then prophethood supports it; but in reality reason does not cover all.
C) By arguing that prophethood is obligatory on God.
D) By denying that reason can understand good and evil.

Key Takeaway
All objections to prophethood are either based on ignorance or flawed premises; the Ahl al-Sunna position remains sound.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you questioned whether a religious obligation was truly beneficial. After reading this passage, how will you respond to such doubts in the future? Will you trust God's wisdom even when the benefit is not immediately visible? Commit to one specific action this week to act on that trust.
?
The passage says each of us endures countless hardships for worldly gain. Are you willing to endure hardship in worshipping God for the immense reward described? What is one sacrifice you can make today in your worship that you have been avoiding?
?
The Mu'tazila claimed that God must send prophets. Do you sometimes treat God's commands as if He owes you explanation or benefit? Reflect on how your attitude might shift if you see prophethood and obligations as pure grace from God. What would change in your daily submission?
